The author of this book, when he had completed for you, my children, this book, which is the complete part of the science of medicine, and had reached the highest degree of clarity and explanation in it, saw fit to complete it for you with this chapter, which is the part of practical application.
Because practical application in our country and in our time is entirely lacking, so much so that its science almost ceased to be taught and its effect disappeared. But only a few traces of it remained in the books of the ancients, which the hands have corrupted, and error and confusion have affected, so that its meanings have become obscure and its benefits have receded.
Therefore, I saw fit to revive it and compose this chapter on the path of explanation, clarity, and conciseness, and to provide illustrations of cauterizing irons and other instruments of practice, as this is an additional means of clarification and a collection of what is needed.
The reason there is no skilled practitioner with a skilled hand in our time is that the practice of medicine is long, and the practitioner must first diligently study the anatomy, as described by Galen. So that he understands the benefits of the organs, their structures, temperaments, connections, and separations; and knows the bones, nerves, muscles, their numbers and origins; the arteries, veins, and their courses.
Therefore, Hippocrates said that physicians are numerous in name, but few in practice, especially in the craft of the hand.  We have mentioned a glimpse of this in the introduction of this book.
Because whoever is not knowledgeable about the anatomy we have mentioned is likely to make mistakes that kill people, as I have witnessed many who have imagined themselves knowledgeable in this field, claiming expertise without knowledge or understanding.
I saw an ignorant physician who incised a scrofulous tumor on a woman's neck, thereby severing some of the arteries of the neck, causing the woman to bleed to death in his hands.
I saw another physician, in attempting to remove a stone from an elderly man's bladder, was overly bold and extracted it with a portion of the bladder wall. The man died about three days later.  I was summoned to remove it, and considering the size of the stone and the patient's condition, I deemed it unadvisable.
I saw another physician who practiced medicine for the sake of profit among some of the important people in our town.
A dark-skinned boy, with a broken leg near the heel and a wound, presented. The physician, in his ignorance, quickly bound the fracture to the wound with bandages and splints in a tight manner, leaving no breathing space for the wound.
Then, he left it to its own devices, and after several days, forbade him from loosening the bandages until his leg and foot had swollen, and he was on the verge of death.
I was called to him, and quickly loosened the bandages. He found relief and his pain subsided, but the damage had already taken hold in the limb, and I was unable to stop it.
The damage continued to spread in the limb until he died.
I saw another physician treat a cancerous tumor.  After a few days, it ulcerated, greatly harming its owner.  This is because if the cancer is purely of a black humor, it should never be treated with iron.
Unless it is in a limb that can be entirely removed.
Therefore, my son, you should know that manual work is divided into two types: work accompanied by safety, and work that in most cases results in injury.
I have warned in every place in this book about work that involves risk and fear. You should avoid and reject it.
So that the ignorant might not find a way to speak ill and criticize, take care and caution for yourselves, and treat your patients with gentleness and firmness.
Use the best path leading to safety and a good outcome, and avoid dangerous diseases difficult to cure.
Keep yourselves free from that which you fear may introduce doubt into your religion and your worldly affairs, for this is the most lasting path to your honor and the greatest elevation of your status in this world and the hereafter.
Galen, in some of his advice, said, "Do not treat a bad disease, lest you be known as a bad physician.
This article is divided into three chapters.
The first chapter.
The chapter on cauterization with fire and with sharp medicines, arranged from the heel to the foot, with descriptions of the tools, cautery irons, and everything needed for manual work.
The second chapter.
On incision, lancing, bloodletting, cupping, surgeries, removing arrows, and the like, all categorized and arranged, with illustrations of the tools.
The third chapter.
On fractures, dislocations, and the treatment of sprains and the like, categorized and arranged from the heel to the foot, with illustrations of the tools.
The first chapter.
On cauterization.
Before we describe the procedure, it is necessary to describe its benefits and harms, and in what temperament it is used.
For I say that the discussion of the benefits and harms of cauterization is lengthy, a precise science, and a hidden secret, and many wise men have spoken on it.
They differed in their views, and I have condensed the essential parts of their discourse for fear of becoming too lengthy.
I say that cauterization is generally beneficial for every bad temperament, whether with or without a material substance, except for two temperaments: the hot temperament without a substance and the dry temperament without a substance.
As for the dry temperament with a substance, they differed in opinion. Some said that cauterization is beneficial in it, while others argued the opposite, stating that cauterization is unsuitable for an illness arising from heat and dryness, because the nature of fire is heat and dryness.
It is impossible to be cured of a hot and dry ailment with a hot and dry remedy.  The one who disagrees says that cauterization by fire may benefit from a hot and dry illness that occurs in people's bodies. Because when you add a person's body and its moisture to the temperament of fire, you make the person's body cold.
And I say in agreement with him, because experience has revealed this to me on several occasions.  However, one should not presume this without thorough and extensive practice in the art of cauterization. And he must understand the differences in people's temperaments, the nature of diseases, their causes, symptoms, and duration.
As for the other temperaments, there is no fear from them, especially cold and moist illnesses; all physicians agree on their benefit from cauterization.
Know, my children, that the secret of effective treatment with cauterization by fire lies in its superiority over cauterization by a burning drug, because fire is a simple element whose effect is limited to the organ cauterized and it harms another adjacent organ only slightly.
Cauterization with a burning drug may extend its effect to other organs, and may cause a difficult-to-treat disease or even death in the treated organ.  Fire, owing to its noble and pure nature, does not do this unless excessive.
This has been made clear to us through experience, long service, and careful attention to the craft and the facts of the matter.
Therefore, I have dispensed with lengthy explanation; were it not unbefitting this book, I would have presented you with a profound secret concerning fire, how it acts on bodies, and how it eliminates illness using rigorous, philosophical, and demonstrative language that is above your understanding.
Know, my children, that there is disagreement regarding the time suitable for cauterization, and they consider spring to be the best time.
I say that cauterization is suitable in all times, because the harm caused by the season is absorbed by the benefit obtained through cauterization. Especially if the cauterization is for a severe, urgent, and critical ailment that cannot tolerate delay, for fear that it may result in a greater misfortune than the minor harm brought about by the season.
Do not, my children, consider what the common people and ignorant physicians imagine, that the cauterization which cures a disease will never return and make it a curse.
The matter is not as you suppose, for cauterization is like a medicine that changes the temperament and dries the moisture which is the cause of the ailments.
However, cauterization is preferable to medicine because of its rapid success, strong effect, and intense power.
The disease may return at some time in the future, depending on the patient's temperament, the strength and severity of the illness, the accumulation of humours in the body, and neglect of acquiring proper nutrition, and similar causes.
Unless the disease for which cauterization is used is a mild one, affecting a part of the body with few humours and moisture. For example, cauterization of a toothache and similar conditions may not cause the pain to return, and this will be the case at least.
And as for the common saying that cauterization is the final stage of medicine, it is a correct statement, not in the way they think, since they believe that no treatment will be effective, whether with medicine or otherwise, after cauterization.  The matter is otherwise.
The meaning of "cauterization is the final stage of medicine" is that if we have used various treatments for an illness, and those medicines have not been effective, and then we finally use cauterization and it is successful, this is why cauterization is called the final stage of medicine. Not in the sense understood by the common people and many ignorant physicians.
The ancients said that cauterization with gold is better than cauterization with iron.  They said this because of gold's moderation and preciousness. They said that the cauterized area does not become inflamed.  This is not universally true.
Because I have experimented and found that it only happens in some bodies, not others. Cauterization with gold is better than with iron, as they said.  However, if you heat a gold cautery iron in the fire, you cannot tell when it has reached the desired heat for the gold's redness.
Because it cools quickly, and if you increase its heat in the fire, it melts and is molded, causing the craftsman to have trouble.  Therefore, cauterization with iron is faster and more reliable for practice, God willing.
I have arranged this chapter on cauterization into sections, ordered from head to foot, so that the student may easily find what he needs, God willing.
The First Chapter.
Cauterization of the head: one cauterization.
This cauterization is beneficial for excess moisture and cold in the brain, which are causes of headaches, frequent colds from the head to the eyes and ears, excessive sleepiness, toothache, and sore throats. In general, for every illness caused by cold, such as paralysis, epilepsy, and catatonia, and similar illnesses.
The method of this cauterization is that the patient should first be induced to vomit using a purgative medicine that cleanses the head for three or four nights, depending on the patient's strength, age, and usual condition.
Then, you should tell him to shave his head with a razor, and then make him sit before you, squatting, with his hands on his chest.
Then, place the base of your palm on the base of his nose, between his eyes.  Wherever your middle finger ends, mark that spot with ink.
Then, heat the olive-oil cautery iron, which is depicted as follows.
If the bone has been exposed to the extent of a needle's head or a carbuncle, remove your hand.  Otherwise, reheat the iron, or use a different one if it has cooled. Until you see the bone as I described, then take some salt, dissolve it in water, and soak a piece of cotton in it. Place it on the area and leave it for three days.
Then apply a piece of cotton soaked in clarified butter and leave it until the inflammation from the burn subsides.  Then treat it with the four-fold ointment until healed, God willing.
They said that the longer a wound remains open, the more it discharges pus, and this is better and more beneficial.
Some have said that the skin should be cauterized to the bone, holding the cautery until some of the bone's thickness is burned, then the burned bone should be scraped off, and then treated.
Others said that the cauterization should be intense, affecting the bone strongly, until the bone comes off like a small piece of gold or a small flake.  They claimed that this releases the vapors of the head from that area and that the wound should be left open for a long time.
Then treat it until it heals.  I do not consider these two types of cauterization at all, except in some people, and by a risky method. I find leaving it alone is better.  With all due respect, if the head's natural connections are disrupted, as we have seen in other organs, the head weakens.
Especially when the patient's head is naturally weak. The first type of cauterization is safer and better in my opinion. Use it, and you will be safe, God willing.
Second Chapter.
On cauterizing the head also.
If a chronic pain occurs throughout the head, and this affliction persists in the patient, and poultices, plasters, flogging, ointments, and dressings have been used, especially if the single cauterization we described has not helped, then consider. If the patient's head is strong constitutionally, and not weak, and if he experiences severe cold, cauterize again a little above the previous one. Then cauterize a cauterization on each horn of his head until the thickness of the skin is gone and the bone is revealed to the degree we described. Cauterize a cauterization at the back of his head in the place known as the axe, and be gentle in this one, and do not expose the bone.  For the patient will experience intense pain, unlike the pain of all the other head cauterizations.  I will describe this cauterization in its place.
The cautery used for the horns and back of the head should be gentler than the cautery used for the middle of the head.  This is its form.
Chapter Three.
In cauterizing the chronic fistula.
If pain occurs in the fissure of the head, accompanied by headache, and the pain extends to the eye, then the patient should be purged with medications that cleanse the head, and employ the rest of the treatment that I have mentioned in the classifications of diseases. If this does not work, then there are two ways to cauterize it. Either by cauterization with a sharp, burning medicine, or by iron.
As for cauterization with the burning medicine, take one clove of garlic, peel it, and cut its ends from both sides. Then, slit the painful area of the temple with a wide scalpel, until a space is made under the skin that can accommodate the clove.
Then insert it under the skin until it disappears, then apply firm pressure with a bandage, and leave it for about fifteen hours, then loosen it and remove the garlic. Leave the wound for two or three days, then apply a dressing.
Place a cotton swab dipped in clarified butter on it until the area becomes purulent.  Then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing. If you wish, you can do this with some of the burning medicines that I have provided in the eighteenth chapter on burning medicines.
As for cauterizing with iron, it is done in this way: heat the cautery iron, which has this form:.
It is called the nail-shaped cautery because its head resembles a nail, with some protrusions and a small knob in the middle.
Then place it on the painful area and hold your hand while slowly rotating the iron, making the depth of the burn about half the thickness of the skin. Then lift your hand so as not to burn the underlying artery and cause bleeding, then soak a cotton swab in salt water and apply it to the area and leave it for three days. Then soak the cotton in oil and treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing.
If you wish, cauterize this vein with the sharp, pointed end of the cautery, and be careful not to cut the artery in this particular, non-chronic vein.
Chapter Four.
Cauterizing the chronic vein.
If you have treated the vein with the previous treatments mentioned and what we have described in the divisions of diseases, and the treatment is ineffective, and you see a cause that cannot be resolved by the initial cautery with medicine or cautery with fire. Then you should heat the sharp cautery until it glows white, after marking the painful area with a line about half a finger in length or similar.  Hold your hand, pulling tightly, once, until the artery is severed and approaches the bone.
However, you must be careful not to touch the jaw, which moves when chewing, lest you burn the muscle or nerve that moves it, causing spasm.
Be cautious and vigilant against the bleeding of the artery you have severed, for there is risk in severing it. Especially for someone who does not know what to do and has not been experienced in this, abandoning the action is preferable.
Treatment of accidental bleeding from the artery will be discussed in its place in the book, God willing, if you see a condition for which this cauterization is not suitable.
If you see the patient's body able to withstand it, cauterize a spot in the middle of the head as described, and treat the wound until it heals, God willing.
If you wish, use the cauterization mentioned in the chapter on artery ligation with the two-bladed cautery, for it is a better and more effective cauterization than this.
Fifth Chapter.
On cauterizing earaches.
If an earache occurs due to cold and has been treated with laxatives and other treatments mentioned in the classification, but the pain has not subsided, cauterize the ear with the cautery called the "point," whose image is this.
Then, after heating it, drop it around the entire ear, or around both ears if the pain is in both. Move the cauterization slightly away from the base of the ear after marking the spot with ink.  The cauterization should be about ten drops for each ear, or thereabouts. Then treat the area until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Six.
In cauterizing the fistula.
The fistula treated by cauterization is of the type that arises from phlegm, as I mentioned in the divisions of diseases.
One should avoid cauterizing the type that arises from dryness and nerve spasm.
If you have treated this type of fistula with poultices, evacuations, and gargles, and your treatment has been unsuccessful, then the afflicted area should be cauterized three times. One at the base of the ear, the second slightly below the temple, and the third at the junction of the lips.
Make your cauterization on the opposite side of the affected area, because relaxation occurs on the side that appears healthy.
The cauterization should be performed with one cautery at the point opposite the upper part of the ear, slightly below the crown of the head, and another in the temple, its length equal to the length of the thumb.
Lower your cauterizing instrument until you burn approximately half the thickness of the skin. This is the image of the cautery, a type of knife, whose image we previously presented, but somewhat gentler, as you see. The knife should have a slight excess of thickness, then treat the area as previously mentioned until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Seven.
In cauterization of chronic stroke.
If the stroke becomes chronic and you treat it as we have mentioned, and your treatment is unsuccessful, and there is no fever in the patient, cauterize four times. One cauterization on each temple, one cauterization in the middle of the head, as we mentioned, and one cauterization at the back of the head, as previously stated.
The cauterization method, as previously mentioned, and one may also cauterize the mouth of the stomach, which would be more effective, then treat as previously mentioned.
Chapter Eight.
On cauterization for forgetfulness.
The one caused by phlegm should first be given large purgatives and cleansing grains for the brain.
Then his entire head should be shaved, and a mustard poultice, as described in the chapter on dressings, should be applied to his back repeatedly, for it is a form of cauterization.
And do this in the same manner as mentioned there.  If he is cured by this, then if not, cauterize him three times on the back of his head, positioned in a line from the top of his head to the bottom of his neck.
Make the space between each cauterization the thickness of a finger. Then treat the cauterization as previously described. If you wish to add more, and the patient can tolerate it, cauterize the middle cauterization.
If you wish to increase it, cauterize at the temples, then treat him until he is cured.  The cauterization should be an olive-shaped one, as previously described.
Chapter Nine.
On cauterization for paralysis and relaxation of the entire body.
First, cleanse the head with the washes we mentioned.  Then shave the patient's head and cauterize one cauterization in the center of the head, one cauterization on each temple, and one on the back of the head.
And three on the cervical vertebrae.  If, in a case of relaxation of the body, more than this is needed, and the patient can tolerate it, and the illness is strong and firmly established, cauterize four cauterizations on his spinal vertebrae. Cauterize deeply until most of the skin is burned, then lift your hand, and treat it as previously described until it is healed, God willing.  Use an olive-oil cautery iron.
Chapter Ten.
On the cauterization of epilepsy.
Only the epileptic whose epilepsy is caused by phlegm should be cauterized.  First, one should cleanse his brain with large syringes and with all other treatments mentioned in the section, if the patient is large and can tolerate the medications.
But if he is a child who cannot tolerate the medications, then use gargles and cleansing rinses for the brain many days beforehand, while also improving his diet.
Then shave his head, then cauterize with one cautery in the middle of the head, as described previously, and another cautery in the back of the head. And on each temple, a cautery.  If the patient is strong and can tolerate it, cauterize with the cauteries mentioned in the section on paralysis and relaxation of the body, on the vertebrae of the neck and the vertebrae of the spine.  The cautery should be olive-shaped, according to the method mentioned previously.
If the patient is a child, make the cautery gentle in this way.
Chapter Eleven.
On cauterizing melancholia.
If the cause of melancholia is putrid moisture and thick phlegm, cauterize with the cauteries we mentioned in the section on paralysis.
If the cause of melancholia is an excess of black bile and the patient's body is damp and putrid, purge it to purify the brain as previously described in the section on classification.
Then shave the patient's head, then make a tight cake of linen like a circle, then place it on the center of his head. The patient sits upright holding the cake from all sides. Then take one pound of old sheep's butter.
Then heat it moderately over the fire, as much as the finger can tolerate when inserted, then pour it into the circle on the center of his head and let it cool.  Do this once a week for the patient, along with good overall treatment, until he is healed, God willing.
And if you wish, cauterize him with many small, scattered points, without holding the cautery in your hand, but rather by a kind of puffing or slight touch.  This type of cauterization moistens the brain moderately.
Then apply a cotton pad soaked in butter or chicken fat.
Chapter twelve.
On cauterizing water flowing from the eye.
If you initially observe the symptoms of water flowing from the eye as described in the classifications, then immediately treat the afflicted person by cleaning their head and protecting it from all moisture. Have them sweat in a bath on an empty stomach for several days.
Then shave their head and cauterize a spot in the middle of the head, and then cauterize twice on the temples if the water flows from both eyes, or from one side only if the water flows from one eye.
Then, with the cautery, cut all the veins and arteries under the skin.  The cauterizations should extend along the length and breadth of the temples, and protect from bleeding.
If you see anything of it [a vessel], cut it at the point by whatever treatment is possible. We will come to the wisdom of ligating and cutting arteries and preventing bleeding.
Two strong cauterizations may be applied to the back of the head, under the bones.
Chapter Thirteen.
On the cauterization of chronic weeping.
If the tears of the eye are chronic and persistent, and originate from veins and arteries on the exterior of the head, and you are certain that it is due to excessive cold, thick, phlegmatic humor, cauterize it with the cauterization described previously for the initial weeping. A cauterization in the middle of the head, and two cauterizations on the temples, and two cauterizations at the back of the head below the bones.  If further cauterization is required, apply a cauterization on each side of the eye's tail, at the edge of the eyebrow, with a small cautery iron.
Chapter Fourteen.
On cauterizing a foul-smelling nose.
If treatment with what we have previously mentioned in the section on classifications does not work, promptly cauterize the patient's skull with the cauterizing pot three nights, then shave his head and cauterize the middle area with the olive-oil cautery iron. Then cauterize with the nail-shaped cautery iron, two cauterizations above the eyebrows, slightly beneath the hair. Be careful to avoid the artery—do not cut it.  This is an image of the nail-shaped cautery.
Chapter Fifteen.
On cauterizing relaxation of the eyelid.
If the eyelid is relaxed due to disease or moisture, cauterize the eyelid once with this crescent-shaped cautery.
And if you wish, cauterize slightly above the eyebrows, two cauterizations in each direction, keeping a distance from the temples.  Each cauterization should be the length of the eyebrow.
Do not overdo the cauterization, but only to the point where one-third of the skin is burned.  The cautery should be of this form.
Chapter Sixteen.
On cauterizing the eyelid.
If the eyelids turn inward, injuring the eye, then there are two types of cauterization for it. Either cauterization with fire or cauterization with a burning medicine.  As for cauterizing with fire, you should first instruct the patient to let their eyelids grow out if they pluck them, so that they become long and even.
If it injures them when they are growing, then bind his eyes with a bandage so that they do not move until they grow.  When they grow and become even, place the patient's head on your lap, then mark the eyelid with ink in the shape of a leaf of an as-tree.
Begin the mark near the eyelashes, then place a cotton swab soaked in egg white or in the saliva of the *bazr qutun* under the eyelid, then heat the cautery—this is its image.
Then cauterize in the shape you marked, little by little, in many applications, until the surface of the skin, which is in the shape of the entire as-leaf, is burned.  A sign of your successful work is seeing the eyelid has shrunk and the hair has lifted from the eye's flesh.
Then raise your hand, and leave it for three days.  Then apply a cotton swab with ghee until the scab comes off, then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing. If any hair returns after a while, and the eyelid relaxes.
Then repeat the cauterization on that spot as you did at first. If the hair is on the lower eyelid, cauterize it until it returns to its natural position and does not injure the eye.  As for cauterization with a burning medicine, instruct the patient to leave the eyelashes to grow long and even.
Then make an image of an as-leaf from paper, then take from the usual soap and quicklime, one dirham or its equivalent of each, grind them thoroughly, doing so quickly so they do not dry. Then spread some of it onto the paper, shaped like an as-leaf, and place it on the one or both eyelids.  Place a cotton swab soaked in egg white under the eye, and the patient's head in your lap.
Place your index finger over the medicine and gently press it, moving it whenever the patient feels the medicine's sting, because it feels like fire.  As long as they feel the sting, leave the medicine and move it with your finger. If the sting subsides, remove the medicine and wash the eye with water.
And observe. If you see the eyelid has risen as it does when cauterized with fire or cut, otherwise, reapply the medicine to the area where it did not take effect and did not turn black, until you have completed your work and the eye is cauterized. Then apply cotton with oil until the burn subsides.
Then treat it with the palm ointment or another, until it heals.  You should be extremely careful during the process to avoid getting any medicine in the eye.  If the eyelid relaxes after a few days and we fear something (hair?) in the eye.
Then reapply the medicine to the relaxed area of the eyelid, just as you did at first, and treat it until it heals. This is a picture of an as-leaf.
Know that people's eyes differ in size and age.  Therefore, your cauterization should be adjusted accordingly.  The correct method is not hidden from one with experience in this craft.
Chapter Seventeen.
On cauterizing the fistula that appears in the eye's entrance.
If you have treated the fistula as described in the sections on diseases, and your treatment was unsuccessful, then it is necessary to cauterize it in this manner.
Command the patient to place his head in your lap, and have an attendant hold the head firmly, without movement or shaking.
Then, place a cotton swab soaked in egg white or in the juice of the wild cucumber on his eyes. Then, heat the cautery iron, which is illustrated in this image.
It should be hollowed out, shaped like the shaft of a hawk's feather, on the side where the cauterization is performed. If you wish, it can be perforated on the other end, or if you wish, it can be solid, like a rolling pin, but this hollowed shape is the better for your work, God willing.
Then, squeeze the fistula if it is open, and remove the matter from it and dry it; or if it is not open, open it and remove the pus, then place the very hot cautery iron on it and hold it in your hand until you reach the bone.
Keep your hand slightly away from the eye when cauterizing, towards the nose, so that your hand does not miss or the patient becomes restless, and the cautery iron does not fall on the eyelid and damage it; if you reach the bone on the first cauterization, then do so; otherwise, reheat the cautery iron again if necessary.
Leave it for three days, then apply a cotton swab with ghee and treat it with the dried ointment until it heals. If forty days pass and it does not heal, apply the strong, corrosive medicine so that the bone is exposed, and then treat it as described in the relevant chapter, God willing.
Another method of cauterizing the fistula, mentioned by some early scholars, is to carefully cut the fistula, then insert a thin funnel into the cut. This is its form.
Pour into it, a quantity of molten lead weighing a dirham and hold the funnel in your hand firmly and tightly, and do not move the patient at all so that the molten lead does not drip into his/her eye.  It is necessary to place a cotton swab soaked in egg white or water over the patient's eye.
The lead will burn the fistula and cure it remarkably. If the fistula is cured by the cauterization and treatment described, then there is no need for further action. Otherwise, one must use a nasal perforation and return the fistula to the nasal passage, as will be described in the dedicated section.
Chapter Eighteen.
Cauterization of cleft lip.
A cleft in the lip, called a fissure, frequently occurs, especially in the lips of boys.  If the treatment described in the section does not work, apply a small, knife-like cautery iron to it in this manner.
Make its cavity as thin as a knife, then apply it heated quickly to the cleft itself until the cauterization reaches the depth of the cleft. Then treat it with the *qiyruqi* until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Nineteen.
Cauterization of the fistula occurring in the mouth.
If a tumor appears at the base of the gums, or in the palate, or at the roots of the teeth, then it ulcerates and bursts, and a fistula develops from the discharge of pus, and if treatment is unsuccessful, a cautery should be heated to a size appropriate for the fistula.
Then, insert it hot into the opening of the fistula, and hold it until the heated iron reaches its bottom and end. Do this once or twice. Then treat it afterwards with the treatment we mentioned, until it heals, God willing.
If the matter is cut off and it heals, otherwise, the area must be examined and the corrupted bone removed, as will be explained in its section, God willing.
Chapter Twenty.
Loose Teeth and Gums.
If the gums have become loose due to moisture, and the teeth have moved, and treatment with medications has been ineffective, then place the patient's head on your lap.  Heat the cautery, whose image will follow after this, then place the tube on the tooth.
Quickly insert the heated cautery, and hold your hand there for a moment until the patient feels the heat of the fire reaching the root of the tooth. Then remove your hand. Then, repeat the application of the cautery as desired. Afterward, the patient should fill their mouth with salt water.
He should hold it for an hour and then spit it out.  The moving tooth will become firm, the loose gums will tighten, and the putrid moisture will dry up.
Chapter Twenty-One.
On cauterizing toothache.
If the toothache is due to cold or worms, and if treatment with medicines is ineffective, then cauterization can be done in two ways: either with oil or with fire.
For cauterization with oil, take cow's oil, heat it in an iron spoon or a dish.  Then take a cotton ball, wrap it around the tip of a rod, dip it in the heated oil, and place it quickly on the aching tooth.  Hold it until it cools.
Then repeat this several times until the heat reaches the root of the tooth. If you wish, dip wool or cotton in cold oil, place it on the aching tooth, and place the heated iron on top until the heat reaches the base of the tooth.
As for cauterization with fire, take a brass or iron tube, somewhat thick-walled so the heat of the fire does not reach the patient's mouth. Then heat the cautery iron, place it on the tooth, and hold it until the iron cools. Repeat this several times.
The pain will disappear either that same day or the next.  Following the cauterization, the patient should fill their mouth with good oil and hold it for an hour, then spit it out.  This is a picture of the cautery iron.
Cauterize with any desired end, as is appropriate.  This is a picture of the tube.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
On cauterizing pigs.
If pigs are afflicted with phlegm and cold humors, and do not respond to maturation with medications, and you desire rapid maturation, heat the hollow cautery iron, whose image is shown here, with both ends pierced.
To make smoke emerge from the other end during cauterization, place it over the tumor repeatedly, if necessary, until reaching the tumor's depth.  If the tumor is small, make the cautery iron the size of the tumor.
Then leave it for three days and apply a cotton swab dipped in ghee until what was burned by fire is gone.  Then treat it with ointment and lint until it is healed, God willing.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
In cauterization for hoarseness of voice and shortness of breath.

Be careful not to reach the throat with the cautery, and burn only half of the skin.  Then cauterize again at the neck joint, at the end of the last bead, using a strong, nail-shaped cautery, in the manner previously described.
Then treat him with what was mentioned previously until he is healed, God willing.
The twenty-fourth chapter.
On cauterizing lung disease and coughing.
If the cough and lung disease are caused by cold moisture, and the patient does not have fever or consumption, and the disease is chronic, cauterize twice above the collarbones, in the soft, empty, depressed areas, and once more in the center of the chest between the breasts.
The cautery should be pointed, as previously described.  If you prefer, the cauterization may be by dripping with the cautery called the 'point', the design of which was previously illustrated in the section on treating earaches.  The number of points should be approximately thirty.
Then treat him with what has been previously mentioned until he is cured.  You can also make a cautery with three prongs, in this style.  Use this cautery quickly, as it allows for three cauterizations in one application.  This is the design of the cautery.
Chapter Twenty-five.
On cauterizing the armpit.
If the head of the humerus dislocates due to slippery moisture or if it is not fixed when put back into place, so that it frequently dislocates and dislocates again with the slightest movement, as we have observed, then the joint should first be repositioned. Then the patient lies on their back or on their right side, and the skin inside the armpit is lifted upward with the fingers of your left hand, if the joint has dislocated inward.
Then the double-pronged cautery, which is shown in this image, is heated.
Then the skin is cauterized with it until it penetrates to the other side. The shape of the cauterization is four cauterizations. It may also be cauterized with a three-pronged cautery, in which case the shape of the cauterization will be six cauterizations, and the prongs are applied on the thinness of the tissue.
This is a picture of the three-pronged cautery.
And one more may be added to this number, making eight cauterizations. Then, crushed balls mixed with salt are applied to the cauterization. Then, the patient must rest and not move the limb for a while until it strengthens.  If the dislocation is upward, it rarely occurs.
Cauterize above the shoulder blade with one good, nail-like cauterization, or with many small, dotted cauterizations; the joint will become firm, moisture will disappear, and the patient will recover.
Chapter Twenty-six.
On cauterizing the stomach.
If the stomach has contracted a cold and excessive moisture, disturbing its temperament, and frequent discharges occur despite treatment with various remedies, then the patient should lie on his back, stretching out his legs and arms, and then be cauterized three times. Cauterize under the breastbone, to a depth of a finger's breadth, using a nail-like cauterizing iron.  Place two more cauterizations below, on either side of the first, so the cauterizations form a triangle, keeping them spaced apart to prevent confluence if they ulcerate.  The depth of the cauterizations should be approximately two-thirds the thickness of the skin, and the pattern should be triangular.
In this manner and to this extent, no more.
And if you wish, cauterize it with one large cauterization in the middle of the stomach, in this manner.
And the cauterizing iron used for this single cauterization should be circular in shape, in this manner.
And the stomach may be cauterized by puncturing, for those who are apprehensive of this cauterization. Chapter Twenty-Seven.
On cauterizing the cold liver.
If a pain in the liver arises from coldness and dampness, or from a hot wind, to the point that it has departed from its natural state in excess, and the patient has been treated as described in the classification, but that treatment was unsuccessful. Then the patient should lie on their back, and three cauterizations should be marked with ink on the liver, in this shape and exact size, beneath the collarbones, where a person's elbow ends.
And each cauterization should be the thickness of a finger, and the cauterization should be straight along the body. Do not press the cautery iron firmly.
The burn should be about half the thickness of the skin, no more.  The patient should be standing on their feet, or if not able to stand, lying with legs extended and arms raised.  This is the shape of the cautery iron.
You may be able to cauterize these areas with a knife-like cautery iron, if you have skill and care in the craft, taking care not to overdo the cauterization and burn all the skin, thus burning the abdomen and reaching the intestines. The skin there is thin; know this.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
Cauterization of a liver tumor.
If an abscess appears in the liver, and you wish to know whether the tumor is in the liver tissue or in its membrane, if it is in the liver tissue, the patient will experience a heavy, dull pain.
If it is in the liver membrane, there will be a sharp, intense pain with it. If you see that the physicians have exhausted treatment, the patient should lie on their back, and then mark the swollen area with ink.
Then heat the cautery in the fire, the cautery that resembles a file, and this is its image. Cauterize it with a single cautery until all the skin is burned and the cautery reaches the membrane, so that all the pus comes out, then treat it with the treatment for abscesses until it heals.
This type of cauterization should only be used by those whose medical experience is extensive and who have treated such illnesses by repeated practice.  Only then should they undertake such a procedure; to leave it to me is better.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
On cauterizing the fistula.
The ancients recommended cauterizing cold fistulas with the roots of the zarawand plant.  One takes a single, long, dry root of the zarawand, as thick as a finger.  Then, it is dipped in oil and held over the fire. Then, apply one cauterization between the collarbone and the neck, and two small cauterizations slightly slanted towards the area below the chin, avoiding the veins, and two large cauterizations above the breasts, between the third and fourth ribs.
And two more cauterizations between the fifth and sixth ribs, slightly slanted backwards, another in the middle of the chest, and one above the stomach.  Three cauterizations from behind, one between the shoulders and two on either side of the lower back, below the cauterization that is between the collarbones.
One should not apply the cauterization deeply, but rather superficially on the skin's surface, as a cauterization.  Some of the ancients have mentioned that some people use a heated iron implement shaped like a blade, heating it and inserting it between the ribs until it reaches the tumor itself.
The pus will drain, as we mentioned in the case of a liver tumor.  In this affliction, cauterization poses a danger; the patient may die immediately, or a persistent ulcer may form at the site.
Chapter Thirty.
In cauterizing the spleen.
If the treatment of spleen disease as described in the classifications was ineffective, then cauterization can be applied in three ways, each correct: one is to cauterize three or four times along the length of the spleen.
In the shape of the liver cauterizations previously described, with a space between each cauterization the thickness of a finger, or slightly more.  The cautery should have the same characteristics as mentioned for liver cauterization. Do not go too deep with the cautery, and the patient should lie on their back.
The other method is at the elbow.  Lift the skin opposite the spleen, where the patient's left elbow ends. Lift the skin across the width of the body so the cauterizations are along the body.
Then, insert the cauterizing probes very carefully until they pierce the skin on the other side. Then remove the cautery, resulting in four cauterizations.  If you wish, use the three-probe cautery.
Then treat the cauterized area after allowing it to discharge pus for many days. This is more effective than any other treatment mentioned.
Chapter Thirty-one.
On cauterizing dropsy.
Cauterization is useful primarily in dropsy of the abdomen. If, after treating the dropsy with the methods of treatment we mentioned in the classification, your treatment is unsuccessful, you should cauterize four times around the navel and once on the stomach.
Another cauterization on the liver, one on the spleen, and two behind the back, one opposite the chest and the other opposite the stomach. The depth of the cauterization should be approximately the thickness of the skin. Then the cauterization should be left open to discharge pus for a long time.
Do not neglect the patient after the cauterization with appropriate treatment so that the two concerned parties will be united to expedite healing. The shape of the cauterizing iron used for the back is pointed, as previously described, and the shape of the one used for the back is olive-shaped.
Chapter Thirty-two.
On cauterizing the feet and legs.
If they swell in dropsy and fill with yellow water, it is necessary to cauterize on the back of the foot at the point between the little finger and ring finger.
Place your hand with the cauterizing iron straight, do not bend it, then lift your hand and do not return it, for it will drain the yellow water, and the cauterizing iron will be in this form.
Then cauterize the legs, two cauterizations on each leg, using the sharp edge of the cauterizing iron.  Make the cauterizations along the length of the leg, one below the knee and another below that, towards the middle of the leg.  And cauterize each thigh, two cauterizations on each.
Leave the cauterization open without treatment for a long time, allowing the water to drain, then treat it with the rest of the methods we mentioned.
Chapter Thirty-three.
On cauterizing diarrhea.

Chapter Thirty-five.
On cauterizing warts.

Chapter Thirty-six.
On cauterizing the fistula in the perineum and its surrounding areas.
If the patient does not respond to the incision and treatment described in its place, and avoids it, perhaps he will be cured by cauterization.  If a fistula occurs in someone and pus and corrupt humours flow from it for a long time, the first thing you should do is measure it with a thin probe.
Then, determine the depth of its penetration with the probe, then heat the cautery iron, which resembles a nail, and insert it hot into the fistula itself, aligned with the depth of the fistula and the extent the probe reached. Cauterize it again until all those corrupted substances are burned, once, twice, or three times, as needed.
And beware of burning a nerve if there is one, or a large vein, if the fistula leads to the bladder or to the intestines.
And be mindful of all these places; you should only do this if the fistula is in a fleshy area and you judge it to be non-perforating.  Then treat the area until it heals. If the area seals, the matter ceases to flow from it, and it remains like that for a time, then know that it has healed completely.
And if the matter does not cease flowing from it, then know that it is perforated, or there is a corrupted bone within its depth, or something similar to that which will be mentioned in its proper place.

Chapter Forty.
On cauterization of hip dislocation.
False humors may accumulate in the hip joint, causing dislocation.  A sign of this is that one leg will be longer than the other when compared.  If you find an empty space at the site of the dislocation, a nearly circular cautery should be applied to the affected area of the hip joint itself.
After applying the cautery around the joint, as if it were rotating so that the joint lies in the center of the circle, use the cautery depicted in the stomach cauterization procedure. If that is unavailable, use three cauteries with the large olive-shaped cautery.
Make the cauterization deep enough to reach all the skin, then treat it until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Forty-One.
Cauterization of the sciatica.
If pain occurs in the hip joint, caused by cold and dampness, and treatment as described in the classification was ineffective and prolonged, then the patient should be relieved of the thick humors with a powdered rotten substance or a powdered prickly pear, or similar.
Then cauterize it.  Cauterization has two types: either with burning medicines or with fire. Cauterization with fire can be done in several ways, one of which is to cauterize the hip joint itself three times in a triangular shape like this.
This produces a beneficial depth.  The interval between each cauterization should be the width of a finger. The cauterizing implement should be olive-colored.  A cauterization could be applied in the center on the head of the hip joint, making four cauterizations in total.  Alternatively, you may cauterize it with the circular method previously mentioned for the dislocation of the hip, with a single cauterization.
So that the circle surrounds the entire hip and burns all the thickness of the skin.  This is the method of cauterization for sciatica if the pain is confined to the hip itself and does not extend to the thigh and leg.
If the pain extends to the thigh and leg, cauterize the thigh twice at the location the patient indicates as painful, and once above the heel (ankle), a few finger-widths towards the outer side.  The cauterizing implement should be a knife-like one, and the depth of the cauterization should be only as deep as the skin's thickness.
If the patient indicates that the pain extends to the toes, cauterize the area he points out with the pointed cautery three, four, or more times as needed. If he indicates pain below the knee towards the leg, cauterize once there with a knife-like cautery.
In all your cauterizations, be careful not to reach a nerve or a large artery, lest you cause the patient a serious or protracted injury.
I have observed cases where someone cauterized above the heel, exceeding the necessary extent, causing the leg to swell to the point that the foot became gangrenous and completely decayed, ruining the entire limb.  Subsequently, diarrhea and death followed. If the pain is in both areas, cauterize them in the same manner.
Some learned physicians have described a hip cauterization using a cautery instrument shaped like a cup made of iron, with a diameter of half a cubit.  The rim of the cup is as thick as a date pit, or slightly thinner. Inside this cup is another cup, and a third cup within that.
The cups should be separated by the width of a thumb's breadth.  All the cups should be open on both sides, and their height should be about one or two thumb's breadths.  A handle of iron, firmly fixed to the cups, should also be included. This is its form.
Then it is heated in the fire until it glows red and sparks, and is placed on the affected hip area, with the patient leaning on their healthy side. Three circular cauterizations are made at once, then the patient is left for three days, after which the wound is bandaged with ghee and the wound is left open for several days.
Then, it is treated with ointment until healed.  The author of this book stated that this type of cautery is rarely used due to its unpleasantness and frightening appearance.  Few can endure it.  However, it is a good cautery for those who can endure it, and for those who have been afflicted in that area.
As for cauterization with burning medicines, it is done by making two cups like the rings into which the hinges of doors are placed, made of copper or iron.
They should have a height of about two fingerbreadths, and the distance between them should be about the thickness of a finger.  The bottom and top should be open, and they are held together in this manner.
Then, place them on the affected hip area, with the patient lying on their healthy side. Gently hold the cup, then pour a little hot, sharp water between the circles. Hold it for about an hour's worth of sand.  The patient must endure the stinging and burning, for it will feel like fire.
Until the stinging subsides. Then lift the instrument and wipe all the sharp water from the hip with fresh water.  Leave it for three days, and apply fat to it until the black marks of the burning disappear.  Leave it for some days until pus runs out, for this is most beneficial.
Then treat it with ointments until it heals. If the pain extends to the thigh or leg, make a mold for it to hold the water, just like you did for the hip. This is the description of sharp water, and I have established it in my treatise on the correction of medicines.
Take one part of potash and one part of quicklime.  Grind them both and place them in a new pot, which has a single small hole at the bottom, just big enough for a rod to enter.  Place another, glazed pot under the bottom of the first pot.
Pour fresh water over the potash and quicklime to cover them by a finger's width, after you have thoroughly pressed them with your hand. Leave the pot until the sharp water collects at the bottom of the glazed pot.  Then gather all of that water and pour it over another, freshly prepared, batch of lime and potash.
Then it will be extremely potent, useful in many medical procedures and for cauterizing other parts of the body, because it acts like fire itself.  Some medicines can also cauterize the hip, such as safflower, baladi honey, and lime with soap, ground up.
Galen, recounting an ancient treatment for hip pain and sciatica, highly praised it, claiming it needed no other treatment and cured the condition in a single application.
Even to the point that a person might be carried into a bath and emerge cured.  This involves taking fresh green shiṭraj, or if unavailable, fresh dried shiṭraj.  Grind it with some fat and apply it to the hip, leg, or thigh where the pain is.
Hold it in place for three hours, or as long as the patient feels the burning subside. Then, take them to a bath.  If their body is damp, place them in a basin. The pain will go away and they will be cured, God willing. If not cured, repeat the treatment after ten days.
Then he will be cured, God willing.  Know that no one should use these cauterizing medicines until the body is emptied. Dioscorides mentions that goat's dung, when used to cauterize sciatica, is beneficial.
The cauterization should be done as follows: soak wool in old oil, and place it on the deep spot between the thumb and the elbow, closer to the elbow.  Then, take dried goat's dung.
Then heat it in the fire until it becomes embers, then place it on the wool and let it extinguish.  Then take another, and continue doing this until the sensation reaches from the middle of the upper arm to the hip, and the pain subsides, God willing.  This type of cauterization is called cauterization with dung.
Chapter Forty-Two.
Concerning cauterization for back pain.
Pain in the back may arise from numerous causes, such as a fall, a blow, or excessive vomiting, and the like.  It may stem from the effusion of cold, damp material.  Cauterization is only effective in this specific type of case.
After the patient has vomited, using a remedy like a rotten herb, he should be cauterized on his back, where the pain is, in three rows the width of the table itself.  Having marked the area with ink, there should be five cauterizations, or more, in each row, according to the patient's tolerance and strength.
The cauterization should be done with a small cauterizing iron, or if preferred, with a medium-sized nail-shaped cauterizing iron, in three or four applications, in this manner.
Chapter Forty-three.
In cauterizing the initial stage of a lump.
This ailment often affects young children.  A sign of its early stage in a child is shortness of breath when standing or moving, and you will find a prominent bead, protruding beyond the other beads, at the end of the spinal vertebrae.
If you see this and wish to stop it, cauterize it with a circular cauterizing iron, in this manner. Cauterize each side of the vertebra evenly. And remember to use cauterization in the lump that arises from nerve spasm.
Chapter Forty-Four.
On cauterizing gout and joint pain.
If joint pains are due to cold moisture flowing to any part of the body, and if the pains occur in the legs, physicians customarily call it gout, especially.

Chapter forty-five.
On cauterizing hernias.
If a hernia appears in the groin, and some intestines and intestines descend to the testicle, and this is a recent occurrence, the patient should be instructed to refrain from eating that day and to use laxatives to evacuate his bowels.
Then, he should lie on his back before you, and you should instruct him to restrain himself until the intestines or bowels are expelled.  Then, you push them back with your finger, and mark the area under the hernia on the pubic bone with ink, a mark resembling a semicircle with its ends toward the upper body.
Then, heat a cauterizing iron, its appearance is...
...until it is white and emitting sparks. Then, return the intestines or bowels to their place, and have a servant place his hand over the spot so the bowels do not protrude again.  Spread the patient's legs apart, place a cushion beneath him, and another servant holds his legs.
And another on his chest, holding his hands.  Then, lower the cauterizing iron onto the mark itself, your hand holding the iron straight, until it reaches the bone; or repeat the process if it did not reach the bone on the first attempt.
And exert your effort to prevent the bowels from protruding while cauterizing, so as not to burn the patient, which would cause either death or great harm.  Know that if the cauterization does not reach the bone, your effort will be unsuccessful.  The cauterizing iron should be appropriate in size for children and adults.
Then, treat the cauterized spot after three days with ghee to remove the harshness of the fire.  Then treat it with other ointments until healed.  Let the patient lie on his back for forty days until the wound is sealed.
His food during his treatment should soften his bowels so the intestines do not protrude during bowel movements.  If he wishes to stand up after forty days, use a tight bandage and keep it on for another forty days.  Reduce exertion, overeating, drinking, and loud noises.
If he follows this procedure, he will be completely healed, God willing.  I will discuss the treatment of hernias by incision in its proper chapter, God willing.  As for a hernia that occurs throughout the abdomen and is new, if you want to prevent its growth, cauterize a circular area around the hernia, appropriate to its size. Let the burnt skin be about one-third of its thickness, then treat it as previously described.  It will not increase, God willing.  Some earlier authorities mentioned cauterizing the hernia with a triangular cautery in this manner.
After marking the hernia with ink, making a line across the top and another to the bottom of the hernia, and placing your hand in the middle with one single nail-like cauterization, the first cauterization is easier and better, God willing.
Chapter Forty-Six.
On cauterizing the fistula.
If, in some organ, a fall or blow causes pain that persists for a long time and is not relieved by medication, it is necessary to cauterize with a pinpoint cautery. The cauterization of each organ should be in accordance with its size, smallness, weakness, and strength.
And if the pain and fistula are removed by the first cauterization, then he is healed. Otherwise, repeat the cauterization, for it is a characteristic of these ailments that they spread from the organ to its neighboring areas.  Therefore, the cauterization must be continued until the patient is healed.
Chapter Forty-Seven.
On cauterizing leprosy.
As for those afflicted with leprosy, they may benefit greatly from cauterization, especially those forms of leprosy caused by the putrefaction of phlegm and black bile.
If you wish to cauterize him, observe. One in the usual center of the head, the second below it towards the forehead at the end of the hair, two on the temples, and one from behind on the nape of the neck. Apply the cauterization somewhat vigorously, so that it affects the bone slightly.
Scales will peel off, making it easier for the thick vapors to escape.  Cauterize the spleen in the same manner as previously mentioned. If the leprosy has persisted and become evident, you should cauterize the head with the previously mentioned cauterizations. One cauterization on the tip of the nose, two on each cheek, two on the cervical vertebrae, six on the thoracic vertebrae, one large one on the coccyx, another above it on the same table, and two on each thigh. Two on each knee, one on each, two on the shoulders, two on the elbows, two on the ribs of the chest, and cauterize each joint of his fingers and toes, and each heel and wrist.
Preserve the nerves behind the heels so as not to burn them.  A cauterization may be applied to the pubic bone, another to the stomach opening, and another to the liver.  Know that the more cauterizations, the more beneficial and effective.
Know that the patient feels less pain from cauterization than a healthy person because their body has become numb.  The cauteries should be of varying sizes, according to the organs and joints, as previously described for the instruments.

Chapter Forty-eight.
On cauterizing numbness.  If a part of the body is numb, and treatment with medications, ointments, and dressings is ineffective, cauterize the same numb part with cauteries, according to the size of the affected part.  Let your cauteries be slightly deeper in the thickness of the skin.
Then treat it with ointment until it heals.  Cauterization may also be used for some numbness that affects the hand and foot in the vertebrae of the spine, at the exit point of the nerve that moves that limb, and this will remove the numbness.
