Chapter Forty-nine.
On cauterizing leprosy.
If leprosy persists and no medical treatment is effective, cauterize it with a slight depth, commensurate with the thickness of the skin, so that the whiteness disappears and its color changes.
Then treat it with lentil flour, rose oil,  celandine leaves, dove's blood, or hawk's blood, each ingredient one part. Mix them together, apply the mixture to a cloth, and keep it on the affected area until it heals.
Chapter Fifty.
On cauterizing cancer.
If the cancer is in its early stages and you wish to stop its progress, cauterize it with a circular iron around it, moving it in a circle.  Some wise men advise a strong cauterization in the center, but I do not agree, as I fear it will ulcerate, and I have seen this happen several times.
Therefore, the correct approach is to cauterize around it in a circle, as we said, or with many small cauterizations.
Chapter Fifty-one.
On cauterizing the fistula.
If a fistula has developed and is slow to mature, either due to the agent that caused it, or the age of the patient (if they are elderly with little blood), or due to time, and you wish to hasten its maturation, then cauterize around it with many small, dotted cauterizations.
Then leave it, as this will hasten its maturation.  If you wish to further cauterize it, heat the cautery iron, which is of this form.
And place it in the center of the fistula, until it penetrates the skin. Let the cauterization be towards the bottom so that the discharge of pus is easier, then treat it as appropriate until it heals.
Chapter Fifty-two.
On cauterizing ulcers.
Ulcers are nothing but corruption that advances in the limb, consuming it as fire consumes dry wood. If you see an ulcer in a place suitable for cauterization with fire, heat a cautery iron with many small and large nails, according to what is appropriate for the location of the ulcer.
Then cauterize it from every side until all corruption is eradicated, leaving nothing behind. Then leave it for three days and apply powdered sulfur with oil to the cauterized areas until all the scab and all the corruption are removed.
Then treat it with healing ointments. If after three days you see that the flesh grows healthily without any corruption, then leave it alone. Otherwise, repeat the cauterization on any remaining corrupted areas. Ulcers can also be treated with a strong medicine.
It acts as a substitute for fire, providing quicker relief.  I have already mentioned the treatment of ulcers with strong medicine in the classification, so take it from there when needed.
Chapter Fifty-three.
On cauterizing inverted and non-inverted nails.
This affliction, a rough, painful condition, often occurs on the soles of the feet.  Cauterization can be done in two ways: either with fire or with strong water.
As for cauterization with fire, heat a hollowed-out cauterizing iron, resembling an eagle's beak, made of iron, fitting snugly around the nail on all sides and having a thin rim. Then, apply it, heated, to the nail.  Then, turn your hand with the cauterizing iron around the nail until the iron reaches the depth of the nail.
Leave it for three days to fester, then dress it with pounded wild barley mixed with salt and leave the dressing on it overnight. This will cause it to pull out from its roots.  Then treat the wound with a flesh-growing ointment until it heals, if the nails are not inverted.
It often occurs on the surface of the body, especially on the hands and feet.  Therefore, one should take a tube of copper, or iron, or of eagle's beak, and apply it to the nail or wart. Then, pour a small amount of strong water into the tube.
Hold your hand and rotate it gently, so that the rim of the tube affects the root of the nail.  The strong water will find its way to penetrate the root of the nail.  Let the patient endure the stinging of the strong water for a while.
Then leave it. The nail will pull out by its roots.  Do this to each one individually until you have dealt with all of them on the body.  Then, treat the areas after they have pulled out with flesh-growing ointments.
Chapter Forty-five.
Concerning the cauterization of the scabies.
If scabies occurs due to cold in the nerve, or a quarter fever, or something similar, then you should cauterize four or five times on the back, with a cauterization between each bead, and one on the chest. And cauterize his stomach with the olive-oil cautery, as the scabies will subside and hasten the ripening of the cold disease.
Chapter Fifty-five.
Concerning the cauterization of a boil that has occurred on the body.
Ugly boils, arising from thick, corrupt, cold matter, may appear on the body.  When they first appear, a gentle cauterization should be applied to the head of each boil with a stick whose end has been set aflame, or with the long end of a heated iron rod, or with a lens cautery.
And boils, in their initial eruption, may be cauterized in this manner.  The causing factor will not increase, and the patient will be cured, but it is necessary that this be done after the patient has been bled.
Chapter Fifty-six.
Concerning the cauterization of bleeding occurring when an artery is cut.
Bleeding often occurs from an artery that has been cut, whether in an external wound, or when incising a tumor or cauterizing a part of the body, and the like.  It is often difficult to stop the bleeding.
If this happens to someone, quickly apply your index finger to the mouth of the artery and firmly press it until the blood is contained under your finger and nothing comes out.  Then, place several small and large olive cauteries in the fire, and blow on them until they become very hot.
Then, take one, either small or large, depending on the wound and the location where the artery has been cut, and place the cautery on the same vein after quickly removing your finger.  Hold the cautery until the bleeding stops.
If the bleeding starts again when you remove your finger from the mouth of the artery and the cautery has cooled down, quickly take another cautery from the prepared ones in the fire, and continue doing this one after the other until the bleeding stops.
And be careful not to burn a nerve, which would cause another injury to the patient.  Know that if an artery is bleeding, it cannot be stopped, especially if it is a large artery, except in one of four ways: either by cauterization, as we have said, or by amputation if it has not already been severed. If it is severed, its ends will contract, and the bleeding will stop, or it may be tied with strong threads, or drugs that stop the flow and constrict the blood vessels may be applied.
However, attempting to stop it with a bandage, or by constricting with cloth, or by applying burning substances, and similar methods, will not stop it at all, except rarely.  If someone experiences this and there is no physician or medicine available, they should immediately place their index finger over the wound opening, as we have described.
He should press it firmly until the blood is contained and flows from above the wound and around the artery and finger.  The finger should be kept constantly on it with very cold water, until the blood solidifies and thickens and stops flowing.  During this time, one should consider what cauterization or medication is needed.
The second chapter.
Concerning incisions, punctures, cupping, and other surgical procedures.
In the first chapter, we have discussed every disease suitable for cauterization with fire and caustic medications, their causes and reasons, and the tools and types of cauterization, arranging this from head to foot in chapters.
I will follow the same method in this chapter, so that the student may easily attain what he seeks.
Before I begin with this, you children should know that this chapter contains even more uncertainty than the first chapter on cauterization.
For this reason, caution must be even greater, as in this chapter, the work often involves the emptying of blood, which sustains life, when opening a vein or making an incision on a tumor or puncturing an abscess or treating a wound or extracting an arrow or cutting into a stone or similar issues.  All these involve uncertainty and fear, and death often results.
I advise you against acting on doubtful matters, as various types of people with various ailments may come to you in this craft. Some may have grown weary of their illness and have accepted death.
Due to the severity of their illness and the prolonged duration of their suffering, and because the sickness shows signs of death, some may offer you their money to make you happy in the hope of health, and their illness is fatal. You should never accept help from those with such a condition.
Let your caution be greater than your desire and eagerness. Do not undertake anything of that sort without certain knowledge of the favorable outcome.  In all treatments of your patients, prioritize knowledge and warnings of what the outcome of safety will be.
For in that, you will have assistance in acquiring praise, fame, remembrance, and thanks. May God inspire you, my children, with wisdom, and may He not deprive you of correctness and success; indeed, that is in His hand. There is no god but He.
I have arranged this chapter into sections, following the previous chapter on cauterization from the head to the feet, so that what you desire from it will be easier for you, God willing.
Chapter One.
On the treatment of water that collects in the heads of children.
This ailment frequently affects children at birth if the midwife presses the child's head without gentleness. It may also arise from a hidden, unknown cause.
I have not seen this ailment in anyone other than children, and all whom I have seen of them have hastened towards death.  Therefore, I have seen fit to abandon treatment for it.  I have indeed seen a child whose head was filled with water, and the head grew larger each day. Until the child was unable to sit up due to the enlargement of his head and the moisture increased until he perished.
This moisture may either collect between the skin and bone, or beneath the bone, on the periosteum.  If the moisture is between the skin and bone, and the swelling is small, a single horizontal incision should be made in the center of the head, about two fingers long, to allow the moisture to drain.  This is the method of incision.
If the moisture is greater and the swelling larger, make two intersecting incisions, in this fashion.
If the moisture is beneath the bone, and the sign is that you see the sutures of the head open on every side, and the water sinks inward when you press it with your hand (this should not be hidden from you), then you should make three incisions in the center of the head, in this manner.
After the incision, all the moisture will drain out.  Then bind the incisions with linen and bandages.  Then cover from above with poultices and oil for five days.  Then loosen the bandages and treat the wound with ointments and plasters.  Do not overly constrict the head.  Give the patient a diet of all dry foods with little moisture.
Until the limb strengthens and heals. Another method of incision is to look where the swelling bone and the gathering of water are visible, because it may be more in the back of the head, or the front, or on the right or left.
You should make the incision where the swelling and the accumulation of water are apparent.  You should make the incision as best you can, taking care not to cut an artery and cause bleeding, because the patient will die from that bleeding while the moisture is discharged.
Chapter Two.
Cutting the two arteries behind the ears, known as the *hassisin*.
If someone suffers a severe affliction of the eyes or chest, and medication is ineffective, then the best treatment is to cut these two arteries.
If you wish to cut them, you should shave the patient's head with a razor, then rub the area with a rough cloth to expose the arteries.  Then, you should tighten the patient's neck with the help of their clothing.  Then, look for where the pulse beats, and their locations - the two sunken areas behind the ears.  They are rarely hidden, except in some people.
Then, mark them with ink, and then cut them with a dissecting scalpel, cutting to the bone.  This should be done across the width of the head.  If desired, insert the scalpel below the artery and sever it upward with the cut, and the length of the cut should be approximately two clenched fingers.
When the artery is cut, the blood will emerge with a pulsating flow, surging forward and continuously.  If the artery is not visible to the touch, then it is necessary to measure from the ear approximately three fingers' width.  Then, mark with ink and cut to the bone.  What is necessary...
About six ounces of blood will be released immediately on average, and perhaps more or less, depending on the strength of the patient and the fullness of their arteries.  Then, examine the wound. If any part of the peritoneum remains on the bone, cut it.
So that a hot swelling does not occur, then bind the wound with a linen thread, and treat it with ointments until it heals.
Chapter Three.
The arteries in the temples.
If a person experiences chronic headaches, or acute attacks from sharp humidity and heat in the muscles of the temples, or severe chronic headaches, and other similar conditions, and treatment using various medical methods has been unsuccessful, then we have tried in these cases, cauterizing or puncturing the arteries of the temples, as described.
The method for puncturing them is to shave the patient's hair on the temples, and locate the visible artery in the temple; its pulse will make it clear, and it is rarely hidden, except perhaps in some individuals.
Or, if it is obscured by intense cold, the patient should tighten their neck with their clothing, then you should rub the area with a cloth or apply warm water until the artery becomes clearly visible. Then take the lancet, which has this image. Then gently slice the skin with the lancet until you reach the artery, then insert a forceps and pull it upwards until you draw it out from the skin and free it from the membranes beneath it on all sides. If the artery is thin, twist it with the forceps' tip. Then cut off a portion equal to the distance between its ends, so that it constricts and no bleeding occurs. If it is not severed and cut, the blood will not subside at all. Then drain the blood from six to three ounces.
If the artery is large, it should be tied in two places with a strong doubled thread, whether of silk or bowstrings, so that pus does not quickly form before the wound heals and cause bleeding.
Then cut the excess between the ligatures; do this at once or after some time. If you wish, cauterize it to the bone with a small knife cautery, so that its ends are severed, and this will achieve the same result, or even better, as we have said.
However, if the patient has a fever or is feverish, because cauterization helps to consume moisture, it is more beneficial.  After removing the arteries, the wound should be packed with old cotton and firm dressings applied. After healing, treat with dry medications that promote flesh growth, and use compresses until healed, God willing.
If bleeding from the artery occurs during your procedure, immediately cut it off either by cauterization or by filling the area with alum and applying pressure to your hand until the blood stops. If none of these are available, use your finger to stop the blood.
Apply very cold water to the area until the inflammation subsides and the wound is properly closed.  A less drastic and easier method than removing the artery is to cauterize the vein with this double-bladed cautery after marking the two areas with ink.
Apply it very hot until it reaches the bone, and sever the vein in two places so that the ends are separated.  It will not heal with this cauterization. This is a picture of the cautery.
The two blades are sharp, resembling small daggers, but much less sharp than a knife.  If they were as sharp as a knife, the cold would reach them quickly, and the flesh would not separate quickly.  If they have some thickness, the heat of the fire will stick to them.
This quickly cuts the flesh, and is the best, easiest, and simplest method.  The distance between the blades should be the thickness of a finger.
Fourth Chapter.
For the treatment of persistent, hot, weeping of the eyes.
If the tears are constant and flow from the veins on the skull's exterior, and no medical treatment with drugs is effective, and you see the patient's face has turned red. He feels a tingling sensation in his forehead, like the crawling of ants, and his eyes are sunken and moist, with their edges eroded and eyelids adhering from the intensity of the tears.  Early treatments for this required this procedure, called the "sword treatment."  It involves ordering the patient to shave the hair from their forehead.
Then, three parallel incisions are made in the forehead, extending the length of the forehead. Each incision is about two fingers long, parallel to the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, the second slightly offset from the movement of the temporal muscle, and the third on the opposite side.
And be careful not to sever the arteries on either side, keeping your hand away from contact with the jawbones.  After each incision, there should be a gap of about three fingers pressed together. Have with you prepared sponges or many dry cloths to absorb the blood.
Then insert the sharp, double-ended scalpel, which has this shape/appearance. From the incision next to the temple to the middle incision, scrape away all the skin between the incisions, along with the periosteum on the bone.  Then do the same from the middle incision to the third incision. Then remove the scalpel. And insert into the first incision another instrument called a one-sided, sharp, and one-sided, smooth, non-sharp scalpel. This is its image/shape.
Place the sharp side of the instrument upward, towards the flesh adhering to the skin, and the smooth side towards the bone. Push it until it reaches the middle incision, cutting all the vessels descending from the head to the eyes, without reaching the surface of the skin.
Then do the same from the middle incision to the other incision. After a moderate amount of blood has flowed, compress the areas of clotted blood. Then insert a cotton wick into each incision, and place a dressing moistened with a mixture of broth, oil, or vinegar and oil on top of it.
So that a hot swelling does not occur. On the third day, the ligaments are resolved. Use copious rinsing with lukewarm water. Then treat it with a poultice of basil and rose oil, and other remedies used for wounds, until it is healed, God willing.
Chapter Five.
On treating tears and discharges to the eyes from within the head.
When someone has had many severe, persistent discharges, and the eyes appear sunken and small, with impaired vision and ulcerated eyelids, with eyelashes falling out. And there will be a severe, painful ache in the depth of the head and repeated sneezing. Know from these symptoms that these substances and discharges arise from numerous deep veins. The best treatment for this is to have the afflicted person shave their forehead and then make a single incision in the middle of the forehead.
And there will be a severe, painful ache in the depth of the head and repeated sneezing. Know from these symptoms that these substances and discharges arise from numerous deep veins. The best treatment for this is to have the afflicted person shave their forehead and then make a single incision in the middle of the forehead. or slightly raise it transversely and begin the incision from the left temple to the right temple.  The incision should be to the bone. Keep your hand away from the chewing muscles of the temples, until the bone is exposed and all the blood is absorbed by a sponge.
Then separate the edges of the incision with used cotton or linen thread, and tie it from above with sutures.  Soak the sutures with medicine and oil to prevent inflammation.  Once you have resolved it and see that the inflammation is gone, you should rub the bone until the flesh begins to grow on it.
Then treat it with the dried preparation that promotes flesh growth, such as taking two parts of wheat flour and four parts of calphonium, and preparing a salve from them.  This salve is used for the growth of flesh in wounds of this type.
Chapter Six.
On treating what falls into the ear.
Everything that falls into the ear is one of four types: either a metallic stone or a semi-metallic stone such as iron or glass; or a plant seed, such as chickpeas, nuts, and the like; or a liquid substance, such as water and vinegar, and the like.
Or, if it is an animal, when a pebble or a similar object that does not proliferate in the ear falls in, expose the ear to the sun.  If you see the pebble, apply a drop of violet or cypress oil to it, then attempt to remove it by moving the head or inducing a sneeze with a blunt instrument. And close the nostrils when a sneeze comes, after placing a bandage of linen or wool around the ear and extending the ear upward.  This treatment often removes the object. If it does not come out, then try to remove it with a gentle, soft material, which is depicted like this.
If it comes out with the soft material, otherwise try to remove it with a gentle, slightly curved blunt probe. If it does not come out with that, then make a brass tube and insert the end of the tube into the ear canal.
Seal the area around the tube with softened pitch, mixed with oil, so that air has no passage other than the tube, then pull it out with a forceful movement. Often the object is removed this way. If it does not come out by the methods described, then take a small piece of mastic or a similar gum used to catch birds, and place it at the tip of the probe.
After wrapping it tightly with cotton, gently insert it into the ear canal after the ear has dried from moisture. If the object does not come out using all the methods described, promptly perform an incision before a hot swelling or spasm occurs.
The method of incision is to first bleed the patient at the temples, letting out blood commensurate with their strength. Then, have the patient sit before you, tilting their ear upward, and make a small incision at the base of the ear near the cartilage, in the lowest part.
Make the incision crescent-shaped until you reach the stone. Then remove it with any available instruments. Immediately suture the incision and treat it until it heals.
If the object fallen into the ear is one of the kinds of seeds that swell and become inflamed, attempt to remove it using the methods mentioned. If that does not work, then take a thin, delicate probe, shaped like this.
Attempt to cut that type of fallen ear seed with it, but only do so if you are certain that the seed has become moistened by the ear's vapor, until it breaks into many small pieces. Then remove it with a blunt hook, a gentle swab, or suction.
As we mentioned, it is easier to remove it.  Concerning water in the ear, the patient should first use a censer to induce sneezing.  The patient should lie down on the affected ear, and fill the ear with some old cotton. If it comes out with this method.
Otherwise, take several smooth, thin stones, warm them slightly by fire, and have the patient insert one into the ear hole. The patient should then place one foot on that side and strike another stone against the stone in the ear.
He should continue doing this with stone after stone until all the water is removed. Water may also be removed by taking a piece of papyrus or feather, inserting one end into the ear, and holding the other end over a fire until most of it burns.  Then use another feather.
Do this repeatedly until all the water is removed, or draw it out with a tube as previously described for the stone.  As for removing an animal that has entered the ear, if it is small, like a flea or similar, treat it with what was mentioned in the section [or: classification].  As for...
If its body is large enough to be felt, try to remove it with lint and hooks. This is easier than anything else that might be in the ear.  As for removing worms that have developed in the ear, if you treated it with what we described in the section on [the treatment of] purulent swellings [or: the classification of purulent swellings] and your treatment was unsuccessful.
You should look into the ear in the sun. If you see any worms, remove them with lint or delicate hooks. If you see nothing, take a tube, this is its shape.
Narrow at the bottom, wide at the top. Insert the narrow end into the ear as far as the patient can tolerate, then suck forcefully.  Do this repeatedly until all the worms are expelled. If this does not work, seal around the tube with wax, as I mentioned to you regarding the stone.
If it is not expelled by what we have described, use the remedies for purulent swellings, which the ancients used to kill worms.  You will find this in the section on purulent swellings.  You can use this instrument to apply ointments and medications to the ear.  This is its shape:.
As you see, you make it from silver or brass, narrow at the bottom with a small hole, wide at the top. If you wish, the tube within the tube can be of solid brass. If you wish, take a reed and wrap cotton tightly around one end.
Then place the ointment or juice, or whatever medicine you want, in the tube that is in the ear. Then insert the reed with the cotton from above and squeeze with your hand moderately until the ointment is driven into the inner ear and is felt.
Administer it to the patient internally.  Let whatever you pour into the ear be warmed slightly over the fire. Be careful that the thing you instill is not excessively cold or excessively hot, as the ear cannot tolerate this.
Chapter Seven.
On treating the obstruction of the ear.
Some children may be born with their ear canals unperforated.
Some people may also experience an obstruction in the ear canal due to a wound or a growth. This obstruction may be deep within the ear canal, beyond the scope of sight.
It may also present slightly outside the canal, and be visible.  Obstructions deep within the ear, unseen, are often difficult to cure. Those visible should be placed in the sun. And examine it.  If you see the obstruction outwardly, open it with a gentle scalpel.  This would be its appearance.
Its tip will have a small, somewhat limited projection, while the rest of the scalpel will be smooth on both sides so as not to injure the ear. If the obstruction is due to a growth in the ear canal, grasp it with a delicate hook and cut it away gently until all of the growth is removed.
If the obstruction is deep within the ear, take a smooth, gentle probe, warm it slightly in the fire, then insert it into the ear canal.  If you feel the obstruction, along with the patient's perceived difficulty hearing, then gently remove it.
Be careful not to injure the ear drum. Then, insert a wick into the ear, fitting the size of the perforation.  Soak it in Egyptian ointment.  Do this for several days until you are sure the wound has closed. Or, take a wick, soak it in water, and sprinkle powdered za'farán on it.
Use it. If you see the ear swollen with a hot swelling, you should remove the wick and replace it with another wick that has been soaked in qīrūtī made with rose oil, so as to relieve the hot swelling. Then treat it until it heals.
If bleeding occurs, soak a sponge or cloth in cold water and apply it to the ear. Use the other treatments for stopping bleeding mentioned in the chapter on hemorrhages.
Chapter Eight.
On the treatment of warts that appear on the eyelids.
These warts that appear on the eyelids may be moist or dry.  They should be grasped with a forceps or a hook and cut off at their roots with a scalpel, then powdered alum should be applied to the area.
If blood flows from it, cauterize it with a gentle, lentil-shaped cautery iron.  Cauterizing is better after cutting.  It often returns if any part of the roots remain. If cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and will not return.
Chapter Nine.
On the treatment of cold affecting the eyelids.
A condition resembling cold in its severity and hardness often appears on the eyelids.
Therefore, it is called "cold," which is a gathering of thick moisture in the upper and lower eyelid.  If the cold is prominent on the surface of the eyelid and moves in every direction, the treatment is easy.  Make a transverse incision.
Then carefully peel it away from every side until it is released.  Then suspend it with a hook and cut it. If you cannot cut it without penetrating the eyelid, that will not harm the patient.  If the incision is large, bring the edges together with sutures and treat it until it heals.
If it is small, there is no need to worry, for the ointment will heal and unite it. If the cold is inclined towards the inside surface of the eyelid, turn the eyelid inside out, and suspend the cold with a hook, without needing an incision, and remove it from all sides.
If the eyelid is penetrated by the incision, this will not harm anything. Then wash the eye with salt water after the cold is cut, and treat the area with something that will unite it until the patient is healed.
Chapter Ten.
On the treatment of the hordeolum that appears on the eyelid.
The hordeolum is a swelling that occurs in the layers of the upper eyelid.  It appears most often in children. It weighs down their eyes and causes them colds at night, and they cannot look at sunlight. Because tears come quickly to them.
Therefore, you see them leaning on their faces, and sleeping on them.  Their eyelids, beneath the eyebrows, are wet, swollen and puffed. The hordeolum is clearly visible.  If you press the area with your fingers, you feel the hordeolum between them.  The procedure is that the afflicted person should place their head in your lap.
Then, take a linen cloth, and make a wick from it, shaping a circle around the hordeolum on all sides.  Place it over the hordeolum and press with your fingers from all sides to collect the hordeolum in the center of the circle.
Then, with a lancet, make a transverse incision in the center of that wetness.  The incision should not be larger than that of a venesection incision.  As for depth, it is necessary to cut through the entire skin until reaching the hordeolum.
In most cases, the hordeolum will protrude from the incision as a white piece of fat.  Pull it out with a cloth wrapped around your fingers, moving your hand back and forth until it heals. Be careful not to enlarge the incision, lest the lancet damage the eye.
If the hordeolum does not appear upon the initial incision, gently increase the incision slightly until the hordeolum protrudes. Then remove it as described.  Dip a cloth in vinegar and water, apply it to the area and secure it with a bandage. Some people crush salt.
Place it in the incision to dissolve any remaining moisture.  Then treat it until it heals. If a hot swelling occurs at the site, treat it with cooling compresses until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
On the types of eye dressings.
If extra eyelashes grow on the eyelid, outside the natural direction beneath the normal eyelashes, and grow thick, they harm the eye and cause various ailments, such as constant tearing, eyelid relaxation, and white discoloration and thickening, which can even lead to the eye being impaired.
Eye dressings are of four types: either by cauterization with fire, or with a sharp medicine, as previously mentioned in the chapter on cauterization, or the dressing may be by cutting and stitching, or with a splint, as I will describe.
You should place the patient's head in your lap, then turn the eyelid with your left hand. If it turns, then insert a needle with thread underneath the eyelid, and the needle with thread pierces through from above, near the hair itself.  Pull the thread upwards on the eyelid.
Turn it with a tool, then cut the inner side of the eyelid without the excess hair using a lancet from the larger incision to the smaller one.  Then, thread the suture and place a small pad of cotton or cloth underneath the eyelid.  Then, bandage the eyelid with a compress.
Like the shape of an asa leaf, except that the shape should be according to the amount of eyelid lifting required, as it may differ between people. Some may need a portion of the eyelid cut according to the amount of eyelid relaxation.
Some need a smaller cut, all depending on the relaxation of the eyelid. Then, make the incision with a lancet along the two lines that you marked, starting from the larger incision to the smaller one. Each incision should be near the natural hair, about the thickness of the tool.
Then, insert the hook into one of the corners of the skin, then peel it all off. Then, stitch the two edges together with a needle and thin wool thread, and clean away the blood.  Apply any adhesive material to the remaining threads on the eyebrows.
You may choose to do this, or not; then the stitches and threads remain for about three or four days, and then treat it. If you prefer, leave the wound without stitches and treat it with something that dries and contracts, as the eyelid will lift when the wound is closed. Stitching is better.
This method of lifting, mentioned by the ancients, is somewhat troublesome for the patient, but is good work and without danger.  Another method of lifting also exists, which is to shape the eyelid like an asa leaf, as we have described. Then, lift the eyelid with three hooks, either separated or grouped in this manner.
Then cut away the excess eyelid with a small pair of scissors, in this manner, making even cuts.
If you cannot hold the hooks in place, or if it is not successful, take a needle with thread, insert it into the center of the shape. Insert another thread near the larger incision, and a third near the smaller incision.  Hold the threads with your fingers evenly, then raise your hand in a measured manner.
Raise the entire marked skin as we described, then suture the edges of the wound and treat it until it heals.  If a hot swelling appears during your cutting or incision, subdue that swelling with *qirrooti* or similar soothing ointments.
The lower eyelid may also have its lashes turn inward.  In this case, use the cutting, suturing, and cauterization with fire and sharp medicine we previously described.  Cauterization with fire or sharp medicine is more gentle and less severe for the patient than cutting and suturing, and is preferable in these cases.
Cauterization with reeds is done in this manner:  Turn over the eyelid, and make the incision from the inside as described.  Then make two thin reeds or pieces of wood, their length equal to the length of the eyelid, and their width less than the width of a scalpel.
Nibble at the ends of each reed from both sides where the threads are held, then gently bring them together over the remaining eyelid.  Tightly tie the reeds from both sides. Leave it for several days, then the tightened skin will die, darken, and decay.
Until it falls away on its own. If it is slow to fall away, nibble it with a pair of shears, then treat it until it heals. When it is rejoined, the eyelid will be raised, and the lashes will not prick the eye. This is the shape and size of the two reeds, made in precisely these dimensions of length and width.
Chapter Twelve.
Removing irritating hairs from the eye with a needle.
If there is one or two hairs, the procedure is to take a thin needle, thread it with a smooth, fine silk thread, tie the ends together with a very gentle knot, and let the thread be about a span long.
Then, thread another, even finer thread, shorter than the first, onto the needle, tying its ends if desired.  The patient should place their head on your lap, near the light, so the work is clear, as it is delicate work.  Then, using the needle and thread, insert it into the root of the excess hair.
And gently pierce the eyelid until the hair is pulled out from above the natural eyelashes, then pull the thread out above the needle and remove the hair(s) – one, two, or three, but no more – from the needle.
Then, pull your hand so that the hair comes out along with the needle through the eyelid, with the natural eyelashes. If pulling the needle doesn't remove the hair, pull the needle downward with the thread you attached, so that the needle comes out from the upper hole.
Then, put the hair back in and pull both. Do this until the hair is raised at the upper hole, and you can see it clearly.  Then, remove the needle and thread, and close the eye; leave it closed for two or three days until the hair is anchored in place and flesh grows over it.
If the hair is short, either add a longer hair from the natural eyelashes so it is raised with it, or leave it until it grows longer, and then raise it.
Chapter Thirteen.
On treating the stye that occurs in the upper eyelid of the eye.
This stye, called a 'rabbit's eye', may be either natural or accidental.  An accidental stye results from an inflammation of a wound, a cut, a burn, or similar causes.
The treatment involves incising the inflammation, separating its edges, and placing a thread of linen between them, tying it off until it heals.  Things that dry and constrict should not be used in its treatment.
If you do this, the stye will return worse.  Instead, use things that loosen it up, such as fenugreek,  soaked in water with a crushed leek, flaxseed, and diachylon ointment mixed with some oils, and apply it to the thread and use it as a treatment.
The key to its treatment is to do everything possible to prevent it from sticking together in its original form.  This method we have described is just one way to improve the stye, not to restore the area to its previous condition exactly.
Chapter Fourteen.
On treating the stye in the lower eyelid.
This stye, which arises from the lower part, is truly a stye and is natural.  An accidental stye arises from a wound, cut, burn, or similar cause.
The method is to take a needle with a doubled thread, pierce the flesh, and pass it from the inner corner to the outer corner, so that the thread is on both ends of the flesh.  Then, pull the flesh upwards with the needle, and cut it with a broad scalpel.
If the eyelid returns to its proper shape, then fine.  Otherwise, take a spatula and place it on the wound; use it to turn the eyelid, and cut two incisions on the inside of the eyelid, so that the ends of the incisions meet at the angles of the incision already made, forming an angle. So that when joined, its shape will be similar to this shape.
Then, remove the flesh, as much as the sharp side is below the eyelid.  Then, gather the separated parts with two stitches, sewing them with wool thread.  Then treat it as previously mentioned with relaxing and softening remedies until it heals.
If the ulcer is from a cut, suture, or cauterization, it is necessary to make a small incision, also without affecting the eyelashes, as mentioned before.  Then separate the lips with softening as described.
In short, for treating an ulcer, whether from above or below, perform the procedure according to the ulcer's appearance.  The appearance of ulcers varies considerably.  The skilled practitioner devises a method in the best possible way.
So that the shape returns to its natural form or approaches it; the disease itself indicates what treatment and instruments are suitable in most cases.
Chapter Fifteen.
On the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or cornea.
This adhesion may occur in many people, causing the upper eyelid to adhere to the white of the eye, hindering its free movement and its natural function.  A pointed instrument should be inserted under the eyelid.
And lift it upwards or stretch it with a hook, then cut the adhesion with a gentle scalpel, not sharp like other scalpels, but rather blunt, so as not to unsettle the patient during the procedure, and for fear the scalpel may slip and injure the eye with its sharpness; your incision should be as if you are peeling away the adhesion.
And wait until the eyelid returns to its normal position and the entire adhesion heals.  Then, instill salt water into the eye to wash it or instill a solution of  zinc sulfate in water, then separate the eyelid from the eye using a linen wick.
Place moistened egg white on top of the eye.  After three days, use oiled cloths until healed.
Chapter Sixteen.
In cutting the nail and deep flesh tumors.
The nail is of two types: either nervous, resembling a thin, hard peritoneum, or non-nervous, resembling a hard, white moisture that, when touched by iron or attempted to be grasped with a hook, breaks apart.
A hook will not attach to either type.  Both types, starting from the largest part, gradually spread until they cover the eye and block the light and movement of the eye.  The method for cutting them is for the patient to place their head on a rock.
Then, the patient opens their eyes, and you raise the eyelid with your hand.  Then, you grasp the nail with a slightly bent hook, pulling it upwards.  Then, take a needle, thread a horsehair or cowhair and strong thread through it, bend the tip of the needle slightly, and insert it into the center of the nail.
Pierce it with the needle, tie the nail to the thread, pull it upwards, and peel the side of the nail closest to the pupil with the hair, as if spreading it to the end.  Then, cut the remaining part at the root of the growth with a gentle scalpel or small scissors, and leave the natural flesh of the growth.
So that constant tears do not result from cutting it.  The nail may be pulled with the hook alone, or with the thread, as we said, then the smooth side is peeled with a scalpel, which appears in this image:.
And be careful not to touch the cornea with the scalpel, lest it cause a tear in the pupil. Then, after cutting, put some powdered salt or black antimony in the eye and leave it until the next day, then treat it appropriately until healed.
If the nail is not sensitive and you cannot insert a needle or secure a hook, this type of nail is often treated with medications, and heals as described in the section. If you wish to cut it, open the patient's eye.
Take a gentle, smooth, blunt scalpel, like this image:. And carefully remove the nail from above with it.  If you see that it is crumbling and only affected by the scraping, put some antimony, red antimony, or powdered salt from your container into the eye, and compress the eye until the next day.
Then, repeat the procedure until it is all gone. However, if a hot boil appears in the eye, leave it alone and treat the boil until it heals, then resume scraping until it is healed.  And regarding the inflamed flesh...
If the growth is causing the eye excessive pain, hook it with a forceps and cut off a portion, but don't overdo it lest you cause tears to flow. Then, apply red or black antimony to the affected area until it heals.
Chapter Seventeen.
On the excision of warts and excess flesh growing in the eye.
In some people's eyes, red, overlapping flesh may grow until it covers the pupil or nearly covers, or spills over the eyelids, and sometimes the eyelids turn outward, resembling a rose.
The procedure involves placing the patient's head on your lap, then opening their eyes, and using appropriate forceps to grasp all the red flesh.
Or grasp it with a curette or a spatula, then cut the flesh one piece at a time until it is all removed, preserving the eye from injury during the procedure.  Your cut should be performed using the scalpel described in the chapter on removing ingrown nails, or with small scissors. As you would use for grasping splinters, as their image appears in the following chapter.
If your cutting is complete and all the growths are gone, fill the eye with pounded salt, or instill red, or similar caustic medicines into it. Apply a bandage of egg white to the outside of the eye to prevent inflammation.
If any growths remain, if bleeding is excessive, and you fear inflammation, leave the eye alone and treat it with something to reduce inflammation, then return to the procedure until healing.  Do the same with any excess flesh that appears in the eye of this type.
However, you should avoid treating any weak eye through natural means or by surgical intervention with iron. Do not insert your hand into any of these procedures until you see that the organ is capable of withstanding the procedure.
Chapter Eighteen.
In the excision of things from the eye.
The vessels are red veins that weave across the eye, preventing the sight from functioning and weakening the eye over time.  Therefore, you must first look; if the eye containing the vessels is strong, and there is no other illness except the vessels.
Then, the excision is to instruct the patient to place their head on your lap.  Then, lift those veins with one or two fine needles, depending on your skill, and the needles should be delicately curved in this manner:.
Or two needles joined in one body, in this manner:.
Then, gently excise those veins with a fine pair of scissors, and wipe the blood occasionally until you see that the eye is clear of those veins and the blood has drained.  Be careful not to injure the eye with the tips of the scissors.  Let your work be in the middle of the day in the presence of the sun.
Be very thorough in your work so that you do not cut anything other than those vessels. Once you are finished, instill green or red acacia into the eye, to consume any remaining vessels with its sharpness. If you are unable to excise all of them at that time, then bandage the eye to relieve eye pain.
Leave it for days until the pain subsides and the inflammation disappears. Then repeat the procedure in exactly the same way until it heals.  This is the image of the scissors used to remove them.
Leave it [and let the head of the patient be in the student's lap and another student wipe away the tears and examine the front and back of the eye.  And you]  The primary method for work is to open the eye with fixed or triangular instruments that a student holds.
Look closely.  The patient's head should be in the student's lap, and another student should wipe away the tears and examine the front and back of the eye. You have the option of starting the excision from the top or bottom of the eye.  It is best to first dry the two edges on the upper side, one at the end of the eyebrow and the other at the beginning.
Hold it between the middle and index fingers.  Then, hook instruments from below opposite them.  Then, hold it between the ring and little fingers, and gently lift your hand until you see all of the vessels rise.  Cut them from the smallest section, if desired, with scissors, or with a gentle scalpel.
Insert the needle into the vessels, and examine all the vessels until they rise above the conjunctiva. Do not cut or dry, for tears and blood will smear (mix) with it.
Then cut with scissors what extends beyond the eyebrow, from above and below, so that nothing remains protruding except for the cornea. If you can, lift it away from the cornea with the needle; this is better, and it may be possible in some cases.
If you cut it, it will form a crust for a few days, which will relieve the pain and prevent the inflammation.  Then, resume the procedure.
Chapter Nineteen.
Regarding returning the quill to the nose.
Physicians call the quill a naṣūr. If, having treated it with cauterization or a caustic, burning medicine as described previously, it does not heal, then the only solution is to lance the swelling when ripe and extract all the moisture or pus within it.
Until the bone is exposed.  If the bone is exposed and you see corruption or blackness in it, scrape it with a tool of this form:
The scraper, called "al-khishna al-ras," is made of Indian iron, and its head is round like a seed. It has a fine, engraved pattern like a file or a scraper.  Place it on the diseased area of the bone and rotate it between your fingers, gently squeezing your hand, until you know that the corruption has been scraped off.
Do this several times, then bind the area with drying, astringent remedies.  If the area is sealed, flesh grows, the discharge stops, and forty days pass without recurrence, festering, or any other incident.
Know then that it has healed, or else there is no remedy but to return the naṣūr to the nasal opening in this manner:  Expose the bone again with iron or a caustic.  Once the bone is exposed, take a forceps of this form:
The end of the iron tool is triangular, and its shaft is a cone narrowing towards the end, as you see. Place it on the bone itself, near the nares, keeping your hand a little away from the eye. Then rotate the forceps until the bone is penetrated and the patient feels it.
By feeling air escaping from it when the patient holds their hand over their nose.  At that point, bind the area with drying, astringent remedies, as I have told you. If the flesh grows and the area hardens, then the matter that had been protruding externally will return to the nose, which will be easier for the patient, God willing.
Chapter Twenty.
On returning the protrusion of the eye.
If the entire eye protrudes, and there is no defect or loss of vision, the patient should be eased, then confined in a bandage, then a cupping vessel placed on the back of the neck without conditions and cupped gently.
Then place on the eye a poultice made from acacia, aloes, frankincense, and henna.  Then the eye is bound from above on the bandage with many bandages. The method for making the bandages is to take many soft cloths and fold each into four layers, making many of them.
Then place them one over another as much as needed for the eye, and bind them tightly with a firm cloth.  Replace the cupping vessel and cup again without conditions.  Then remove the cupping vessel and leave the eye bandaged for a day and a night.
If the bandage loosens during this time, it should be tightened.  Then remove the bandage after a day and a night, as we have said. If you see the eye has returned, then otherwise, repeat the bandage, the bandages, the tightening, and the cupping until it returns.
Chapter Twenty-one.
In cutting the sinew.
If a rupture occurs in the conjunctiva, then it swells and protrudes outside the eyelids like a sinew, disfiguring the person's appearance, and you wish to cut it, then the procedure is as I describe.
Insert a needle into the base of the sinew from bottom to top, then insert another needle with a double thread from the inside out, piercing it, leaving the first needle in place.
Then cut the bend in the double thread, tie part of the sinew around the needle with it and pull tight. Then remove the needle and apply a cloth moistened with egg white to the eye, leaving it until the threads and sinew fall out. Then treat the eye with a strengthening remedy.
Until the wound heals.  The sinew may be tightened from every side with a bandage until the eye swells more tightly.  Then it is lanced with a thin scalpel that reaches the back of the eye, causing the white moisture to flow out and the eye to droop and wither in its position. Then it is tightened until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-two.
On the treatment of abscesses.
This ailment, called an abscess, is a collection in the eye resembling descending water, but without it.  The method of treatment is that the patient sits upright on a chair, and then you grasp their head from both sides with your hands.
And you move it until you see the collection descend downward with your eyes. Then you stabilize it, and it does not move, and the light is released. The patient sees things as he used to. If it does not descend, we know that it is water. If the collection does not descend as described,
Otherwise, have the patient sit before you. Then take a thin scalpel and make a small incision just above the cornea where the adhesion meets the cornea at the limbus, until the material comes out.
If it comes out, instill warm water into the eye, which has been mixed with honey, or water in which you boiled licorice root and honey. Then treat with other remedies until it is healed.
The twenty-third chapter.
We have already described the types of water in the classification, and which are suitable for instillation, with detailed and explained words. Take that from there for accuracy.  Then, it is necessary that the patient sit before you, squatting facing the light near the sun.
Tie and firmly hold his healthy eye, then lift the eyelid with your left hand if the eye containing the water is the left eye, or with your right hand if it is the right eye.
Then, place the tip of the cup near the rim, with a thickness of a date-stone, onto the same white part of the eye, from the side of the smaller aperture. Then, push the cup forcefully while rotating it with your hand until it penetrates the white of the eye and you feel the cup has reached an empty space.
The cup should go to a depth equal to the distance from the child to the edge of the iris, which is the rim of the eye.  The copper will be seen by the viewer as if the eye were seeing it, due to the clarity of the cornea.  Then, move the cup upwards to the spot where the water is.
Then press it downwards repeatedly.  If water descends at once, the patient will see what has been opened to his vision at once, the cup being in his eye.  Then, rest a little. If the water rises, lower it again without removing the cup.
If it settles and does not rise, remove the cup gently, twisting your hand a little at a time.  Then, dissolve some clear, fine rock salt in the water and wash the inside of the eye with it.  Then, apply a moistened compress or wool to the entire outside of the eye.
Anoint it with rose water and egg white, and bandage the healthy eye.  As for us and our contemporaries, we place crushed cumin with egg white on it. If the cup will not enter the eye due to its hardness (because some people have very hard eyes),... Therefore, one should take the lancet called the *bayrid*, which has this form:
Pierce only the adhering tissue with it; do not go too deep.  Instead, lightly pierce the cup's position.  Then, introduce the cup as we described. When your work is complete, prepare a secure bed for the patient, with him lying on his back in a dark room.
He must refrain from all movement and coughing.  Provide food that softens his nature, and do not allow him to move his head to the right or left at all.  Keep the bandage on for three days. Then, remove it in that dark room and test his vision.
Show him objects, and then replace the bandage for another seven days.  You should not do this during or immediately after the cupping, but rather before the water rises quickly due to excessive looking.
If a hot swelling appears, then you should remove the eye bandage before the seventh day and treat it with something that calms the swelling. Once it has subsided, then release his vision. Place a cloth over his face, under which he trains his eyesight for several days while in the dark room. Then...
He is gradually released from the room and allowed to manage his affairs.  Know that the student cannot dispense with observing the procedure repeatedly before undertaking the action.  I have heard some Iraqis say that a cupping device is made in Iraq which draws out water.
I have not seen anyone in our country make such a thing, nor have I read about it in any of the books of the ancients.  It may be a recent invention.  Here are pictures of various types of cupping devices for your understanding.
They are made of copper, specifically, and their tip is precisely this thin, triangular shape with a sharp point.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
In the treatment of flesh growing in the nose.
Various excess growths may arise in the nose, some resembling many-legged scorpions, some cancerous and hardened, dark-colored, and some soft, non-dark-colored flesh.
For those growths that are soft, not malignant or cancerous, the patient should be seated facing the sun, their nostril opened, and a snare applied to the flesh, pulling it outward, then cutting the portion caught with a gentle, sharp scalpel on one side.
Until you know that all the flesh has been removed. If any remains that you cannot cut, detach it with one of the gentler tools, gently, so that no part remains. If blood or a hot swelling occurs.
If it is a malignant tumor, then cauterize it until the bleeding stops and all the flesh is gone.  Then, after the cutting, place in the nose vinegar and water or a drink, so that the nose opens and moisture flows into the throat.
Know then that it has healed. If the moisture does not drain properly, know that there is a new growth of flesh on the weakened bone, higher up than where the instrument reached with the cut. Then you must take a thick linen thread.
Tie many knots in it, and make each knot the size of a finger or smaller.  The patient must manipulate one end of the thread into their nose using a probe, or whatever means possible, to shape it like a button, and pull on it until it reaches the septum.
It will come out through their throat.  Children often do this sort of thing in school; it is easy for anyone who wants to do it. Then bring together the two ends of the thread, the one that came out of the mouth and the one that remained in the nose.
Then use the knots in the thread to dissect the flesh.  Continue until you know that the flesh has been separated by the knots in the thread.  Then remove the thread and apply a bandage soaked in Egyptian salve to the nose.  Do this for three days or more.
Until the salve eats away all remaining flesh.  Then, finally, a lead tube is placed in the nose for a few days until healed. If further drying treatment is needed, use that.
This is an image of the instrument used to instill ointments and medicines into the nose.
A silver or bronze vessel, shaped like a small lamp, with an open mouth and channel.
If you wish, make the tube closed like a reed and flatten the instrument's applicator.
It has a handle at its end, as you see, used to hold it when heating ointments or any other liquid substances within it.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
On warts growing on the tip of the nose.
Warts often grow on the tip of the nose, increasing and enlarging over time, until they disfigure the appearance.
Therefore, one should cut it off at its first appearance and completely remove it, then apply either cauterization or a burning medicine, which serves as a cauterization.  If it has been missed until it grows large, observe; if it is hard, stony, dark-colored, and insensitive.
Do not use iron on it, for it is a cancerous tumor.  I have often seen that cutting this tumor brings great harm to the afflicted person.  If the tumor feels soft, not dark-colored, and I see that cutting it is possible throughout its extent. Drain the afflicted area and cut it without hesitation or delay. Treat the area with something that dries and contracts until healed.
Chapter Twenty-six.
On suturing the nose, lip, and ear. If its connection is separated by a wound or similar, know that if a separation of connection occurs in one of these cartilages, rarely will treatment succeed except in some individuals.
Therefore, if one of these things occurs, examine if the wound is fresh with blood.  If so, bring the edges of the wound together with sutures, then treat it until it heals.  If the separation of connection has separated its edges, and each gap is complete, then each gap should be scraped with the thickness of its visible skin until it bleeds.
Then bring the edges together with sutures, tighten them, and sprinkle powdered syagrus and frankincense on them.  Place a plaster of palm ointment, or other adhesive ointment, over it. Leave it tightened for two or three days, then loosen it.
And change the medication, and leave it until the threads come loose on their own. Then treat it with the ointment until it heals.  The method of suturing is to bring together the separation, either with needles as described in the suturing of the abdomen, or with thread, as I have explained there.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
On removing the knots that appear on the lips.
Many people may develop small, hard tumors inside their lips, some resembling small thorns, others larger or smaller.  It is necessary to turn back the lip and cut each knot and remove it with tweezers.
And cut it from every side, then fill the area after cutting with powdered zea to stop the bleeding.  Then rinse with vinegar and salt, and treat the areas with astringents until the wounds heal.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
On cutting excess flesh in the gums.
Often excess flesh grows on the gums, which the ancients called "apholis".  It should be grasped with tweezers or a scalpel, cut at its root, and allow the flow of blood or pus to cease. Then apply powdered zea to the area.
Or one of the astringent, dried powders.  If the flesh returns after treatment, it often does so; cut it again and cauterize it, for it will not return after cauterization.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
On scraping teeth with iron.
Rough, unsightly scales may accumulate on the surfaces of the teeth, both inside and outside, and between the gums.  They may turn black, yellow, or green, leading to gum decay and discoloration of the teeth.
The patient should sit before you, head resting on your lap.  Remove the tooth or teeth showing scales and sand-like material until none remain.  Do the same for black, green, and yellow discoloration, and so forth, until cleaned.
If the first scraping removes all the impurities, then good.  Otherwise, repeat the scraping the next day, and again on the third day, until the desired result is achieved.  Know that teeth require various scrapers of different shapes and forms, according to the task at hand.
Because the scraper used for the inside of the tooth is different from the scraper used for the outside, and the one used between the teeth is different yet again.  Various shapes of scrapers should be prepared.
Chapter Thirty.
On the extraction of teeth.
One should attempt all possible treatments for toothache before resorting to extraction, for there is no cure after extraction since it is a noble substance.  Only extract a tooth if absolutely necessary.
If the patient is determined to have the tooth extracted, one must ensure that the toothache is fully confirmed. Often the patient is mistaken about the location of the pain, believing it to be in a healthy tooth and extracting it.  The pain does not go away until the diseased tooth is extracted.  We have seen this many times in the actions of barbers.
If the source of the pain is definitively located in the tooth, then one should use a strong forceps to cut around the gum on all sides.  Then gently move it back and forth, using fingers or fine forceps, until it is loose.
Then, firmly grasp the tooth with large forceps, holding the patient's head between your knees to keep them still.  Pull the tooth straight to avoid breaking it. If it does not come out, take one of those instruments and gently insert it underneath the tooth from all sides, moving it as you did before.
If the tooth is decayed or has a hole, you should fill the hole with cloth and seal it tightly with a thin, pliable piece of material, to prevent it from breaking when you pull on it with the forceps.  Carefully cut around the gum on all sides.
Use caution so as not to break the tooth, leaving fragments that will cause the patient greater harm than the original pain.  Do not imitate the careless actions of barbers, who extract teeth without using the methods we have described.
Often they inflict great harm on people, the least of which is breaking the tooth and leaving the roots, some or all of them, behind. Or, they may pull out part of the jawbone, as we have witnessed repeatedly.  After extraction, the patient should rinse the area with a drink or with vinegar and salt.  If bleeding occurs at the site, which frequently happens, then crush some alum and apply it to the area.
Otherwise, if the alum does not help, use a pair of delicate forceps.  The forceps should have long tips and a short, thick handle, so as not to bend when you grasp the tooth.  Here is a description of the large forceps.
They should be, as you see, thick in the handles, so that when grasped, they do not give way or bend. The tips should be short.  They should be made of Indian iron or tempered steel, with the tips firmly joined and fitted with teeth that interlock for a secure grip.
The tips may also be shaped like a file, making them stronger and more precise.
