Chapter Thirty-Two.
In the spreading of protruding teeth.
Teeth, if they sprout out of their natural course, mar the appearance, especially in women and slaves.  One should look closely; if a tooth has grown behind another,
If it cannot be spread or cooled, remove it. If it is adhering to another tooth, cut it off using this instrument, whose image is this:
It resembles a small beak, made of sharp-edged Indian steel.  Cut it in several days, because of the tooth's hardness and to avoid dislodging other teeth.  If it is protruding and firmly fixed, cool it with an Indian file, the image of which is this.
It should be entirely of Indian steel, with very fine engravings, like the file used for making needles.  Cool the tooth gradually over many days, gently, so as not to dislodge it and cause it to fall out.  Then, smooth it further with some other instruments.
If a tooth has broken, causing pain to the tongue when speaking, it should also be cooled until the roughness of the fracture disappears, it becomes smooth, and no longer hurts the tongue or impairs speech.
Chapter Thirty-three.
On fastening loose teeth with silver or gold thread.
If older teeth become loose and shift due to a blow or fall, and the patient cannot eat because they fear the teeth will fall out, and treatment with astringent medicines has failed.
The solution is to tie them with gold or silver thread. Gold is preferable, because silver will corrode and fester after a few days, while gold remains unchanged. The thread should be of medium thickness and thinness, just wide enough to fit between the teeth.
The method of fastening is to take the thread, insert its loop between the two sound teeth, then weave the ends of the thread through the loose teeth, one or more, until you reach the sound tooth on the other side.
Then weave back to the starting point, pulling gently and skillfully to ensure no further movement.  The thread should be tightened near the roots of the teeth to prevent slippage.  The excess ends of the thread are then cut with scissors, joined and twisted together with some material, and hidden between the sound tooth and the loose tooth.
To prevent tongue irritation, and the patient will keep it tightened for as long as needed.  If it loosens or breaks, tighten it again with another thread, and the patient can use their teeth in this way for a lifetime.  This is how the teeth are fixed, with two sound teeth and two loose teeth, as shown.
A single or two teeth that have fallen out can sometimes be repositioned and fastened as described, and remain.  However, only a skilled craftsman can accomplish this.  Sometimes a bone, such as ox bone, is carved to resemble a tooth and placed in the position of the missing tooth. And it is tightened, as we said, and remains in place.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
On cutting the ligament that lies beneath the tongue and prevents speech.
This ligament beneath the tongue may be a natural part of the human anatomy, or it may be the result of a healed wound.
The procedure is to open the patient's mouth, hold their head in your lap, and lift their tongue.  Then, cut this nervous ligament transversely until the tongue is released from its constraint.  If it is somewhat firm and knotted, as might be the case from a healed wound, then insert a probe and split it transversely until the ligament is severed and the knots are unraveled.
Beware of cutting too deeply into the flesh, as this might sever an artery and cause bleeding.  Then, after the incision, rinse the mouth with rosewater or a mixture of vinegar and cold water.  Place a linen thread under the tongue for the patient to hold in place every night.
To prevent it from re-adhering.  If bleeding occurs, apply powdered alum to the area.  If the bleeding is excessive, cauterize the area with a lentil-shaped cauterizing iron. Then treat it with other remedies until it is healed.
Chapter Thirty-five.
On removing the toad that has developed under the tongue.
A tumor resembling a small toad may develop under the tongue, hindering its normal function, and possibly growing until it fills the mouth.  The procedure is to open the patient's mouth facing the sun and examine the tumor.
If you see it dark-colored or black, hard, and the patient feels no sensation, do not attempt to treat it, for it is cancer. If it is tending towards white and moist, use forceps to pry it open and gently cut it with a scalpel, freeing it from all sides.
If blood flows excessively while working, apply powdered alum until it stops, then continue the procedure until the tumor is completely removed.  Then have the patient gargle with vinegar and salt.
Then treat it with further appropriate treatment until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-six.
On the treatment of tonsil tumors.
Any tumors growing in the throat may present glands within the throat resembling those found externally, called tonsils.  If these are treated as described in the previous sections and do not heal, ...
If the tumor is hard, dark-colored, and insensitive, do not use the knife.  If it is red and its origin thick, also avoid the knife, fearing blood loss. Instead, allow it to ripen, either by applying compresses or by spontaneous rupture.
If it is white, round, and its origin thin, then it is advisable to cut it.  Before doing so, observe whether the inflammation has subsided completely or diminished somewhat.
Then, have the patient sit facing the sun, with their head resting on your lap, and their mouth open.  Take an assistant, who will press the patient's tongue downwards with an instrument, which appears as follows:
Made of silver or copper, thin like a knife.  After pressing the tongue with it and the tumor is clearly visible, take a forceps and pierce it into the tonsil, pulling it outwards as much as possible without pulling any of the surrounding membranes.
Then, cut it with an instrument, which resembles scissors but has bent tips, each with sharply pointed ends.  Made of Indian iron or Damascene steel.
If this instrument is unavailable, cut it with a scalpel, which appears as follows:
Sharp on one side and blunt on the other. After cutting one tonsil, cut the other in the same manner.  Then, after the cut, the patient should gargle with cool water or vinegar and water.
If bleeding occurs, gargle with water boiled with pomegranate rind or rue leaves, or similar astringents, until the bleeding stops. Then treat it until healed. Other tumors may grow in the throat, besides the tonsils, and these are cut in the same way as the tonsils.
I treated a woman for a tumor that had grown inside her throat, resembling a small, hardly sensitive polyp, which nearly blocked her throat.  The woman was breathing through a narrow passageway, and it had prevented her from eating and drinking.
She was close to death if she had lived another day or two.  The tumor had grown two branches that protruded through her nostrils.  I acted quickly, impaling one with a hook and pulling it out.  A suitable piece was extracted.
Then I cut it where it emerged from the nostril, then did the same with the other nostril.  Then I opened her mouth, pressed down her tongue, and impaled the tumor with the hook.  I cut off some of it, and only a small amount of blood was released.
The woman's throat opened up, and she immediately drank water and then ate.  We repeatedly cut away at the tumor over a long period, and it kept growing back what we removed, thus extending the treatment for both of us.
I devised a method and cauterized the tumor inside her throat, which stopped its growth.  Then I left the area and do not know what God did for her after I departed.
Chapter Thirty-Seven.
On cutting a tumor of the larynx.
If a tumor, called *'unaba'* descends to the larynx and swells, being white and oblong, it is called a *'amud'*; if it is thick at the bottom and round, it is called *'unaba'*.  If treated as described in the classification and the treatment does not succeed...
If the hot tumor has subsided and is thin, it should be cut.  If it is a collected, round mass without length, and is blood-red, or dark-coloured, or black, or without sensation, it should be avoided.
There is risk to the patient.  Therefore, if it is as described, white and long, the patient should sit facing the sun, and the tongue should be pressed down with the instrument previously described, then the snare should be inserted into the *'unaba'*.
Pull it downwards and cut it with one of the two instruments mentioned in the section on cutting the tonsils.  Only the excess beyond the natural state should be cut, without more, for if you cut more, you will harm the voice and speech.
Then, after cutting, use what we described in the section on cutting the tonsils and treat it until it heals.  If the patient refuses the cutting, then use the cauterization technique without fear or caution.  The method of cauterization involves a sharp medicine, and the head of the patient should be placed on your lap.
Then, press down the patient's tongue with the instrument we mentioned, then take the sharp water mentioned in the chapter on cauterization, mix it with quicklime, not extinguished, and make it neither thick nor thin, and fill the concavity of this instrument.  This is its image/form.
The tip of the instrument, where you place the medicine, has a concavity like the concavity of a marud spoon.  Place the instrument with the medicine on the throat itself, and the patient should lie on their side so that saliva runs out of the mouth from inside the throat, so that nothing goes down the throat and hurts them.
Then, hold the instrument with the medicine and press it on the throat for about half an hour until you see it has turned black and the burning of the medicine subsides.  If you wish, take a piece of cotton, wrap it around the tip of a marud spoon, and soak the cotton in the medicine.
Insert the marud spoon with the cotton into the opening from above so that the cotton adheres to the throat.  Do this repeatedly until you achieve the desired degree of cauterization of the ulcer. Then leave it; it will wither and fall off in three or four days. If you need to repeat the medicine, do so.
After the cauterization, wipe around the ulcer with cotton soaked in clarified butter, removing any remaining medicine.  Then, rinse with cool water and treat externally with lotions, and internally with gargles, until it heals. This type of treatment is safer than cutting and avoids fear.
The throat can also be treated with methods gentler than cauterization and cutting, such as fumigation.  Take frankincense, zofah, savory, wormwood, safflower, chamomile, and similar herbs. Gather them all, or some of them, in a pot and immerse them in vinegar.
Bring it to a boil, with the pot sealed with clay.  A hole is made in the center of the cover, over which the hollow instrument is placed.  This instrument is made of silver or copper.
Insert the end with the pomegranate into the patient's mouth so that the vapor rises to the throat through the tube until the throat is healed. Then repeat several times until it withers.  Be careful not to use this treatment at the onset of the swelling.
For it often aggravates the swelling.  Instead, it should be done when the hot swelling has subsided. If you do not have this instrument, take a tube and attach a hard-boiled egg shell to its end, so as to prevent the patient's mouth from burning.
Because the egg shell prevents the heat of the vapor from burning the mouth. This is a good and safe treatment.
Chapter Thirty-eight.
On removing thorns and other foreign objects lodged in the throat.
Thorns, fish bones, or other objects often lodge in the throat.  One should remove any visible ones with the instrument, pressing the tongue with it in the light.
To see what is in the throat.  If something is hidden in the throat and cannot be seen, the patient should vomit before their food has digested in their stomach.  Perhaps the lodged object will come out with the vomit, or the patient may swallow a piece of a twig or a piece of lettuce stalk.
Or a piece of dry bread might be swallowed.  Or a piece of soft sea sponge is taken, tied to a thread, and swallowed.  If it reaches the place of the thorn, pull the thread quickly.  Repeat this several times.
Often the thorn or bone adheres to it and comes out.  If it does not come out by the methods described, use a lead instrument like this...
It is slightly thicker than a stylus.  At the tip, there is a curve. The patient, holding their head up, gently inserts it into their throat, avoiding touching their larynx to prevent coughing, and pushing the bone or thorn out.  Or the physician inserts it with their hand.
Having the patient insert it is better, as they know the location of the lodged object.  They push it downward or pull with the instrument upward, as best they can until it comes out.
Chapter Thirty-nine.
Removing a leech lodged in the throat.
If treating the leech with the medications mentioned in the classifications did not work, then examine the patient's throat in the light of the sun after pressing their tongue down with the instrument that I described to you.
If you see the leech, pull it out with a small hook or a gentle, secure forceps. If that is not possible, take a hollow tube, insert it into the patient's throat near the leech, and then insert a heated iron into the tube.
Do this several times. The patient should abstain from drinking water all day, then take a bowl filled with cool water, open their mouth over it, and gargle without swallowing a drop.  Move the water back and forth with their hand periodically.
If the leech feels the water, it will fall off. If it does not come out with the method described, fumigate the throat with urine and hellebore using the instrument described for fumigation of the larynx. Do this several times; this will cause it to fall off.
The method for fumigation is to take a vessel containing embers heated by fire, covered with a lid having a hole in the center.  Insert the end of the instrument into the hole. Place the incense in the vessel. The patient places their mouth on the end of the tube and closes their mouth tightly to ensure the incense reaches their throat.
The leech will fall off at this point. If it does not, the fumigation is repeated several times. The patient must endure thirst and eat salty food and garlic, but not drink any water.  The leech must come out with this procedure. This is an illustration of the instrument used to pull the leech from the throat if it is seen.
It resembles pliers, as you see, except for this curve that enters the throat. Its ends are like a bird's beak, with the roughness of an emery board.  If it bites something, it does not let go.
Another instrument is used to restrain the tongue.  It is a wide ring of iron, the size of the mouth, positioned above the tongue. It is inserted into the mouth, lying on its side, keeping the mouth open against the patient's will.  The patient cannot remove it until it is removed.
Description of the instrument that opens the mouth in this exact manner.
Chapter Forty.
It discusses the lancing and incision of tumors.
Tumors are of many varied types, as will be described one by one in this book.  They differ in lancing and incision in two ways: one is the nature of the tumor itself and the humors it contains.
The second type [of difference] is from the locations in the body where they occur, because a tumor arising in the head is different from one arising in the buttocks, and a tumor in a fleshy area differs from one in a joint; each has its own operative procedure.
Some tumors should not be lanced until the pus has matured and is fully formed; others, such as those close to joints, should be lanced while still unripe and not fully matured.
Because if a tumor occurs near a joint and spreads until it affects the surrounding tissues, it may damage the ligaments or nerves of that joint, causing weakness or paralysis of that limb, or the tumor may be near a vital organ.
Because if you delay lancing it until it matures, you will damage the vital organ, or if it is near the buttocks, you should lance it while unripe.  You should lance a tumor that is unripe and near the buttocks to prevent the infection from spreading deep into the rectum and causing a fistula.
The condition may become incurable.  You should know when to lance fully matured tumors, which is when the tumor pain subsides, the fever disappears, redness and pulsation diminish, the tumor head softens, and other symptoms subside.
The incision should be made at the lowest part of the tumor, if possible, for easier drainage downwards, or at the thinnest and most prominent part of the tumor.  If the tumors are on the hands or feet, the incision should run along the length of the limb.
The incision should be made in the muscles, tendons, nerves, and arteries, and generally in all flat, non-flexed areas.  For areas that are flexed, the incision should be made according to the specific location.  If the tumor is in a fleshy area, it is better to leave it unlanced.
Until its maturity is complete, as we said. If you lance it before that, the pus will drain excessively, causing considerable harm and filth, and perhaps its edges and cavity will become hard. Some tumors may need a transverse incision, depending on the extremity, if necessary.
For small tumors, a single incision should be used; for large tumors, a wide incision or multiple incisions, in proportion to the size of the tumor.  Some tumors may require skin excision or incision if they have become like a perforated pouch.
Some have developed a dead or necrotic appearance, like many abscesses and cysts. Some are incised with a three-sided opening, while others are excised in the shape of a rue leaf, such as a hernia.
Some tumors require a circular or crescent-shaped incision, and similar cuts. Tumors without a head, such as smooth, flat tumors, should only be lanced with a simple incision. If the tumor is large and has accumulated pus over a long period, and you have lanced it, do not rush to drain all the pus at once.
Instead, drain some of it, and then compress the tumor for another day, and then drain some more pus. Repeat this process gradually until all of it is removed.
Especially if the patient is weak, a pregnant woman, a young child, or an elderly person, the vital spirit is often weakened by sudden pus discharge.  The patient may die unexpectedly; therefore, exercise extreme caution in these cases.
After lancing these tumors, you should clean and examine the wound. If the tumor wound is small or the incision simple, use a bandage made of linen or old cotton.  If the tumor is large and there are multiple incisions,
You should insert a thread into each incision, so that some of them connect. If part of the skin has been cut or eroded by the tumor, you should fill it with old cotton or linen strips without moisture and bandage it for three days.
Then remove it and treat it with appropriate ointments until it heals. If bleeding occurs during your work, use cold water and vinegar, after soaking a linen cloth in them, and apply it to the bleeding area repeatedly.
If bleeding persists, you should use the treatment and remedies described in many places in this book and in the classification.  If it is winter and the tumor area is richly supplied with nerves, you should wet the dressings with warm water and oil.
And place them on the affected area. If it is summer and the areas are fleshy, you should apply dressings soaked in water and oil, or water and cool oil, until, as we said, the third day. Then, you should undo the bandage and clean the tumor.
Use similar treatments until it heals.  This is the general knowledge you need for treating tumors. As for detailed methods, every tumor's treatment is described in a summarized, categorized manner.
Chapter Forty-One.
On the incision of tumors that appear on the scalp.
Small tumors appear on the scalp, which are a type of swelling containing sacs.  They resemble a hen's egg sac and come in various types, including fatty ones and those containing moisture resembling mud.
Some contain moisture like mush or soup, and others are hard and stony.  All of them are harmless to incise and remove, unless an artery is encountered during the incision.
The procedure for incision is to first probe it with the instrument whose image will follow, called the *midas*, so that you know what it contains. If it contains moisture, incise it lengthwise with a simple incision, like this:
Begin the incision from line ~b~ to line ~c~. If the moisture has drained, dissect the sac containing the moisture and remove it entirely, leaving nothing behind.  Often it returns if any is left.
Then, dip a piece of cotton in the astringent ointment, if available, or in salt water, and fill the wound with it. Leave it for another day, as it will consume any remaining sac tissue. Then, reapply the cotton with the astringent a second and third time if needed.
Until you are certain that no part of the sac remains. Then treat the wound with ointments until healed. If the swelling contains fatty tissue, incise it in this manner:
Make a cross-shaped incision, and place forceps on the edges of the wound.  Dissect it from all sides, and make every effort to remove the peritoneum containing it. If an artery is encountered, do as described previously.  Also, do this with the swelling if it is hardened from the incision.
The treatment is the same as mentioned.  Incising hardened swellings is easier, because they contain little blood and moisture. Some of these swellings, in the head of some people, contain no moisture at all. I incised a swelling in...
the head of an old woman and found the swelling as hard as solid, rough, white stone. No one could break it, even if they threw it, it would only splinter.  Other swellings on the head, unlike this one, such as those found in the heads of children, are different.
At the roots of the ears, make a simple incision in all of them, and cut along the bottom so that the material flows downwards more easily. Then treat it with the appropriate treatment.
Chapter Forty-two.
Concerning the incision of swine-like tumors that often appear on the neck. These tumors frequently appear on the neck, under the armpits, and in the groins, and may appear elsewhere on the body.  One may appear on the neck, or there may be many, and some arise from others.
Each such tumor is contained within its own membrane, like the swellings and tumors of the head, as we have described.  These tumors are of many types: some are stony, some contain moisture, and some are malignant, not responding to treatment.
Of those I saw, some were pleasing to the touch, their surface appearing close to the color of skin, and movable in all directions. They were not attached to the neck's nerves, veins, or arteries, nor were they deeply embedded. Therefore, it should be incised simply from top to bottom of the body, in this manner, from line ~J~ to line ~B~.
Peel it away from all sides, stretching the edges of the skin with a hook or two, or many hooks if necessary, as we have said for tumors of the head.  Remove it little by little, being careful not to cut any vein or nerve.  The scalpel should not be overly sharp.
So that your hand does not make more cuts or cause the patient distress, and you do not cut what is unnecessary. If you cut a vein or artery and are unable to proceed, apply powdered styptic or some of the astringents that stop bleeding to the wound.
Close the wound and leave it until the inflammation subsides, the wound relaxes, and it begins to fester.  The bleeding will stop at that point, so continue with your work until you are finished.  Then, probe with your index finger to see if there are any remaining, small, similar tumors.  Cut and remove them.
If there is a large vein at the base of the tumor, it should not be cut from its base. Instead, it should be tied with a strong double thread and left to fall away of its own accord without causing harm.  Then, fill the wound with used cotton that has been soaked in Egyptian ointment, and then treat it.
If you have cut the entire tumor, then you should bring the edges of the wound together and stitch it immediately after confirming that there is no remaining excess.  However, if you see that excess remains, due to the size of the tumor, then...
In that case, you must aim to cut the base and origin of the tumor, and use the stitching and other methods that we have described.  For tumors containing moisture, apply a simple compress where you see the area of ripening.  Place the compress beneath, as we have said.
Then, after the compress, apply bandages soaked in Egyptian ointment, or similar, to consume any remaining corruption.  Once you see that the wound is clean, treat it with ointments that promote healing until it is healed.
Chapter Forty-three.
On the incision of the throat for a tumor occurring within the throat.
The ancients mentioned this incision of the throat, but I have not seen anyone in our country perform it, and this follows their words.  However, those suffering from quinsy should avoid incision of the throat, as it will not benefit them.
In order that all the tumors and lungs may be diseased, and for those with a hot tumor in the mouth, throat, or tonsils, if there is no problem in the trachea, incision of the throat should be used to escape the harm caused by suffocation.
Therefore, if we wish to do this, we should incise the throat below three rings of the trachea, or four small transverse incisions between two rings, to the extent of the incision in the diaphragm, not in the cartilage.  This location is suitable for the incision.
Because it is devoid of flesh and its blood vessels are distant, if the surgeon is timid, he should stretch the throat skin with a hook, then cut the skin until, if he reaches the trachea, he should avoid the blood vessels if he sees any.
Then he should cut the diaphragm which we described, and he should determine the incision of the trachea from the phlegm that emerges from it, along with what is punctured, and from the cessation of sound.  He should leave the wound open for a time.  And when the time of fear of suffocation has passed, he should bring the edges of the wound together from the skin.
Then he should suture it alone, without the cartilage.  Thereafter, the remedies which promote flesh growth should be used until it heals.  Said the author of this book: The explanation of this passage which we have recounted is that if they see the patient with his throat obstructed by one of these tumors which we have mentioned...
When the patient was near death, and he feared imminent death, they would go to incising the throat, so that the patient might breathe somewhat through the wound and thus escape death.  They were instructed to leave the wound open until the disease's course was completed, which would be about three days or so.
Then they instructed to suture the wound and treat it until it healed.  What I witnessed myself, a servant took a knife and slashed her throat, cutting some of her lung's trachea.  I was summoned to treat her. I found her weakening, as a slaughtered animal does.  I uncovered the wound.
I found little blood coming from the wound, so I realized she had not cut an artery or vein. Air was escaping from the wound.  I quickly sutured the wound and treated it until she healed.  Nothing further happened to the servant except a slight hoarseness in her voice.
And after a few days, she returned to her best condition.  From this, we say that incising the throat is not dangerous.
Chapter Forty-Four.
On incision of the tumor appearing on the larynx from the outside, called the laryngeal tumor.
This tumor, called a laryngeal tumor, is a large tumor the color of the body, and is common in women. It comes in two types.
Either it is natural or it is accidental; as for the natural one, there is no remedy.  As for the accidental one, it is of two kinds; one resembling fatty tumors, and the other resembling the tumor that arises from arterial complication.  Incising it is dangerous. Therefore, it is not appropriate to use iron on it at all, unless it is small.  If you probe and investigate it with a probe and find it resembles a fatty tumor and is not connected to any blood vessels, then incise it as you would a tumor. And remove it with the sac it is contained in, if it is in a sac; otherwise, remove it entirely. Then treat the area with the appropriate treatment.
Chapter Forty-five.
On incising various tumors.
Tumors are of many kinds, and all their types have been mentioned in the classification.  Here, it is necessary to explain the difference between a tumor and an abscess, since it is problematic.  I say that an abscess is accompanied by heat, fever, and terrifying pain until the boil's fervor subsides. And when the pus is complete, the fever and intensity subside. A tumor is not accompanied by heat, fever, or pain. It is enclosed in a serous sac, a special covering, and is the same color as the body.  Its beginning is like a boil and it becomes like a grape, larger or smaller.
And it is of two types: either fatty or containing moisture. The colors of moisture are numerous, as mentioned in the classification. Therefore, when treating a tumor, one must first probe and examine it with the instrument called the probe, as its image will be shown in the chapter after this one.
Estimate the size of the tumor, then withdraw the probe and observe what follows. If a flowing moisture of any color emerges, make a simple incision, as described in other tumors. If no moisture follows the probe, know it is fatty.
Make a firm incision on it in this manner, as I have instructed you, and suspend it with hooks. Carefully dissect the skin from all sides and, if possible, remove the sac intact along with the tumor.
If the sac is punctured during the procedure and you cannot remove it whole, which often happens, remove it piece by piece until nothing remains.  If any part remains, small or large, the tumor will likely recur.
If you are overcome and a small portion remains, cauterize the wound with sharp cautery after you're finished.  Then, suture the wound and apply something to soothe the inflamed tumor. Treat it with other remedies until healed. If the tumor is large, stitch its edges and treat them with something that promotes healing.
If a bleeding vessel, whether pulsing or not, obstructs you, immediately apply powdered alum to the affected area.  Leave it applied for two or three days until the wound has suppurated and the bleeding subsides.  Then return to removing any remaining tumor tissue.

Chapter forty-seven.
In the treatment of the male breast.
The breast of some men, resembling that of women, may swell at the time of puberty, becoming swollen and unsightly.  Therefore, if one dislikes this, it is necessary to incise the breast with a crescent-shaped incision, of this form.
From line ~B~ to line ~C~. Then scrape away all the fat, then fill the wound with the medicated plaster, then stitch the edges of the wound together and treat it until it heals.  If the breast droops and relaxes due to weakness in the underlying structure, as occurs in women.
Therefore, it is necessary to make incisions in its upper sides, in the form of two crescent-shaped incisions, each of which connects to the other at their ends, so that the larger line encircles the smaller one, in this manner. From line ~B~ to line ~C~, then the skin between the incisions is scraped, the fat is removed, and the previously mentioned sutures, astringents, and necessary treatment are applied until it heals.
If you are unable to remove all that needs to be removed, either due to the patient's distress or bleeding, you should fill the wound with cotton soaked in corrosive ointment and leave it until it consumes the remaining fat, then treat it until it heals.
Chapter Forty-Eight.
On treating tumors that appear under the armpit.
These tumors that appear under the armpit are of the swine type, hard and contained within a cyst. Some contain moisture. Those containing moisture should be incised with a crescent-shaped incision, like this.
From line ~b~ to line ~c~.  If all the moisture in the tumor has been discharged, absorb it with worn-out cotton and leave it until the next day, then remove the cotton.
And treat it with the appropriate ointment until it heals. If the tumor is of the knotted swine type, incise it as described for the swine type tumors, as mentioned above. If the wound becomes purulent, use cautery as previously described.
Chapter forty-nine.
Incising the tumor that appears near the artery or vein, called an aneurysm.
If an artery is wounded and the skin above it adheres, a tumor often results.  Similarly, a vein can also cause swelling and a tumor.
The signs by which one knows whether the swelling and inflation are from an artery or a vein: if from an artery, the tumor is oblong, compact, deep within the body; if you press the tumor with your finger, you feel it like a creaking sound.
And that which is from the vein, the swelling is round, on the surface of the body.  Incising these tumors is dangerous, especially those in the armpit, groin, and neck, and in many other parts of the body, if they are very large.  It is necessary to avoid treating them with iron.
Also, those in the extremities or head should be avoided.  For those that are a swelling of the arterial opening, make a lengthwise incision in the skin, then widen the incision with hooks.  Then, dissect the artery and free it from the membranes.
Until it is exposed, then insert a needle beneath it, piercing it to the other side.  Tie the artery with a doubled thread in two places, as I instructed you regarding the arteries in the temples.  Then, pierce with a scalpel the area between the ligaments, until all the blood within it is expelled.
The swelling will subside, then use a treatment that produces pus, until the ligaments fall off.  Then, treat with appropriate ointments until it heals.
If the swelling is from a vein rupture, you should grasp as much of the swelling and skin as possible.  Then, insert a needle beneath the area you have grasped, passing it through with a double thread, until you bring it out the other side. Then, firmly tie the swelling with it, as I described to you in tying the abscess on both sides with the ends of the thread. If you fear the threads will slip, insert another needle with a different thread under the entire swelling at the intersection point of the first needle.
Tie your threads in four places, then slit the swelling in the middle.  Once what is within has come out, cut off any excess skin and leave what was tied.  Then, apply a compress soaked in potion and oil. Then use a treatment involving twisting and ointments until it heals.
Chapter Fifty.
In the swelling that arises from nerve twisting.
As swelling occurs in arteries and veins, so too does swelling occur in the nerve if struck or caused by excessive exertion, or similar.  This is more common in the wrists and ankles.
And in every location where joints move, it is a hard swelling resembling the color of the rest of the body, and in most cases is painless.  If pressed firmly, the patient feels a sensation akin to numbness, and the swelling is not deeply embedded in the body.
Rather, it is beneath the skin and moves in every direction, but does not go forward or backward.  What is in the joints should not be treated with iron, for it may cause a lasting injury.  And what is in the head or forehead...
Cut the skin with a scalpel. If the swelling is small, grasp it with a forceps and cut it from the root. If large, tie it with a ligature and dissect it, then remove it. Close the wound with stitches and treat it until it heals.
Chapter Fifty-One.
On the removal of warts that appear on the abdomen.
Warts, called "fungi" because of their resemblance to mushrooms, often appear on the abdomen and other parts of the body in many people.  Their base is thin and their head thick; sometimes their edges thicken, and they can range from small to very large.
I observed a man with two warts on his abdomen, resembling mushrooms in appearance.  Their bases were thin and white, their edges thickened, and they constantly secreted moisture. I removed them, weighing approximately eighteen ounces for one and six ounces for the other.
If the wart is moist, white, and thin-based, cut it with a wide scalpel, having heated cautery instruments at hand, as considerable bleeding often occurs when removing them.
If bleeding is excessive, cauterize it. If the patient is fearful of cutting with metal, take a strong lead thread and bind the wart.  Leave it for two days, then increase the tension of the lead.  Continue tightening the lead.
Continue tightening until the wart breaks off and falls off of its own accord. If the wart has a thick base, there is some risk and danger in removing it completely, especially if it is on the abdomen.  However, it may be possible to cut it in half or partially remove it.
Then cauterize it to prevent recurrence, and treat the wound until it heals. Beware of removing a wart that is dark, insensitive, and poorly defined, for it is a cancerous tumor. We will discuss cancer later.
The Fifty-Second Chapter.
On the treatment of umbilical protrusions.
Umbilical protrusions arise from various causes: either from a rupture in the peritoneum lining the abdomen, through which the intestines or bowels protrude, as occurs in other hernias; or from blood emanating from a vein or artery, as previously mentioned; or from gas that has accumulated within it.
If it is due to a rupture of the peritoneum and the protrusion of viscera, the tumor will be similar in color to the body, soft and painless, and of varying position. If it is due to the protrusion of the bowels, its position will be even more varied than we have described.  When you press it with your fingers, it will disappear and then return, and perhaps accompanied by gurgling.  It will greatly increase in size during bathing and severe exertion.  If it is due to moisture, it will be soft.
If it does not disappear when pressed with your hand, and neither increases nor decreases in size, if it is due to blood, the swelling will appear dark in color with these symptoms. If it is due to a growing mass of flesh, the swelling will be hard, firm, and remain at the same size.
If it is due to gas, the touch will be soft.  The course of action in this case is to observe.  If the umbilical protrusion is due to blood from an artery or vein, or gas, then one should refrain from treating it, for there is danger and deception in this, as I informed you in the chapter where I discussed tumors arising from arteries and veins.
If the umbilical protrusion is due to the bowels or viscera, you should instruct the patient to hold himself, stand upright, and extend himself, then apply a bandage around the entire umbilicus. Then, instruct him to lie on his back before you.  Then, use a wide scalpel to incise around the umbilicus at the area marked with the bandage.  Then, elevate the center of the swelling upward with a large hook.  Finally, tie the incision site with strong thread or fine silk thread, securely.
Tie the bandage in a knot, then open the center of the constricted swelling above the bandage and insert your index finger into it.  Feel for the bowels. If you find the bowels have been caught by the bandage, loosen the knot and push the bowels back into the abdomen. If you find a viscus, pull it out with a hook.
And cut off the excess. If an artery or vein is encountered, tie it securely. Then, return to your task. Take two needles, and thread them with strong threads.  Insert both needles into the incision you made around the swelling, so they pierce it firmly. Then pull the swelling tight in four places on the needles.
If you wish, remove the needles and leave the area until the pulled flesh rots and falls off on its own, or cut it if it becomes thin and rotten. Then treat it with the appropriate ointments until it heals. If the umbilical protrusion is from flesh grown there.
Or if it is from moisture, then you should cauterize the swelling, as we said, and remove the blood or moisture found within. Then treat it with what will heal the area.
Chapter Fifty-three.
On the treatment of cancer.
We have previously mentioned the types of cancer in the classification, and how to treat it with medications, and warned against treating it with iron lest it ulcerate.  We have also mentioned cancer that develops in the uterus and warned against treating it.
The ancients stated that if the cancer is in a place where it can be completely removed, like cancer in the breast or the thigh, and similar accessible organs, it should be removed entirely, especially if it is small and newly formed.  However, if it is advanced and large, it should not be approached.
I have not been able to cure anyone of it, nor have I seen anyone before me achieve this.  If it is accessible, as we have said, the procedure is to first make incisions, multiple times, to remove the black fluid and then dissect it if it is evident that there is an abundance of blood vessels.
Then, the afflicted area is incised in a way that allows for access to the work.  Then, appropriate hooks are placed on the cancer and it is scraped from all sides, along with the skin, thoroughly, until nothing of the roots remains.  Allow the blood to flow, and do not cut it quickly.
Instead, squeeze the affected areas and remove all the thick blood with your hand or with any suitable tools. If during the procedure, you encounter a significant blood flow from a cut artery or vein, cauterize the vessel until the bleeding stops, and then treat the affected area with other methods until healed.

Chapter fifty-seven.

The easiest path to safety, I say, is that the ancients did not mention circumcision in any of their books because it was not used in their religions, but rather, it is what we have acquired.

One is then forced to make another cut, causing renewed pain, and cleansing with a cautery does not prevent cutting the urethral tip, because it might enter its opening.  As for cleansing with a nail, the skin might tear, spoiling your work, or the boy's foreskin might be too short by nature.
Many are born in this condition, and thus do not require cleansing.  I have seen this.  As for cleansing with scissors and tying with thread, experience has revealed its superiority to me, because the scissors make a uniform cut, since the blade on top is like the blade on the bottom.
When you squeeze your hand with the blades in alignment, you make a cut of equal measure and in a single motion.  The thread then acts as a wall for the urethral skin from all sides, preventing any mistakes.  First, you must reassure the boy.

Chapter Fifty-Eight.
On treating urine retention in the bladder.
Urine retention in the bladder occurs due to blockage by a stone, clotted blood, pus, or overgrown flesh, and similar things.  If you treat this with the various treatments described in the classification, as we mentioned.

In this case, the neck of the bladder will be forced to push the stone backward, and the urine will pass. If the urine does not pass by the method mentioned and the patient's condition worsens, then the removal of the obstruction by an instrument called a catheter, of which this is an image [of], must be used.
It is made of silver, thin and smooth, hollow like the tube of a bird's feather, about a cubit and a half long.  It has a gentle funnel at its head.  The method of using it to draw out urine is to take a doubled thread and tie to its end wool or cotton securely.
The thread's end is inserted into the bottom of the catheter, and any excess wool is trimmed with nippers to ensure it fits snugly inside the tube, like a stopper.  Then, the catheter is lubricated with oil, butter, or egg white, and the patient sits on a chair.
The patient's bladder and urethra are lubricated with wet dressings or lukewarm oil and water.  Then, the catheter is gently inserted into the urethra until it reaches the base of the urethra.  The urethra is then pulled upwards towards the navel, and the catheter is pushed inside.
Once the urethra is pulled, the catheter is pushed inside until it is near the rectum.  The penis is then tilted downwards, with the catheter inside, and pushed further until it reaches the bladder and the patient feels a void.
This method is used because the urinary passage is curved.  The thread with wool is pulled slightly taut, allowing the urine to follow the wool.  Then it is removed, and the urine is drained.  The catheter is then reinserted.
Continue doing this until the bladder is empty and the patient feels relief.

Chapter Sixty.
On removing the stone.
We have previously discussed the types of stones, their treatment with medicine, and the differences between stones formed in the kidneys and those formed in the bladder, along with the signs of each. We have also described stones requiring manual removal and incision.
This is particularly the stone formed in the bladder, and that which originates in the urethra.  I will describe this with clear explanation and brevity.
I say that bladder stones are most often found in children.  A sign is that the urine expelled from the bladder is thin, like water.
Sand appears in the urine, the child rubs and plays with their penis, and often the testicles hang down and spread out.  The anus also protrudes in many cases.  Children recover more easily from this up to the age of fourteen, whereas it is more difficult in the elderly.  Young adults fall somewhere in between.
The larger the stone, the easier the treatment; the smaller the stone, the opposite is true.  Therefore, in treatment, the patient should first be given an enema to remove all the debris in their bowels, since this may prevent the stone from being found during examination.
Then, the patient's legs are held and shaken downwards to bring the stone to the neck of the bladder, or they are made to jump from a raised position several times.  Then, the patient is seated upright before you, with hands under their thighs, so the entire bladder inclines downwards, and then it is examined.
Palpate it from the outside. If you feel a stone in the space, immediately make an incision on it. If it is not at all within your reach, then you should lubricate the index finger with grease from the left hand, if the patient is a child, or the middle finger.
If it is a fully developed male, then insert it into their rectum and search for the stone.  Once it is under your finger, move it little by little to the neck of the bladder, then press on it with your finger and push it out towards the place where you want to make the incision.
Then order a servant to squeeze the bladder with his hand, and order another servant to stretch the labia upwards with his right hand, and with his other hand stretch the skin below the labia towards the place where the incision should be made.  Then take the scalpel, which has this form:
Make the incision between the anus and the labia, not in the middle, but on the side of the left pubic region.  The incision should be aligned with the stone, your finger in the rectum, pressing it outwards in an oblique manner so that the incision is wide on the outside and narrow on the inside, to the extent possible for the stone to exit.
Do not increase the pressure. Perhaps the finger in the rectum will push the stone out during the incision, with little difficulty. Be aware that some stones have angles and edges, making their removal difficult.
Some are smooth, like acorns, and round, making removal easy.  If a stone has angles and edges, increase the incision slightly. If it does not come out this way, you must devise a method, perhaps grasping it with a firm instrument, having a tip like a scraper.
So that it grips the stone and does not slip, or insert a delicate, curved instrument beneath it. If you cannot manage it this way, widen the incision slightly.  If much blood is present, cauterize it with a cautery iron.
If there are multiple stones, remove the largest first into the bladder opening, then make an incision.  After that, remove the smaller ones.  Do the same if there are more than two.  If the stone is very large, it is unwise to make a large incision, as this could expose the patient to one of two dangers.
Either he will die, or he will suffer from constant urine leakage, because the area will not heal.  However, try to push it out. Or devise a method for breaking it with forceps to remove it in pieces.
Once you have completed your procedure, treat the large wound with kandar, sopor, shiyan, and a tight bandage. Place a moistened cloth with oil, syrup, rosewater, or cool water on top to reduce inflammation. The patient should lie on their back, and do not loosen the bandages until the third day.
When the swelling subsides, bathe the area with copious amounts of water and oil.  Then treat it with the نخلى (palm) ointment and the basilikon ointment until healed. If a large, inflammatory swelling, ulceration, or similar occurs at the wound site, or if blood clots in the bladder, preventing urination, this can be diagnosed by the presence of blood in the urine.
Insert your finger into the wound and remove the blood.  If it remains, it will lead to the corruption and putrefaction of the bladder.  Then wash the wound with vinegar, water, and salt.  Treat each type of issue with the appropriate remedy until it heals.
Throughout the treatment, the thighs should be tied together to hold the medications in place on the area. If the stone is small and lodged in the urethra, causing inflammation and preventing urination, treat it with the methods I've described before it becomes fractured.

The tip of the file is sharpened to a point, and it is attached to a stick.  Then, a thread is used to tie the penis below the stone, to prevent it from returning to the bladder.  Then, the file is gently inserted into the urethra until it reaches the stone itself.

Chapter Sixty-one.
On removing stones in women.
Stones rarely develop in women.  If one of them presents with a stone, treatment is difficult and impossible for many reasons.  One is that the woman may be a virgin, and another that you will not find a woman who allows herself to be examined by a physician.
If she is virtuous or from a household of women with modesty, and the third that you do not find a woman skilled in this craft, particularly in manual work.  And the fourth, that the location of the incision for a stone in a woman is far from the stone's location, requiring a deep incision.
And there is danger in this. If necessity dictates it, one should find a skilled female physician; this is rare.  If unavailable, seek a virtuous and compassionate physician, or bring a skilled midwife or a woman with some knowledge in this craft.
You should have her present and instruct her to do all that you command her to do, first to examine the stone. If the woman is a virgin, you should insert a finger into her vagina and search for the stone. If found, you should press it under your finger.
Then you should instruct her to cut it open. If she is not a virgin, but a married woman, instruct the midwife to insert her finger into the patient's vagina and search for the stone, after placing her left hand on the bladder and squeezing it well.
If she finds it, she should guide it out from the opening of the bladder downward to the extent of her ability, until it reaches the root of the thigh. Then she should make an incision along the middle of the vagina, near the root of the thigh, whichever side the stone is felt in that area.

If she feels the stone, she should widen the incision as much as she knows is necessary for the stone to come out.  Know that there are many types of stones.
Among them are small and large, smooth and rough, long and round, and those with branches. Know their types so that you can ascertain what you want. If blood prevails, sprinkle the powdered alum on the area and hold it for an hour until the bleeding stops, then resume your work.
Until the stone comes out.  Prepare the instruments mentioned for removing stones in men, to aid you in your work.  If you are overwhelmed by bleeding and know the bleeding is from a severed artery, place the styptic on the area.
