Chapter Sixty-two.
In the fracture of the watery membranes.
Watery membranes are the accumulation of moisture in the white peritoneum which is under the skin of the scrotum surrounding the testicle.  It is called the scrotum.  It may also be in a special membrane which nature prepares for it on one side of the testicle.
Until one might think it is another testicle. It lies between the skin of the scrotum and the white peritoneum that we mentioned. This only happens rarely.  This collection of fluid arises from weakness affecting the testicles; this substance is deposited on them.  It may also be caused by a blow to the testicles.
This moisture can be of various colors; either yellow, or bloody red, or dark-green, or white-watery, which is the most common.  The signs by which it is recognized are...
Where the water accumulates, if it is in the white peritoneum we mentioned, the swelling is somewhat oval in shape, like an egg, and the testicle is not visible because the moisture surrounds it from all sides.
If the moisture is in a separate membrane, the swelling will be round on one side of the testicle.
Therefore, one might imagine it to be another testicle. If the moisture is between the scrotal skin and the white peritoneum, it can be felt.  To determine the color of the moisture, probe the swelling with the square probe whose image was previously given; judge its color based on what is within the probe's impression.
If we turn to treatment with leeches, the patient should be bled, if possible, and if you find his body is full. Then he should lie on his back on a slightly raised surface, placing many cloths underneath, and you should sit on his left side.
And instruct a servant to sit on his right, extending the patient's penis to one side of the scrotum and towards the flank of the abdomen. Then, take a wide scalpel and incise the scrotal skin lengthwise from the center to near the pubic area.
The incision should be straight and parallel to the line that divides the scrotal skin in half, until you reach the white peritoneum.  Carefully peel it back, avoiding cutting it.  Focus your peeling on the side closest to the testicle, and extend the peeling as far as you can.
Then, widely separate the water-filled peritoneum and remove all the water.  Then, separate the edges of the incision with forceps, lift the peritoneum upward without touching the scrotal skin, and cut the peritoneum as you can, either in one piece or in sections.
Especially its thin side, for if you do not completely sever it, you cannot prevent the water from returning.  If the testicle protrudes from its sheath during your work, return it to place, then close the edges of the scrotal skin with sutures.
Then treat it as you would any other surgical wound until it heals. If the testicle is damaged from another disease, you must tie off the vessels in the sac to prevent bleeding, then sever the testicle from the sac, remove the testicle, and treat it as previously described.
If the accumulated fluid is in both sides, know that there are two hernias.  Incise the other side as you did the first, or if you prefer, perform the procedure once and then clean away the blood.
Place wool soaked in oil or rose oil into the incisions.  Place another layer of wool soaked in a mixture of liquid and oil over that, and spread it over the testicles and the abdominal area.  Place folded cloths, the *rafā'id*, on top of that. Bind it from above with the six-pointed bandage in this manner:
Then take two cloths, sew them together, and fill them with fluffy wool in this manner.  Make the straps for binding out of damp cotton or wool, and secure them tightly with the belt of the trousers.  One of the straps should run from the buttock to the back.
The second strap should run towards the pubic area, with the other two straps on the lower part of the thighs, and the remaining two on the inner sides of the thighs.  Secure all the straps to the belt of the trousers.  This hernia can also be treated by cauterization instead of incision with an iron.
This is done by taking a thin, heated cautery iron and making an incision in the scrotum's skin, continuing until the white membrane containing water is exposed.  Take another cautery iron in this manner:
It resembles a Greek eye, then cauterize that membrane while hot until all moisture is expelled. Then, grasp the membrane with forceps and cauterize the containing layer, cutting it as much as possible to remove it completely, while preserving the testicle, avoiding any contact with fire.
Know that this method is safer and avoids blood loss, and is better than incision.  If during the incision or cauterization you encounter a hot swelling, blood loss, or anything else, then you should treat each occurrence with the appropriate remedies as previously described.
If the patient is timid and cannot endure this procedure due to its unpleasantness, then let him use the cauterization method as described, if it is appropriate according to the previous indications.
If the discharge is watery, the patient should sit on a high chair.  Then cauterize the swelling below with a wide probe.  Keep the cauterization along the body, and make the opening wide enough for all the water to drain.  Then apply cotton to the area and bandage it.
Allow the remaining water to drain, then treat it until the wound heals.  If the water does not drain during the cauterization, it is likely because the white membrane is obstructing the wound opening, preventing the water from exiting.
In that case, you should insert a goose feather, or the instrument used to extract water from abscesses, as previously shown, or slightly enlarge the wound opening.
Know that the water may return and accumulate after six months or so.  If it does, cauterize it in the same manner, and the patient should endure the days.
Chapter Sixty-three.
On the fissure of fleshy tissues and their treatment.
Know that a fissure on these fleshy tissues is a perilous condition, often leading to death.  Therefore, we advise against intervention and for a non-invasive approach.  I will now describe the procedures for handling them and the types of tumors.
I say that many tumors can arise in bodies composed of two layers.  This occurs from various causes, either from a sharp discharge accumulated in the layers or from a blow.  The tumor's color matches the body's color and is devoid of moisture.
The tumor is hard, and it may be calcified, its color like lead, and without sensation.  It may also result from the constriction and swelling of the arteries, as previously mentioned, or from swelling of the veins.  If from the swelling of the arteries, this is recognized by the scattering of the tumor.
If you press it with your fingers, it should not be incised.  As for that which arises from swelling of the veins, nothing disperses upon pressing with the fingers.  This type may be incised, along with other fleshy tissues.
This is done by incising the scrotum's skin, then pulling the testicle upward and removing it from the white peritoneum.  The attachment is separated from the vessels, the vessels are tied off, and the attachment is cut after it is separated from all sides of the testicle. If the testicle has adhered to the growing tissues.
Then the testicle must be extracted and cut.  If the adhesion is between any of the peritonea or between the vessels, then all such adhesions must be separated and cut in a circular fashion.
If the fleshy growth is at the point of adhesion, which is on the back side, it must all be cut, and the testicle removed, as we have said.  Once your work is complete, wash the wound with wet wool soaked in rose-scented oil and medicate it with other treatments until it heals.
Sixty-fourth Chapter.
On treating dropsy with hydrocele.
Hydrocele is a swollen, twisted mass resembling a bunch of grapes, with relaxation of the testicles.  It makes movement, exercise, and walking difficult for the patient.  The treatment for this condition is similar to that previously mentioned.
However, it is necessary to describe the procedure as practiced by our predecessors.  I say that the patient should sit on a raised chair, then push the testicles downward, and grasp the skin of the scrotum with your fingers, near the vessels close to the penis.
A servant of yours holds it and stretches it forcefully.  Then, using a wide, sharp scalpel, make a slanted incision alongside the vessels until the vessels are exposed.  Then, dissect from each side, as I described to you in the dissection of the arteries of the temples, and insert a needle with a doubled thread into them.
Tie it at the initial points where the hydrocele appeared and tie it again at its end. Then, cut it in the middle with a vertical incision along the body and remove the foul, putrid moisture collected within.
Then, treat the wound as with other surgical wounds that require time to heal. Removing the vessels that nourish one testicle is acceptable.  If the hydrocele affects all vessels, then one testicle must be removed along with the vessels so that the remaining testicle will not be deprived of nourishment. Before cutting the vessels, it will wither and be of no use.
Chapter Sixty-five.
In the treatment of intestinal obstruction.
This obstruction occurs when a fissure appears in the peritoneum extending across the abdomen near the groin, and the intestine from that hernia protrudes into one testicle.
This hernia is either from a fissure in the peritoneum or from its extension. These two types arise from various causes, such as a blow, a leap, a shout, or lifting something heavy, and the like.  If it's from an extension of the peritoneum, the hernia develops gradually over a long period.
It does not occur suddenly.  The swelling is gradual, extending inwards as the peritoneum constricts the intestine.  If it is from a fissure in the peritoneum, it is accompanied by a severe immediate pain at the onset, and the swelling will be distinctly visible under the skin nearby.
The intestine protrudes and passes outside the peritoneum.  The hernia may also include intestinal contents, making it an intestinal and fecal hernia.  It might also include wind, or the intestinal contents might become stagnant, potentially leading to the patient's demise.
Because it causes severe pain and rumbling, especially if constricted.  Treating these types of ailments with iron is dangerous, so one should avoid that.  The procedure is to instruct the patient to return the intestine into its cavity with their hand, if possible.
Then the patient lies on their back before you, and their legs are raised.  The skin near the groin is then pulled upwards, and the entire scrotum is incised lengthwise.  Hooks are inserted into the edges of the incision, as needed, to widen the opening.
The incision should be large enough to allow the hernia to emerge. Then, the peritoneum beneath the scrotal skin is peeled back until the tough, white peritoneum is exposed on all sides.  At that point, insert your index finger below the hernia sac.
Separate the white peritoneum beneath the hernia sac from the second peritoneum, thus releasing any adhesions behind the hernia.  Then, with your right hand, pull inwards towards the scrotal skin.  Simultaneously, with your left hand, pull the white peritoneum upwards and lift the hernia sac towards the incision.
Instruct the attendant to pull the hernia sac upwards, and you release any adhesions behind it completely.  Examine with your finger to ensure there is no twisted intestine within the tough white peritoneum.  If you find any, push it back into the abdomen below.
Then take a needle with a thick thread, twisted from ten strands, and insert it at the end of the peritoneum beneath the scrotal skin near the incision.  Cut the ends of the thread, leaving four strands. Then, interweave the strands in a secure manner and tie the peritoneum, which we mentioned is beneath the scrotal skin, tightly on both sides.  Then also wrap and tie the ends of the threads securely, so that nothing can reach the blood vessels supplying the area. To prevent a painful swelling, also tie a second knot, external to the first, a distance of less than two fingers. After these two ligatures, leave a length of peritoneum beneath the scrotal skin equal to the width of a finger.
To prevent a painful swelling, also tie a second knot, external to the first, a distance of less than two fingers. After these two ligatures, leave a length of peritoneum beneath the scrotal skin equal to the width of a finger. Cut the remaining portion entirely around and remove the hernia sac. Then, make an incision below the scrotal skin, one that bleeds and oozes as we previously described. Then use wool soaked in oil and place it in the wound. Apply the ligature as we described.
The white peritoneum, which we mentioned, may need to be cauterized after cutting it to prevent bleeding, which often occurs. Leave the ligature until it falls off naturally. If it takes too long to fall off, apply warm water and something that will cause sweating to hasten its removal.
Then treat the wound with all the other treatments for surgical wounds until it heals.
Chapter Sixty-Six.
In this case of flatulence, I have seen no one dare treat it with iron.  The ancients have described a method, as we described for the flatulence with a hernia, which involves tying the vessels gently below the incision.
Then, make an incision in the middle and treat the swelling to open it, until the vessels fall out. Treat the wound as we described until it heals.
Chapter Seventy-Six.
In the hernia that is in the groin.
The hernia in the groin may appear, as we said, but nothing from the intestines descends into the groin. If it does descend, it is slight and returns at all times.
But if it lasts a long time, the tear in the peritoneum increases, so that the intestines and the hernia descend into the scrotum. This occurs because of the extension of the peritoneum which is in the groin, as we said. This is because the peritoneum extends, then relaxes and bulges the place, and its treatment is by cauterization, as I have previously described.
And it may be treated with iron in this manner: the patient lies on their back before you, then you cut the swollen protruding hernia site transversely, about the size of three fingers. Then you cauterize the peritonea beneath the skin.
Until the white peritoneum beneath is exposed, then you take a probe, place it on the protruding peritoneum and press it inward towards the abdomen. Then you stitch the two protruding parts of the peritoneum on the probe's edge, and join one to the other with stitches.
Then you withdraw the probe, and do not cut the peritoneum at all, nor touch the testicle or anything else, as I instructed you in treating intestinal hernia. Then treat it with the treatment for surgical injuries.
If the threads are severed, clean and close the wound until it heals, then the peritoneum will contract and the hernia will not increase.
And this is the best method in this location, because it is closer to safety.
Chapter Sixty-eight.
Relaxation of the scrotum skin.
The scrotum skin often relaxes in some people without the underlying tissues relaxing, which makes the appearance unsightly.  Therefore, whoever wishes to treat this, should have the patient lie on their back.
And he cuts all the relaxed skin from the thick skin, then brings the edges together.  If you wish, first stitch the excess relaxed skin with three or four stitches, ensuring they are secure, then cut between the stitches.
Then treat it with the treatment of other surgical wounds until it heals and the stitches fall out.
Chapter Sixty-nine.
On castration.
Castration is forbidden in our religion.
For this reason, I should not have mentioned it in this book, but I have done so for two reasons.  One is so that a physician, when asked about it, will have knowledge of it, and will know how to treat those affected. The other is that we often need to castrate certain animals for our benefit.
Such as sheep, oxen, and cats, and similar animals.  I say that castration is of two types: either by crushing or by cutting.  In the case of crushing, we place the animal in hot water until its testicles relax and soften and hang down.
Then you crush it with your hand until it breaks down and no longer feels distinct when touched.  As for castration by cutting, you should hold the animal and grasp the skin of its testicles with your left hand, then tie off the attachments and cut a single incision into each testicle.
Until the testicles appear, then cut them after removing their coverings, leaving no part of the tunics except the thin tunics that are on the vessels. This type of castration is better than the one that is done by crushing.
Because crushing may leave some of the testicles, and the animal will desire mating, and then the wound is treated until it heals.
Chapter Seventy.
In the treatment of hermaphrodites.
Hermaphrodites in males come in two types. One type appears as a space below or on the scrotal skin between the testicles, shaped like a woman's vulva with hair, and urine may flow from the area on the scrotal skin.
And in females, there is one type, occurring above the vulva on the pubis like small, protruding male testicles, one of them resembling a man's penis, and both resembling the vulva.  Treatment for all three types.
The two types of males and the single type of female: the excess flesh should be cut away until the effect is gone, then treated as other surgical wounds until healed.  As for the second type of male from whom urine emerges from the scrotal skin, there is no procedure nor healing.
Chapter Seventy-One.
On cutting the penis and the protruding flesh of women's vulvas.
The clitoris may increase in size beyond the natural state, becoming unsightly and misshapen.  In some women, it may enlarge to the extent it resembles that of men, interfering with sexual intercourse.
The excess clitoris should be grasped with your hand or forceps and cut away, but do not cut excessively deep into the root, lest there be profuse bleeding.
Then treat it with surgical remedies until healed. As for the growing flesh, it is flesh that grows in the mouth of the uterus, filling it up.
And it may protrude outwards, like a tail. Therefore, some of the earlier physicians called it the "tail-like disease". It should be cut, like the clitoris, and treated until healed.
Chapter Seventy-Two.
On treating varicose veins.
Varicose veins are when a woman's vagina is not perforated or the perforation is small, and this may be either natural or accidental. The accidental case stems from a prior ailment, and may result from excess, overgrown tissue.
Or thin or thick peritoneum, either deep within the uterus, or on its sides, or at its top or bottom. It prevents sexual intercourse and procreation, and possibly menstruation.  The midwife can diagnose it by observation.
If the obstruction is visible and close, but if not visible, then she should probe with her fingers or a probe. If the obstruction is from thin peritoneum, and is close to the labia, then promptly pierce it. This is done by making a sort of flap on the labia, and then placing both thumbs of the hands together.
The woman should be on her back with her legs spread, and then the labia should be pulled firmly until the thin peritoneum is pierced and the obstruction is opened. Then, take wool, soak it in oil, and place it on the area.  The woman should be sexually intimate every day to prevent the area from closing again.
If the peritoneum is thick and dense, it should be cut with a wide scalpel, resembling a thin, flat blade. If the obstruction is from overgrown flesh, it should be suspended with forceps and cut. Have with you remedies to stop bleeding without causing further injury, such as gum arabic, dates, and frankincense mixed with egg white.
Then a wide lead tube should be used to prevent the wound from closing quickly.  It is held in place for days, and dried flax thread is used.  Then, treat with the usual methods for treating abscesses until healed.  Additional growths of flesh may appear in the uterus, and these should be cut in the same manner.
Unless it is a cancerous tumor, a cancerous tumor in the uterus should never be treated with iron.
Chapter Seventy-Three.
On treating hemorrhoids, warts, and red pimples that appear in the vulva of women.
Hemorrhoids are the swelling of the mouths of the veins, causing the continuous flow of much blood.  If the hemorrhoids progress, they become warts.  We have already described their types and signs in the classification, and we will mention them here.
Concerning which hemorrhoids and warts are treatable and which are not: I say that hemorrhoids and warts that are deep within the uterus and do not become apparent to touch are not treatable with iron.  Those that are in the mouth of the uterus and are felt are the ones to be treated.
The woman should enter a cool room, then use a lancet or a rough cloth to remove the warts from their roots, cutting them off.  Then, after bleeding from one of the blood-stopping instruments, sprinkle it with something that stops the bleeding, without causing further irritation.
Such as acacia, myrrh, frankincense, and similar substances. Then raise her legs to the wall for an hour, then lower them into cold water.  If the bleeding persists, have her sit in a decoction of sumac, pomegranate peels, oak bark, and similar substances.  Then take barley flour, mix it with honey and vinegar.
Apply this as a poultice to her back.  If the bleeding stops, then cease. Otherwise, apply leeches to the lower back, breasts, and chest without any stipulations. Then take wool, soak it in the juice of asphodel or the juice of goat's rue or the juice of buckthorn twigs, mixed with oak-bark infusion, and apply it to the affected area.
If the swelling subsides, treat the area with ointment until healed.  As for the red scab, it resembles the heads of boils, rough in appearance.  It is necessary to cut off what appears of it, just as described for warts, and treat it until healed.
Chapter seventy-four.
Concerning the treatment of abscesses appearing in the uterus.
Many types of tumors may appear in the uterus, such as cancer, hard tumors, ulcers, gangrene, eating sores, polyps, hemorrhoids, fissures, warts, and hot tumors.  All of these diseases and their types and signs have been described in the classification.
Therefore, in this section, we must discuss the hot tumor appearing in the uterus, if it is among the tumors that gather over a period of time, how to treat it with iron.  One must observe if the initial pain of the tumor is sharp, with throbbing, inflammation, and fever.
If you see it red, and if it is sensitive to touch, do not rush to treat it with iron. Treat it with what aids in maturation until these symptoms subside.  Then, the woman should sit on a chair with two seats, leaning back.
And she should bring her legs together towards the lower abdomen, with her thighs spread apart, and place her arms under her knees, securing them with a suitable bandage.
Then the midwife should sit on the right side and use the instrument for opening the cervix, whose image will follow later.  If you wish to use this instrument, you must first assess the depth of the woman's uterus to ensure the instrument does not penetrate deeper than the uterus.
If the instrument is too deep, it will harm the patient.  If the instrument is deeper than necessary, applicators should be placed on the lip of the cervix to prevent the entire instrument from entering the uterine cavity.  The spiral that runs within the instrument should be situated at the top.
While you hold the instrument, the attendant turns the spiral until the cervix is opened. If a boil appears and is soft and tender when felt by hand, it should be incised at its head with a wide scalpel.  If the entire discharge has been evacuated.
A soft wick soaked in rose oil or green oil with some astringent should be placed in the wound. The wick should protrude from the incision in the cervix, and pure wool soaked in boiled baker's water should be applied externally to the uterus and pubis.
Then, after two days, it should be treated with the appropriate ointment until healed.
The uterus and wound may be washed by injecting water and honey, then water boiled with licorice root or long wormwood. Then, return to the ointment. This is if the boil is hidden and inside the uterus.
Iron should not be used for treatment; rather, it should be treated as described in the division.
Chapter seventy-five.
On instructing midwives on how to treat live fetuses that emerge in an unnatural form.
The midwife should first know the natural form of childbirth.  Signs include the woman's desire to descend, to breathe air, and the lessening of her postpartum discomfort.
And if the expulsion of the fetus is rapid, know that this birth is natural.  The fetus will emerge head-first, with the placenta either attached or hanging from the umbilical cord.  If you see these signs, you should press her abdomen to facilitate the rapid expulsion of the fetus.
If it descends head-first, the placenta descends with it, and it is cleaned well of those impurities.  Any birth other than this is unnatural and reprehensible, because the fetus may emerge on its feet, and its hands or feet may emerge before its head.
And one hand or one foot, or its head and hand, or it emerges curled up, or perhaps turned upside down, and similar reprehensible forms.  The midwife should be skillful and gentle with all these things, and cautious of mistakes and errors.
And I am explaining each form and how to manage it so you may recognize it. If the fetus emerges headfirst—the natural way—and the woman feels intense pain, and childbirth is difficult, and you see her strength weakening, have her sit on a chair and have women restrain her.
Then bandage her uterus with cooked herbs and moist ointments.  Then the midwife should insert a small scalpel between her fingers, to cut the placenta, or cut it with her fingernail, until the moisture within drains out.  Then press the woman's abdomen until the fetus descends.
If it does not descend, the woman should be injected with the juice of fenugreek mixed with the grease of an ointment, then after the injection she should be encouraged to cough and be induced to sneeze with a stick, and her mouth and nose should be held. The fetus will emerge shortly. If the fetus's hands emerge first, they should gently be pushed back in, little by little.
If entry is still not possible, place the woman on a raised platform and lift her legs up.  Then shake the platform on the ground, and the woman should hold on to prevent falling during the shaking.  If the hands do not enter and the fetus dies, cut off the hands and pull out the rest of the fetus.
Or tie his hands with a cloth and pull them gently, and he will come out.  If the fetus emerges on its feet, the feet should be pushed upwards, then the fetus should be turned gradually until it is straight, then one of the legs should be pulled gradually.
If they emerge to the thighs, encourage her to cough and sneeze her with a stick, and it will emerge.  If it does not emerge as described, then gently reposition the fetus bit by bit until it is in its natural position, then it will emerge smoothly. If it still does not emerge using all the methods described.
Take the juice of hyssop, the juice of fenugreek, ointment of shirg, and dissolved gum. Grind them well in a mortar. Then apply this mixture to the woman's vagina and lower abdomen.  Then have her sit in lukewarm water until the hips are submerged.
If you see that the lower part has softened, make her a pessary of myrrh and have her tolerate it. If she holds the pessary for an hour, have her sit on a chair, then make her cough and close her mouth and nose, and gently press on her lower abdomen. The fetus will emerge immediately.  If the fetus emerges on its knees and hands...
If it emerges in this manner, gently insert your hands a little bit. Then have the woman lie on her back, her legs dangling downward while lying on a bed, and her hands clasped together. Then gently press on her upper abdomen bit by bit. Then remove the pillows from under her legs so that her feet are elevated. If the fetus does not emerge, take both her legs and move them vigorously. Then press gently on the upper hips bit by bit until the fetus rises upward. Then the midwife inserts her hand and positions the fetus gently.
Instruct the woman to strain until the fetus emerges. The fetus emerges sideways, with one arm dangling.  Try to reposition the arm. If you cannot reposition it, have the woman stand up and walk.  If she cannot walk, have her lie down.
Shake her legs vigorously. Then use the poultice I described, made from saliva. If it is expelled, have her sit on a chair and try to reposition the arm and straighten the fetus. Have the woman cough and instruct her to strain until it emerges.
The fetus emerges on its back, arms outstretched, face toward the mother's back. The midwife should grasp the fetus's arm and gently turn and straighten it.  Once straightened, apply the grease and saliva we described to the area.
Then instruct her to move slightly, and when she does, have her sit on a chair.  Instruct her to lean to her right side, then to strain and cough with the instrument.  If this is done, the fetus will emerge upright on its side.
If it descends into the cervix in this condition, with the placenta, cut the placenta with your fingernail until it is free of moisture.  If it is attached to the fetus's face or neck, cut it from the umbilical cord to prevent suffocation and death.  Then push the fetus inwards to the proper position.
Instruct the woman to strain repeatedly, for this will cause easy expulsion.  For the delivery of twins or multiple fetuses, know that twins are common, and triplets or quadruplets are sometimes born alive, but this is rare; a quintuplet is unnatural and will not survive.
They are surrounded by a single placenta, even if numerous, and are separated by a membraneous partition connected to the umbilical cord of each.  They may emerge like a single fetus, a natural and easy expulsion, or they may emerge as a difficult expulsion as previously described.
If any of them are difficult to deliver and take on the unnatural forms we have mentioned, treat them as previously described.  Let the midwife be gentle, acting with care and patience, and employing any resource available to ensure safety.
Fetuses that can be conceived in the womb may be one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, or more than ten.  It has been confirmed that one woman miscarried seven and another fifteen, all conceived.
By the estimation of the all-knowing and all-powerful God, this all should be in the knowledge of the midwife, as she may encounter such a case some day and deal with it appropriately.
Chapter Sixty-Seven.
In the removal of the dead fetus.
If, after treating the dead fetus with the treatments described in the section, it does not come out and you are forced to use your hands, you must examine the woman. If she is strong and has no symptoms that cause concern of harm, the woman should lie on her back on a bed.
Her head should be tilted downward, and her legs elevated. A servant should hold her firmly from both sides, or tie her to the bed, to prevent her body from moving when the fetus is extracted.
Then, the mouth of her uterus should be moistened with moist ointments, along with the saliva of the [a certain type of bird], fenugreek, and linseed. Then, the midwife should anoint her hand with these ointments and saliva, then gently insert it into the uterus.  Thereafter, she should seek a place to insert hooks into the fetus and the appropriate areas for this.
Examine, and if the fetus is positioned to descend headfirst, insert the hooks into its eyes, nape, mouth, palate, under its chin, collarbone, or in areas near the ribs or beneath the clavicles.
And if it is descending feet first, then in the pubis and the middle ribs.  One should hold the hook in the right hand, and curl it between the fingers of the left hand.  The hand should be gently inserted and a hook planted in one of the aforementioned locations.
Until it reaches an empty space, then insert another or a third hook, if necessary, to ensure the pulling is balanced and does not lean to one side.  Then, the pulling should be even and not simply in a straight line, but moving to all sides.
To facilitate its exit, as is done with a tooth when extracted.  And in between, the pulling should be relaxed.  If it becomes lodged in one place, then the midwife should anoint some fingers with the ointment and insert them to rotate what is obstructed.  If some part of the fetus is expelled, then move the hooks to other locations.
She should slightly elevate it to do this until the entire fetus is expelled.  If a hand emerges before the rest and cannot be returned due to its constriction, then a cloth should be wrapped around it, and it should be pulled out until, when the whole hand is out, it is severed.
Either at the wrist joint or at the elbow.  Similarly, one should do this with the other hand and the legs. If the fetus's head is large and faces pressure during its exit or if there is accumulated water in its head, then a pointed, sharp instrument should be inserted between the fingers.
With it, the head is split open to release the water, or it is punctured with the instrument called the *mashdak*, the image of which appears with the images of other instruments in the subsequent section.
Likewise, if the fetus has a naturally large head, one must split the skull or puncture it with the *mashdak*, as we said, and then remove the bones with the forceps. If the head emerges and becomes constricted at the collarbone, then one should split it open to release the moisture within the chest.
Then the chest will contract. If the chest does not contract and cannot be removed, cut as much as you can.  If the lower abdomen is swollen or contains a tumor, it should be incised to allow the moisture to drain out.
If the fetus is on its feet, pulling it and positioning it toward the cervix is easy.  If it is constricted at the abdomen or chest, it should be pulled with a cloth wrapped around your hand, and the abdomen or chest should be split open to allow the contents to drain.
If all the other organs have been removed and the head remains lodged, the midwife should insert her left hand. If the cervix is open, she should insert her hand into the neck of the uterus, locate the head, and pull it with her fingers towards the cervix. Then she should insert a hook or two hooks and pull it with them.
If the cervix has contracted around a hot tumor, it should not be forced.  Instead, moist, fatty substances should be applied, bandages used, and she should sit in relaxing, moist water.
If the fetus is on its side, if possible, use the methods described for a living fetus. If not, it should be cut open and removed.  Nothing of the placenta should be left inside at all.  After finishing your treatment, use the methods described for hot tumors that appear in the wombs.
If bleeding occurs, she should descend into binding waters and use all other treatments.  I have seen a woman who conceived, then had a fetus die within her, and later conceived again.
Then the other fetus also died.  After a long time, a tumor appeared on her abdomen, swelled until it opened, and discharged pus.  I was summoned to treat her and treated her for a long time, but the wound did not heal.  I then applied some powerful, astringent ointments.
Then a bone came out of the area, and after a few days, another bone emerged. I was amazed by this, as the abdomen has no bones. I concluded that they were from the bones of the dead fetus.  I probed the wound and removed many bones from the fetus's skull.
I continued doing this until I removed many bones, and the woman was in excellent condition.  She lived for a time, with a small amount of pus discharging from the area. I have included this unusual case here.
Because it contains knowledge and assistance for what the skilled physician attempts with his hands in treatment.
Chapter Seventy-seven.
It contains images of the instruments needed for removing the fetus.
Image of a spiral for opening the cervix.
This binder, with which books are bound, has two spirals at the ends of the planks, but this spiral should be gentler than the binder and made either of ebony or boxwood. The width of each plank should be the thickness of two fingers.
Their edges are about a finger's width, and their length is one and a half spans.  In the middle of the planks, there are two pegs of the same wood, fastened to them.  Their length is half a span or a little more, and their width is about two fingers or a little more.  These pegs.
They are the two that enter the cervix to open it when you turn the spirals; an image of another tool for the same purpose, gentler and lighter.
Made of ebony or boxwood, shaped like tongs, but with two pegs at the end, as you see.  The length of each peg is about a span, and their width is two fingers. If you wish to open the cervix with it...
Then place the woman on a couch, with her legs spread apart.  Insert these two pegs, together, into the cervix.  While holding the end of the instrument between her thighs, then open your hand with the instrument, as you would with tongs, to the extent that you want to open the cervix. Until the midwife achieves what she desires; an image of another spiral mentioned by the ancients.
Image of the instrument used to expel the fetus.
Image of the instrument used to crush the fetal head.
It resembles a pair of scissors, with teeth on the tip, as you see.  It can also be made into a rectangular shape, like tongs, with teeth like a saw's teeth, to cut and separate.

Image of a hook.
