Image of two broad dissecting instruments for cutting the fetus.
All these instruments, the more varied their types and the more prepared they are by the craftsman, the faster his work and the higher his standing amongst people.  Therefore, do not despise any instrument for having it prepared, for you will inevitably need it.
Chapter seventy-eight.
In the removal of the placenta.
If the placenta is retained after childbirth, it is necessary to instruct the woman to hold herself, then to make her sneeze with a cupping instrument, and to grasp your hand over her mouth and nostrils. If it comes out in this way, then do so; otherwise, take a certain amount.
Pierce a hole in the cover and place in it herbs that open the cervix, such as fennel, dill, parsley, chamomile, rue, and centaury, or some of these herbs.  Soak them with water and heat on the fire.  Then place a silver tube on the hole of the pot's cover, and the other end in the mouth of the uterus.
Hold it until the steam reaches the bottom of the uterus, then make her sneeze as we said, and the placenta will quickly come out.  If it remains after this treatment and does not come out, instruct the midwife to dip her left hand in the grease of the she-camel or the saliva of the horned viper.
Then, she should insert it into the vagina and search for the placenta. If she finds it, she should grasp it and pull it out slowly, little by little. If it is stuck deep in the uterus, insert the hand as we described, until she finds the placenta, and then pull it out slowly, gently.
So that the uterus does not fall when pulled too hard, it should be gently moved sideways, left and right. Then, the amount of pulling should be increased.  This will then respond and become free from the adhesion. If the cervix is closed, we have described treatment with sneezing, the pot remedy, and the herbs.
If it does not come out with all the methods described, be careful not to use force when pulling again. Instead, tie what has come out of it to the woman's thigh. Then, inject her with the four-component ointment.  It will cause suppuration after a few days.
It will dissolve and come out. However, if it becomes suppurated, a foul odor will rise from it to the stomach and head, harming the patient.  Therefore, the appropriate fumigation should be used.
Therefore, some early practitioners tried fumigation with incense, and dried figs.  The image of the apparatus with which the woman was fumigated during retention of menses and the placenta, and similar conditions, is as follows:
It is made of lead, resembling a funnel, or made of copper. The narrow end is placed at the vulva, and the wide end on the fire. Incense is placed on the embers, held until it is gone, and then more is placed.
Chapter Seventy-Nine.
In the treatment of the unperforated rectum.
Many boys are born with an unperforated rectum, often blocked by a thin membrane.  The midwife should pierce this membrane with her finger, or if not, use a sharp scalpel to dissect it, being careful not to touch the muscle.
Then apply wool soaked in wine and oil, and treat with ointments until healed.  If you fear it will close up again, insert a lead tube into the opening for many days, removing it when the child wishes to defecate.
The rectum may also be blocked by a scar from a wound or a tumor.  In this case, the scar tissue should be incised, and treated with the remedies mentioned previously, and with cauterization.
Chapter Eighty.
In treating warts that occur on the lower part of the body.
Warts that occur on the lower part of the body are a complex, thickened growth near the anus, externally or in the space on one side. They can be one or more in number.
If this thickened growth becomes chronic, it may open and discharge a watery, whitish fluid or thin pus.  Some of these warts may penetrate into the rectum and bowels.  Those that do not penetrate may be identified by the discharge of stool and flatulence when using the enema.
And worms may also be discharged from them.  Some warts, if in the space and penetrating to the bladder and urethra, may also penetrate to the hip joint and tailbone.  A wart that penetrates the rectum is also identified from the one that does not penetrate.
Insert your index finger into the rectum and insert a thin probe of copper or iron into the wart, if the wart is not winding.  If it is winding, insert a thin lead probe or a horsehair.
Until you feel the probe or hair in your finger. If you do not feel it at all, and nothing of stool, wind, or worms emerges from the opening, as we have said, know that it is not penetrative.  Proceed with treatment immediately.
If the wart penetrates the bladder or urethra, the sign is the discharge of urine from it and the failure of the area to heal with medications.  If it penetrates the hip joint or tailbone, the sign is the probe reaching there.
If it is not winding toward the bone, with pain on one side, and constant pus discharge, and if it does not respond to treatment and does not heal with ointment, then these penetrating warts will never heal.
Treatment is futile for those who attempt to treat them by the ignorant physicians.  As for the non-penetrating, non-chronic ones, healing is hoped for with iron, as I describe and as I have experienced.  The patient should lie on their back before you.
Raise the patient's legs up, and angle their thighs toward their abdomen.  Then, insert the copper or lead probe into the wart, if there is a winding path, to ascertain where the probe ends. If the patient feels it near the anus, then you should insert your index finger into the anus.
If you feel the probe has penetrated through your finger, exposed without feeling any skin or flesh between your finger and the probe, then know for certain that it is penetrating, and do not exhaust yourself with it, for there is no healing from it, as we have said.
They say that it heals in some people rarely.  Of the treatments that may be helpful, heat a thin cautery according to the size of the wart, as previously mentioned, and insert it into the wart until it reaches the vicinity of the anus, then repeat it two or three times.
So that you know that all the excess, compacted flesh, resembling a bird's feather tube, has been burned.  Then treat it with a wick moistened with clarified butter until the burned flesh comes out.  Then treat it with healing ointments, if it heals.
Otherwise, it will never heal with any other treatment.  However, if you insert the probe and it does not reach your finger in the anus, and there was a thick layer of flesh or skin between it and the probe, and you see the wart close to the skin's surface, then cut the skin from the beginning of the wart. And as you make the cut with the probe, which is in the wart, until the cut reaches where the tip of the probe ends, the probe is removed and falls out.  Then clean out the compacted flesh, resembling a bird's feather tube, and leave nothing of it.
Then treat it with healing ointments until it heals. If bleeding overwhelms you and prevents you from completing the excision of the flesh, the best thing to do and the quickest remedy is to cauterize it with fire or a sharp medicine, because cauterization with fire combines two beneficial actions: burning the excess flesh.
And stop the bleeding and dry the moisture. Then treat it with a wick moistened in clarified butter or powdered sulfur mixed with oil until the area becomes suppurated and the burned flesh comes out in the pus. Then treat the wound with healing ointments that promote the growth of firm flesh.
These are the remedies that have a binding and drying effect.  If it is bound, it has healed and there is no need for a return visit.  If the wart extends deep into the anus and is far from the surface of the body, insert your finger into the anus and probe with it.
If you feel the probe and between it and you there is a barrier of peritoneum or flesh, and it is close to penetrating, then there is no action in it except by way of hope and expectation, that is to use one of three methods: either to cauterize it, as we said.
Or to cut it open until you reach its bottom so that you can easily insert the wick and treatment from close.  Do not continue the incision lest you sever the surrounding muscle of the anus, causing involuntary bowel movements. Then treat it as we mentioned, and perhaps it will heal as we said.
Or to penetrate that barrier and pierce it after cutting it open close to the anus, as we said, either with a probe or another sharp-edged instrument. Then clean away all the coagulated flesh that is in the incision as far as you can with thoroughness.
Then close the whole wound, including the incision and the upper opening, until it is sealed and the wart remains open inside the anus, which will be less burdensome for the patient.  The wart may heal in this manner.
If, when inserting the probe into the wart, it is found on the side of the anus near the body surface, along with the skin and the ring of the anus, then use a probe with a pierced tip, like a shoemaker's awl, in this manner:
And insert a twisted thread of five threads or thereabouts into it. Then insert the probe with the thread into the wart until it reaches the bottom. If it is penetrating into the edge of the anus from within, near the anus.
Then pull the thread out of that hole by inserting your finger into the anus and pulling the end of the thread. Join both ends together and tighten them, and leave it for one or two days.  Every time the thread cuts through the flesh, tighten it firmly until the flesh between the ends of the threads breaks and falls away.  Then treat the wound until it heals and is cured.
If the opening of the wart is not penetrating, then penetrate it as much as possible, but if it is very deep, do not do this because of the muscle, so as not to cut it. Then do as we mentioned above until it heals.
The image of the pointed lancet with which warts are pierced should have a very sharp curvature on one side and a blunt side on the other, so as not to cut anything unnecessary, as you see.
Chapter Eighty-one.
Concerning hemorrhoids that bleed, their cutting, and the treatment of fissures.
Hemorrhoids are of two types: either within the anus, resembling red sacs or like grapes, sometimes small, sometimes large, and blood constantly flows from them.
They may be single or multiple. Some are outside the anus, on its edges. However, those outside the anus are generally less moist; a yellowish or slightly bloody fluid constantly drips from them, and they are the same color as the skin.
Treatment for those inside the anus is to instruct the patient to defecate and strain until the anus protrudes and the polyps are visible. Then quickly grasp and remove them with forceps or a fingernail, cutting them at their roots.  If the forceps cannot hold them due to moisture and laxity,
Then grasp it with a rough cloth or a piece of sharpened wood, or pull it out with your fingers, and cut it. After cutting, sprinkle sharp powders over it to act as cauterization, or cauterize it as described in the chapter on cauterization.
Then treat it with further remedies until healed. If the anus does not protrude, administer an enema with a slight sting to wash what is inside the anus and encourage its rapid protrusion when the patient strains.  As for the polyps outside the anus, treatment is easy and simple.
To treat it, grasp it with your fingernail or forceps, cut it, and then treat it as previously described until healed.  If one dislikes cutting with iron, one should use a needle.  Take a threaded string and insert it into a needle.
Then pull the polyp upwards and pierce it with the needle at its base from the opposite side, wrap the ends of the string below the needle as it lies across, firmly pull the polyp, then tie the string and remove the needle.  Do this with all the polyps, leaving one unpierced.
To cause the excess blood to drain, place a cloth soaked in rose or quince fat on the anus, tell the patient to rest, and leave it until the polyps fall out. If the polyps fall out, treat them with ointments and other remedies until healed.
Concerning the treatment of fissures, they often arise from dryness of the stool and a blockage of the temperament. If they are chronic and no medicine is effective, it is necessary to scrape them with a scalpel or your fingernail until they become moist and the upper crust which prevents healing is removed.
Then treat it until it heals as it should. If it does not heal, scrape it more forcefully than before until it bleeds and peels, then treat it, and it will heal.
The Eighty-second Chapter.
On the treatment of ingrown nails, both ingrown and not, dry warts, and the pimple.
The ingrown nail is a round swelling, the color of the body, resembling the head of a nail, that can occur throughout the body, especially on the soles of the feet and toes, and causes pain when walking.
It is necessary to cut around the nail, and to grip it with a lancet or a hook and pull it out from its root, then treat it.  If you wish, cauterize it as described in the relevant chapter.  As for dry warts, each is a small, rough, hardened, round protuberance on the surface of the body.
They frequently appear on the hands of children.  Treatment involves stretching the wart, cutting it, or piercing it with a silk thread or a hair until it falls off.  If you wish, cauterize it as previously mentioned with fire or a strong medication.
Ingrown and non-ingrown warts can be removed with the instrument I have described in the treatment of the pimple.  The pimple is also a small, hardened, thick protuberance on the surface of the body, extending deep into it, and if cold affects it, it is felt as if stung by an ant.
It can be found throughout the body, and occurs most often on the hands.  Treatment involves taking a strong tube made from a goose feather or an eagle feather, placing the tube around the pimple so that the tube surrounds it from all sides, and then turning your hand with the tube until it breaks the skin around the pimple.
And it is placed deep into the flesh, and the pimple is pulled out from its root.  If you wish, you can do this with a copper or iron tube in this way:
The top of the tube should be smooth and flexible, so that the fingers can easily grip and twist it.  If you wish, cauterize the pimple as previously mentioned in the cauterization of warts in the chapter on cauterization.
Chapter Eighty-Three.
Images of instruments used for injections in ailments of the rectum, diarrhea, and colic.
The injector may be made of silver, tin, or hollow or hammered copper.  Small and large versions of this instrument may be made, depending on the users. Those used to treat young children, or those with narrow or painful rectums, should be very slender.  Image of a large injector:.
Its upper funnel, where the syringe is attached, should be wide in this fashion, with a barrier where the syringe is attached, as you see.  The lower end, which enters the rectum, should be very smooth and tight, with two holes on one side and a single hole on the other side.
The size of the hole should be the thickness of a reed, or slightly thicker. The syringe, in which the medicines are placed, is made from the bladder of an animal or the thin skin of a sheep, in the form of a small tray, with a diameter of a span.
It is pierced with numerous holes, as it rotates, with the distance between each hole being the thickness of a finger.  Then a strong thread of doubled yarn, of ten threads or so, is inserted into these holes, and the syringe is thus gathered together like a tray. If the medicine is placed within it, tighten the head of the injector between the stones firmly, then inject with it. This is an image of the circular syringe and the hole:.
The method of injection is to fill the membrane or bladder with the medicine, while it is warmed to the patient's tolerance.  Be careful not to make it too hot or too cold. Then the patient lies on their back and raises their legs.
A mat or blanket is placed underneath.  Then, the tip of the injector is lubricated with oil, or egg white, or licorice saliva, or the like.  Then, it is gently inserted into the rectum.  Then, the syringe attendant holds the syringe with both hands and squeezes it firmly until all that is in the injector is emptied into the intestines.
Then the injector is removed, and the patient lowers their legs to the wall. If the medicine prompts expulsion, let him hold on as much as possible. If he sleeps with it all night, it will be more effective in relieving congestion. If it is used for ailments of diarrhea, and expulsion is rushed, then repeat the injection until recovery.
If there are hemorrhoids or painful tumors in the rectum, the injector should be very gentle, smooth, unlike the injectors used for children. This is a picture of a gentle injector with its skin stretched tightly over it.
Chapter Eighty-Four.
In the treatment of wounds.
I have previously mentioned the classification of treating conditions similar to burns, and I am now describing their treatment here with both hands and medications in detail.  I say that wounds differ according to the nature of the injury and the location of the wound.
The causes of wounds are numerous, such as a blow from a stone, a cut from a sword or knife, a stab from a lance or stick or arrow, and other similar things.
And as for wounds according to the location on the body, such as a wound on the head, neck, chest, abdomen, liver, and similar organs.
I am describing the treatment of some wounds to make them a standard and rule for all wounds, and I will begin with simple head wounds in particular, because the complex ones will be discussed in the first chapter of the third book of this book.
I say that if a simple wound occurs on the head, and it is not a fracture, then if it is from a blow with a stone or the like, and only the skin has been bruised, and the wound is large, and you fear the onset of a hot swelling, then immediately lance the wound with a lancet on the spot. Do not delay this, and draw out blood according to its strength, if nothing prevents the phlebotomy, especially if the blood flowing from the wound is little. Let the phlebotomy be from the opposite side of the injured area.
Warn the patient against fullness, drink, and thick foods. If something prevents the phlebotomy, then he should reduce his food intake, avoid meat and drinks, and if a hot swelling occurs on the wound, apply a cotton swab dipped in rose oil alone or with a drink that has astringent properties.
If you fear a hot swelling, then apply to the wound, if it is fresh and bloody and the air has not changed it (as I will describe to you shortly), and if the air has changed it, apply some ointments.
Until the pus matures, then treat it until it heals. If the wound is large, and from a sword cut or the like, and its edges do not come together naturally, then bring them together by stitching as I describe in the stitching of abdominal wounds.
If the skin has exposed the bone and is hanging, and its adhesion is only in a small amount, then cut it, and then treat the wound with medications that will build strong flesh to replace the skin.  Instruct the patient to eat foods with strength, such as heads and similar.
If a putrefaction occurs in the skin and it is not attached to the bone, then cut out all the putrefied part and remove it, and then treat it.  If in these wounds there is an artery or vein bleeding and the bleeding does not stop with medication, then search for the artery.
If you strike it and it does not cease, then sever it with a lancet and tie it off.  If the situation demands it, and what we have mentioned is not effective, cauterize it until the bleeding stops.  If these wounds are small and superficial, their treatment is easy and simple; it suffices to apply this powder while they are bloody.
Before the air changes it, the preparation of the powder is: one part frankincense, two parts cinnamon, and three parts slaked or unslaked lime.  Grind all of them, sift, and fill the wound with it, and tie it tightly so that it adheres firmly.
It will clot with the blood, then tie it from above with bandages, and leave it without loosening as long as it does not swell. The medicine will not be removed until it heals.  The lime alone can accomplish this if the frankincense and cinnamon are not available.
This action can also be performed with lime in many large wounds, if its application is skillful and the wounds are fresh and bloody, and the air has not altered them.  However, if the air has changed them somewhat, especially if it is in the summer.
Therefore, there is no effective use of this powder; treat it with the remedies we mentioned, applying some ripening ointments or a poultice of barley flour made with water and honey, until the pus forms.  Then treat it with other remedies until it heals.
If a slight fracture of the bone occurs in the wound, extract it with a compress.  We have described the treatment of head fractures in the section on the neck.  If the wound is on the neck, there is no difference in treatment from that of a simple head wound.
If a nerve or artery in the neck has been severed, if it is a nerve, there is no remedy except to avoid treatments that cause strong constriction, like verdigris, alum, and similar substances, because they harm and contract the nerve.
Nothing cold should be applied to the wound at all, because the essence of the nerve is cold, and its connection to the brain, which is the noblest of organs.  Use remedies that are gentle, such as soaked, washed, fresh water-soaked nard, or rose-and-lead ointment.
If the wound is also washed with fresh water and other mineral stones in this manner, and apply moist poultices.  If the wound is large, use sutures or bring the edges together with bandages, then treat it until it heals.
If the wound has a cavity and pus has collected at the bottom, drain it at the lowest point. If an artery has been severed in the wound and you see blood flowing, tie it off or cauterize it if necessary.
If part or all of the cartilages of the windpipe have been cut, but the veins are intact, bring the skin edges together with sutures on the windpipe. Do not touch the windpipe, but smooth it and return it to its natural shape, then tie it tightly and leave it for several days.
Then treat it with astringent and drying ointments until it heals.  If the wound has affected the bone, probe it. If there are bone fragments, remove all or as many as possible. If you cannot, leave it until the wound becomes necrotic and heals, making removal easier.
If the wound is fresh, sprinkle it with the powder we described. If bone in the wound becomes necrotic and forms a fistula, treat it as described in the chapter on chest and shoulder surgery.
If the wound is from a spear or knife, and you see a cavity, look to see if air comes out when the patient breathes. If so, know that it is a fatal wound. If there is no cavity and it is fresh with blood, do not apply the powder immediately nor tighten it, lest blood be trapped in the cavity.
Otherwise, it will recede to the heart and kill the patient. However, apply a drawing ointment, and reapply it twice a day. If you do not have an ointment, place a cotton pad in the wound to absorb the moisture that comes out of it, and have the patient sleep with the wound on top.
To drain what gathers there. If three days or more have passed and the patient has no spasms, bad palpitations, or shortness of breath, and you see that all their other conditions are sound, know that the wound is intact. Treat it with bandages and other remedies until it heals.
If it cannot be seen and the pus is constantly discharged, know that it has become a fistula. Treat it as such.  The patient should lie on their wound so that what gathers there may drain. If the wound is on the chest or back, treat it with the mentioned stitching.
If it is large or small, if it has affected the bone and broken off fragments, examine the wound and immediately remove those fragments if they are loose. If they are not loose, leave them until...
The wound will fester, and then it will be easier to remove them. As for other injuries occurring in other parts of the body, the treatment is the same as we have described.
Chapter Eighty-five.
On abdominal wounds, intestinal prolapse, and its repair.
A wound that appears on the abdomen may be large, small, or medium. A large wound may expose one or several intestines, making their repositioning and suture more difficult and challenging.
A small wound can also present difficulties in another way, making its repair more challenging. If the intestines are not immediately returned to their proper position, they swell and thicken, making them harder to reposition.  For this reason, the most favorable wound is the medium one, because the return of the intestines is not as difficult as it is with the other two types.
Know that there are four types of abdominal sutures. Two are general, suitable for abdominal and other bodily wound repairs, while the other two are specific, primarily used for abdominal wounds, though applicable to other injuries as well.
I will describe them to you one by one with explanation. If the wound is small and something from the intestines protrudes, its return may be difficult for one of two reasons: either because the wound is small, as we said, or because the intestines have been inflated due to the cold air.
If this is the case, it is necessary to warm it by dipping a sponge or damp cloth in lukewarm water, or water in which herbs such as iz-khar, su'adi, and sinbal have been boiled.  Apply this to the intestines until the swelling subsides.
A drink with a binding quality may also do this, and is better than plain water for resolving the swelling. If you see that the swelling has subsided, moisten the intestines with water in which khutmi or khabazi herbs have been boiled, as this will facilitate their return with greater ease.
If its return is still not possible after this treatment, make a small incision in the wound using a lancet for treating boils.  The initial treatment, if successful, is preferable to incision; only resorting to incision after all other methods have failed.  (Here is an illustration of the tool.)
Its one curved side is limited, and the other side is unlimited. The thin end is not as thin as a scalpel blade, but slightly blunted.  It is a tool resembling a small awl, as you see. If the wound is wide and the intestines have entered, then it is necessary that their return is to their natural position.
Its specific location, if possible, is best. However, if the wound is wide and in the lower abdomen, the patient should lie on their back with their legs raised higher than their head. If it is in the upper abdomen, the head and chest should be raised higher than the lower part of the body.
Likewise, if the wound is on one side of the abdomen, always make sure the side with the surgery is higher than the other side.  This is how it should be used in major and minor surgeries.
For minor surgeries, position it as best you can. Then, summon a helpful assistant who will hold the entire wound with both hands, bringing the edges together, and then expose sections of it to the surgeon one by one.
This is a common method of stitching. Take a needle or several needles, appropriate to the size of the wound. Leave about the thickness of the little finger at each edge of the wound.  Insert one needle without thread into the edges of the skin. Inserting it into the edges of the peritoneum (lining of the abdomen) beneath the skin from inside, so that the edges of the skin and the peritoneum are brought together, forming four layers.  Then, with a doubled thread around the needle, tighten it repeatedly from both sides until the edges of the wound are firmly joined.
Then, leave another space of about the thickness of a finger, and insert another needle.  Thread it with the same thread as the first needle, continuing in this manner using as many needles as required to close the entire wound.  The needles should be of medium thickness.
Because very thin needles readily cut through the flesh, and thick needles have difficulty penetrating the skin, therefore the needles should be of medium thickness.  The needle should not be inserted too close to the edge of the skin.
So that the flesh is not cut quickly and the wound reopens before healing, nor should the stitches be too far apart, so that the wound does not fail to heal. Then, cut off the ends of the needles so they do not harm the patient when they sleep, and make for them a protective covering of linen from each side, securing the ends of the needles.
And leave it until you know that the wound has healed. This type of stitching with needles is thus most suitable for minor surgeries, since one or two needles, or so, may suffice for the stitching.
As for the method of stitching for the common people, it is to gather together the four edges—that is, the two edges of the skin and the two edges of the peritoneum—at the same time with a needle that has an even thread of moderate thickness, and then, after the needle has passed through all four edges, it is returned from the same side from which it started.
So that the thread is interwoven from the top of the wound, making the stitches like the stitches used to secure bags of goods.  And place a space of the thickness of the little finger between each stitch. This type of stitching is known to all people.
With this stitching, a surgical procedure was performed on a man in his abdomen who had been wounded by a knife. The surgical opening was more than a span, and approximately two spans of the small intestine had protruded.  The wound was in the center of the abdomen.
After the intestines remained outside the wound for twenty-four hours, they were returned. The wound healed in about fifteen days, and he was treated until he was completely recovered.  He lived many years afterward, functioning normally in all aspects.  The physicians had previously judged that he would not recover.
Surprisingly, I did not treat it with any ointment, because I was in a location where there were no medications.  I used worn cotton on the wound twice a day, and I ensured it was washed with honey water until it healed.
As for one of the specific types of stitching, I mention it according to Galen's text: Begin stitching from the skin, inserting the needle from the outside to the inside.  Once the needle penetrates the skin and the muscle, continue in a straight line along the entire length of the abdomen.
Leave the edge of the peritoneum on the side where you inserted the needle.  Then insert the needle into the opposite edge of the peritoneum, from inside to outside.  Then, insert it again into this same peritoneal edge, from outside to inside.
Leave the edge of the peritoneum on this side, and insert the needle into its opposite edge, from inside to outside.  Along with inserting it into the peritoneum, insert it into the edge of the mesentery on its side, until it penetrates completely.
Then, beginning again from the same side, trace it along the edge of the peritoneum on the opposite side, and remove the needle from the skin near it, then return it to that skin and trace the edge of the peritoneum on the other side along this edge of the mesentery.
Remove it from the skin on its side. Do this repeatedly, repeating it until all the surgical wound is stitched in this manner.  The general description of this stitch is like that of a furrier's stitching for furs.
By stitching the peritoneum once from one side with the edge of the skin, and leaving it once from the other side, until it is empty.  As for the second, special type of stitching, also mentioned by Galen, this is his text.
It is that you stitch it in the manner some healers stitch it, by bringing every part to its matching counterpart naturally; joining the edge of the peritoneum to its other edge, and the edge of the mesentery to its other edge.  This is as I describe to you.
The needle should be inserted into the edge of the mesentery closest to you, from the outside, piercing it from the outside to the inside; leaving the edges of the peritoneum. Then return the needle and pierce from the outside to the inside of both edges of the peritoneum; then return it again.
And pierce it from the inside to the outside in the other edge of the mesentery on the opposite side. This type of stitching is better than the common, easy stitching previously mentioned. This is Galen's text. Some have said that stitching the abdomen with two needles can be successful. That is, to insert a single thread into both of them, beginning by inserting the needle from your side, piercing it to the other side. Then insert the other needle from the opposite side, piercing it to your side, in the manner of stitching sacks.
Know that if the rupture is in the middle of the abdomen, stitching it is more difficult than in other parts of the abdomen.  As for treating the wound, its treatment is the same as for other wounds.  If you find the wound fresh with its blood before the air alters it, replace the intestines, stitch it, and make it firm, then apply a medicated dressing.
If the wound has been altered by air, apply some of the ointments you have available until it suppurates, the sutures fall out, and the peritoneum and mesentery adhere.  Then treat it like any other wound until it heals.
If you do not have medicines available, apply to it, from when it begins to suppurate, old cotton.  Change it twice a day as I instructed you until it heals.  If the wounds are simple, you will not need any further treatment in most cases, unless you fear the wound will cause pain to the major organs.
You should dip soft wool in moderately warm oil or rose oil and place it around the areas between the groin and the armpits.  If he feels pain or foulness in his intestines, which often occurs, irrigate with a lukewarm, black astringent drink.
Especially if the foulness has reached the intestines and become a penetrating wound into the interior.  Know that what is thick from the intestines heals more easily, and what is thin from them heals more difficultly.
As for the intestine known as the jejunum, it will not heal at all from a wound occurring in it, due to the many and large veins within it, the thinness of its substance, and its proximity to the nature of the nerve. If the protruding part of the wound is the mesentery, and you have caught it promptly, return it according to how you returned the intestine itself.
If it has been a while and it has turned green or black, you should tie it with a thread above the blackened area so as to avoid a blood flow, for there are veins and arteries in the mesentery. Then cut what is below that ligament.
And you should hang the ends of the thread from below the wound, outside of it, so it is easier for you to pull it out and remove it when the mesentery falls and the wound becomes purulent.  This describes a wound that appears in the intestine. Now, if a perforation in the intestine appears and is small...
For it may sometimes heal in some people, as I have seen a person who had been wounded in the abdomen by a spear thrust, and the wound was on the right side of the stomach. The wound healed and became a fistula, from which feces and wind were expelled. I treated it, not expecting a cure. I continued to treat him until he recovered and the area healed.  When I saw the area had healed, I feared for the patient that some misfortune might occur in his belly, but no such misfortune occurred at all, and he remained in the best of health. Indeed, he ate, drank, engaged in sexual activity, bathed, and exercised in his service.  Some experienced practitioners have stated that if a small wound appears in the intestine, it should be treated in this manner.
One should take large, large-headed ants, then bring together the edges of the wound.  Then one places one of these ants, its mouth open, on the edges of the wound. When it grasps and closes its mouth, its head will be severed; it will adhere and not come loose. Then another ant is placed near the first one.
Continue doing this with several ants, according to the size of the wound. Then close and sew the wound. These heads remain adhered to the intestines until the intestines heal and the patient recovers without any harm.  The intestines can also be stitched with fine thread.
One takes the end of this thread from the animal's intestines after it has entered the needle.  Then, one takes the end of the thread from the intestines and cuts it, and ties a fine linen thread to it.  Then that thread is inserted into the needle. The intestinal thread is used to stitch the intestines, then returned to the abdomen.  This type of stitching with ants and intestines is only a matter of hope and expectation.
However, if the tear is large and extensive, especially if it is in one of the thin intestines, there is no remedy, and there will be no recovery at all.
Chapter Eighty-Six.
On the treatment of the common cold and nasal polyps.
Know that any wound or tumor that becomes chronic, longstanding, and ulcerated, without healing and continually discharging pus, is generally called a fistula, wherever it may be located.  And we call it a cold or a fistula.
It is, in fact, a hard, compacted, white mass, without pain, having a cavity like the cavity of a bird's feather.
Therefore, some have called it a feather.  Sometimes it is moist, continually discharging pus, and sometimes the moisture might stop. This moisture can be copious or scant, thick or thin.
I have previously stated in the classification of surgical conditions that any ulcer that does not heal and in which flesh does not grow is due to one of nine causes: either insufficient blood in the body, or poor quality blood, or the presence of hard flesh within the ulcer and on its edges that prevents the growth of healthy flesh. Or because the ulcer is excessively contaminated and dirty, or because the ulcer itself is foul and the material it discharges is poor in quality, or because the treatment is unsuitable, or because of a widespread epidemic disease in the locality, or because of a local affliction.
Or because the ulcer is excessively contaminated and dirty, or because the ulcer itself is foul and the material it discharges is poor in quality, or because the treatment is unsuitable, or because of a widespread epidemic disease in the locality, or because of a local affliction. As was observed in the city of Zaragoza, where the maturation of diseases is difficult and the healing of tumors is slow by nature, or because it contains one or multiple bones.  All of these causes, their signs, and their treatments with medications were detailed in the classification.
Here I must inform you of the treatment for fistulas and nasal congestion, which is done by manual means. Know that fistulas can occur in all parts of the body, some terminating in large vessels, arteries, or nerves.
or to the peritoneum, or to the intestines, or to the bladder, or to a rib, or to a vertebra of the spine, or to a joint of the complex joints like the hand and foot, and some lead near to a major organ and the like.
Those fistulas of this nature are among the difficult-to-heal diseases, which do not accept treatment and dealing with them is arduous and fraught with ignorance.  If there are fistulas that do not reach the locations mentioned, and I hope for and expect treatment for them.
If you wish to ascertain this, take a probe of copper or iron. If the fistula runs straight, probe it with that. If there is a bend in the fistula, probe it with a thin lead probe, since lead is pliable.
If it slants upon entry and bends toward the bend, if the fistula has many openings that you cannot locate with the probe, inject one opening; the moisture you inject will travel to the other openings and flow out.
Then, investigate in any way possible to determine if there is bone or nerve, if the fistula's bottom is distant or close, or if it is a single fistula with many openings.  Determine all of this to the best of your ability by inquiring and examining the pain when you touch the area.
And similar are the signs and causes of the tumor.  If you ascertain all of this accurately, then proceed to treatment with confidence.  It is then to look to see if...
If the fistula is visible, close, or in a healthy area distant from a joint, nerve, artery, vein, or one of the locations mentioned to you, then incise the fistula as described previously, and remove the hardened tissue, corrupted flesh, and excessive flesh from within.
And treat any flesh or warts that grow in its mouth, until it heals. If the fistula is deep-seated and is straight, then you should incise it deeply as much as possible and clean it of all corrupted flesh.
Then use finely twisted, sharp medicaments and push them into the fistula's bottom, which you can locate with an instrument. Do this repeatedly until the sharp medicine consumes any remaining corruption in the fistula's bottom.
Then apply ointments that promote healthy flesh growth until it heals. If it does not heal with that, cauterize it as previously described. If the cause of the fistula is bone, and you are certain of this, explore and incise it as I have instructed you, unless something prevents you from doing so.
If it is a vein, nerve, or a major organ, as we said, and the bone is exposed to you, and there is some corruption and blackness in it, then scrape it until its corruption is gone, then treat it with something that will heal it until it is cured. If it does not heal and the pus persists as it was,
Know that you have not reached complete eradication of the corruption.  Expose all of it again, and thoroughly scrape and clean it to the best of your ability. Then apply a dressing. If it heals, well and good. If not, know that the corruption lies in a deep, hidden place of decay. Expose it all again.
Thoroughly scrape and clean it with the utmost effort, if the corrupt bone is small and you can extract it, remove it with the finest forceps suitable for this.
If there are many bones, thoroughly remove them all; do not leave any behind. If any ligaments obstruct their removal, and cutting them poses no risk, cut those ligaments and the flesh, if any, and free the bones.
If you cannot remove the bones at that time, apply something to the wound to cause putrefaction, and leave it for some days until the flesh and ligaments around those bones are putrid. Protect the wound so that the opening you made during your work does not close.
Instead, if you fear this, apply a cotton swab soaked in powdered sulfur mixed with oil, or a cotton swab soaked in Egyptian ointment or one of the green ointments.  When these ligaments become putrid and the bones separate, they will be easier to remove and extract.
If it is a single large bone, such as a thigh bone or a similar bone, and only its surface has become corrupted, thoroughly scrape it to remove the blackness and corruption, then close the wound.  If a large portion has become corrupted, and the corruption has reached the bone marrow, it is necessary to spread the wound.
Cut it completely to where the corruption ends.  Then treat it until it heals.  I will tell you about a case of a man who had a corruption in his leg to provide an example and aid to your treatment.  This man was young.
About thirty years had passed, and he had pain in his leg due to a cause originating within the body, such that materials reached the leg and swelled into a large tumor.  There was no external cause.  Then time went on with the errors of the physicians, until the swelling opened.
A large amount of material flowed from it, and he was poorly treated, until the leg became rotten and had many openings, all discharging pus and bodily moisture.  A group of physicians treated him for approximately two years, but none of them were skilled surgeons.
Until they came to me. I saw his leg, and a large amount of material flowed from those openings. The man's body had wasted away, and his complexion had turned yellow.  I inserted a probe into one of those openings, and the probe reached the bone.  Then I examined all the openings.
I found that some of them connected to each other from all sides of the leg.  I proceeded to open one of those openings until I exposed some of the bone.  I found it corrupted, decayed, blackened, and rotten, with holes penetrating to the marrow.  I reported what I had uncovered.
I removed the corrupted bone, and I believe that there was no other corruption in the bone except what I had cut and exposed. I believe I had removed it completely.  Then I used joining medications for a long time, but the wound did not heal.  Then, I exposed the bone again, beyond the first exposure.
I found the corruption connected to the bone. I exposed what I saw of that corruption.  Then I attempted to mend it, but it did not heal or unite. Then I exposed it again.  I continued to cut the bone piece by piece and attempted to mend it, but it would not heal, until I had cut approximately a span of bone.
I removed it with its marrow.  Then I mended it with medications, and it healed quickly and recovered.  This repetition of work and incision was necessary because of the patient's weakness and low tolerance, and my fear of his death, as he frequently fainted from excessive vomiting.  He recovered completely.
Firm flesh grew in place of the bone, and his condition improved in his body. His strength returned, and he was able to manage his affairs.  He suffered no impairment in his walking that harmed him in any way.  If a bone had broken in any part of the body,
It is necessary to expose it in this manner: take a bandage and tie it to the end of the protruding bone.  Have someone pull it upward.  Then take another bandage made of thicker wool than the first.
Then tie it to the flesh beneath the bone, pulling its end to draw the flesh downward.  Expose the flesh in the area you want to expose so that the saw does not injure the flesh. Place a piece of wood or board beneath the bone from below, firmly.
For if you do this, the saw will not be prevented from cutting the corrupted bone.  The incision should be slightly above the area of corruption, so that there is no corruption within the bone's cavity, which would not appear on the surface.  This will prevent you from needing to make another incision.
If the bone is corrupted but not protruding, and its parts are joined together, with the corruption in the middle or in some part of it, expose the flesh from all sides. Then place the wood underneath, and make the incision from the side where the corruption is located.
Once you have finished the incision, make another one on the other side.  Let the incision be slightly away from the corruption, as we said. If the corruption is in a joint, cut the corrupted joint itself, and make an incision on the bone where it connects on the other side.
If the corruption is in the connection of two joints, there is no remedy except removal. If the corruption is in the hand or foot, the treatment is extremely difficult. However, you should scrape off the corruption, however it presents itself, and remove and cleanse it in any way possible.
By whatever means you can make it work, as long as you do not encounter a vein or nerve.  Know that the saws and chisels for cutting these bones are numerous, depending on the position, arrangement, thickness, thinness, size, smallness, hardness, and fragility of the bones. Therefore, it is necessary to prepare a tool suitable for each type of work.
Know that the work itself will indicate the type of tool you need, if you have long experience and knowledge of the arts of this craft, because the expert craftsman, having seen many types of diseases, knows well. He can thus devise tools for each disease, and I will illustrate for you a variety of tools at the end of this chapter.

Chapter seventy-eight.
In the severing of limbs and the spreading of bones.
Limbs may become putrid either from an external cause or from an internal one.  If you treat this corruption with medicinal remedies and the treatment proves ineffective, and you see the corruption spreading in the limb, nothing will stop it.
Then you should sever the limb where the corruption has reached, so that the patient is saved from death or from a great affliction worse than losing the limb. A sign of this happening is that the limb will turn black, as though fire had burned it.
Or it may rot after becoming black, so that the rot spreads to the adjacent limb and takes hold of the entire body.  Proceed with its amputation; likewise if the cause of the corruption is from the sting of venomous creatures, such as the sea scorpion, serpent, or centipede, and the like.
If the corruption or sting is in the tip of a finger, amputate the finger, and do not delay the spread of the corruption to the hand. Likewise, if it has taken hold of the hand, amputate it from the base of the forearm, and do not delay the spread of corruption until it affects the elbow of the arm.
Likewise, if it has reached the arm, amputate the arm at the elbow joint itself. If the corruption spreads and you see it reaching towards the shoulder, do not amputate the shoulder at all, for that would lead to the patient's death. Employ other treatments to the extent of your ability.
Likewise, do this with the leg. If the corruption has reached the toe, amputate the toe at one of the phalanges. If it has reached the foot, amputate the entire leg. If it has ascended to the knee, amputate the leg at the knee joint.
If the corruption has reached above the knee, there is no remedy but to abandon it and allow the patient to succumb to death. The method of amputating or spreading the limb is to tie a bandage at the lower portion of the place you intend to cut, and tie another bandage above the place.
One attendant stretches the lower bandage downward, and another stretches the upper bandage upward. You yourself expose the flesh between the bandages with a wide scalpel until all the flesh is uncovered, then cut or spread it.  It is necessary to place linen cloths on all sides.
So that the saw does not touch the correct place and cause the patient excessive pain and a hot swelling. If bleeding occurs during your work, cauterize the place quickly or apply some hemostatic agents, then return to your treatment until you are finished.
Then bandage the injured limb with a suitable bandage and treat it until it heals. I tell you an example of a case that occurred to a man in his leg, this very affliction which I am describing to you.  It was a blackening of the leg with a burning sensation like fire.
This corruption first appeared in his finger, until it spread to the whole limb.  The man, when he saw the corruption spreading through the limb and the intense pain and burning, cut it off at the joint, and recovered.
After a long time, this same corruption appeared in his index finger.  He came to me, and I treated it with the remedies I had on hand, after cleaning his body.  The corruption did not yield, and it began to spread to the other finger.
Until the corruption spread to the whole hand, and he asked me to cut it off.  I refused, hoping to be able to control the corruption, and fearing for his life if his hand were amputated because he was weak.  When he lost hope in me, he left for his home.
I was later told that he ultimately cut off his entire hand and recovered.  I have related this story to be a help in similar illnesses, and as an example for guidance.
Chapter Eighty-eight.
In the treatment of abscesses and how to inject medicines into them.
If a tumor occurs in some fleshy organ and the duration of the tumor extends to a period, and then it bursts or opens and expels all the contents that were within, leaving the area empty as if it were a vessel.
And the skin on it, like a tattered garment, has thinned, and was not so deeply corrupted as to affect the bone, nerve, or ligament.  Therefore it is entitled to be called an abscess, and is not termed a fistula unless the duration extends to the point that the corruption affects any of these organs.
Then it is called a fistula or a sore, and its treatment is to cut away all that skin, as it surrounds it, especially if it has thinned and become like a tattered garment, and you are certain that it is not adherent to the area due to its corruption.
If you suspect that the skin is still attached, and the corruption has not reached that degree, and there is still thick flesh on the skin, then treat it with an injection.  Observe the abscess; if it is large and the pus emanating from it has a foul odor, then inject it with the Egyptian medicine.
Which is vinegar, oil, honey, and verdigris, equal parts, mixed in a vessel and cooked over fire until the medicine thickens and reaches the consistency of honey, then take what you need of it, dilute it with water and honey, and inject it into the abscess.  Tie off its opening and leave the medicine inside for about two hours.
Then remove it by squeezing; repeat this for several days until the abscess is cleansed and the foul odor is gone.  You may also inject it with ash water, if you do not have the other medicine. This means take the ashes of the wood of vines or the ashes of oak wood and pour water over it.
Then filter it and inject it until you are certain that the abscess has been washed out. If the patient is not responsive to the Egyptian medicine or ash water, then inject it with water and honey mixed with some pulverized verdigris.
Or inject it with honey and drink mixed together, because honey cleanses and washes, and the drink adheres to the abscess, especially if the drink has a strong astringent and drying quality.  If you have done this repeatedly and are certain that the abscess is no longer corrupted, then inject it with something that promotes the growth of flesh within it.
For example, take some of the palm ointment and dissolve it with rose oil and a binding drink, or inject it with other remedies and ointments whose properties we have described in the section on ointments. If the opening of the abscess is narrow and the injecting tube cannot fit,
Widen it slightly with an iron instrument, or place a thread dipped in Egyptian ointment or Syrian ointment within it, so that it widens. Similarly, if the opening is also wide, bring together its edges with stitches, leaving as much space as necessary for the injecting tube without excess.
Also, if the opening from which pus is draining is elevated, then slit it at the lowest point so that the pus drains downwards, because if the pus is confined within the depths of the abscess, it prevents the flesh from growing there.
If you cannot slit the abscess downwards as desired, then try to position the limb so that the pus drains easily, as you find convenient, and make sure that no pus remains trapped inside.
As for the uniting remedies that are applied to the abscess from the outside, and how to apply them, take one of the uniting ointments that matches what you want, and spread it on a linen cloth; let the cloth cover the entire abscess. Then, using scissors, cut a hole slightly larger than the opening of the abscess.  Spread some soft ointment on another cloth, sized to fit the hole, and place it on top, slightly away from the abscess opening to facilitate the drainage of pus. Do not remove the larger cloth until later.
For several days, the cloth over the abscess opening should be removed frequently to drain the collected pus.  Note whether the pus is copious or scant, and whether it is mature or immature.
Also, examine the abscess itself to see if the patient feels pain, and if there is swelling.  If your treatment continues in this manner and you see the substance draining from the abscess opening is scant,
Know that the abscess has adhered, or is close to adhering.  Then, treat it again tomorrow or on the third day.  When you treat the wound, replace the cloths and renew the ointment.  If thin pus is discharged from the abscess after a long period, do not despair of its adhesion.
Then recovery is near, because some people experience slow tissue growth in their wounds, and others experience rapid growth.  If non-matured pus is discharged from the abscess after many days, know that the abscess has not adhered.  If the abscess adhesion is delayed,
Then, increase the application of drying medicines.  Let the medicines be moist in consistency, but dry in potency, like the palm ointment, if more opoponax has been added.  A particularly effective treatment is to use palm ointment.
It can be treated with rose-water and sprinkled with moderate-strength old wine, then mixed with it and used.  A similar process can be employed if you do not have palm ointment.  Honey, when boiled until it thickens, may be used instead, or take myrrh, aloe, and frankincense, grind them all together and sprinkle them onto the honey while it's on the fire. Then apply it to a cloth and bind it to the abscess, or apply the boiled, thickened honey, sprinkle the medicines onto it, and place the cloths over it and bind it.
Similar treatments can use long-stemmed fennel, iris root, chickpea flour, and wormwood, either singly or combined. Grind and sift them, sprinkle them on the described honey, and use it. If the abscess persists and does not heal with this treatment, know that there is corruption within it.
If it has affected the bones, or other hard nerves or ligaments, treat it like a boil as previously described.
Eighty-ninth Chapter.
In the treatment of the impacted nail, the injured fingernail, the removal of excess finger, and the separation of joined fingers.
The "daḥs" is a thick growth of flesh that develops beneath the toenail or fingernail, and sometimes on other fingers. If left untreated for too long, it swells into a hot tumor.
Or it may fester and decay, even consuming the nail's root, perhaps destroying it entirely, and the decay may even reach the bone, so that it gives off a foul odor and the fingertip becomes broad and discolored, turning livid. If you treat it with the methods described in the previous section, and the treatment is not successful,
then you should cut away all the excess growth from the nail with a blade, and then cauterize the wound.  Cauterization is very effective in this case. However, if the bone is healthy and the nail is also healthy,
and if the nail's edge has pressed the flesh inward, causing it to be poked and irritated, then a thin strip of material should be placed under the edge of the offending nail, lifting it upward, and the flesh should be carefully severed.
and apply caustic, burning medicines to the remaining flesh until it is all gone, then treat it with ointments until it heals.  However, if the decay has affected the bone, then you must cut away and remove that bone, as it will never heal as long as there is infected bone.
If you see decay and ulceration spreading along the finger, cut it off at one of the phalanges, as previously described, and then treat it until healed.  If the nail is struck or injured and causes severe pain, you should first apply blood-letting.
Then, using a sharp scalpel, split the nail with an oblique cut from top to bottom, and be careful not to reach the flesh underneath the nail, as this would cause the afflicted area severe pain and lead to excessive flesh growth at the site. Then treat the area with remedies that alleviate pain.
And as for the extra finger which sometimes grows on the hands of some people, it may be entirely fleshy, or it may contain bone in some parts, or it may have a nail, and some parts may grow from the base of a joint of some fingers.
Or it may grow on some of the finger's phalanges; those which grow on some of the finger's phalanges do not move, and those which grow near the finger joint may move.  Whatever part is purely fleshy can be easily removed by cutting it at its base with a broad scalpel.
However, if it grows from the base of the joint, treatment is difficult; therefore, avoid cutting it.  If it grows on the finger at one of the phalanges, one should first cut the flesh in a circular fashion, around the bone, then use one of those appropriate saws to divide the bone.
Then treat it until it heals.  As for the adhesion that sometimes occurs between fingers, it frequently happens, either present at birth, or resulting from the healing of a wound, burn, or similar injury.  It is necessary to sever this adhesion.
So that the fingers return to their natural state.  Then place between them a piece of linen or cloth soaked in rose oil, so that they do not quickly reunite; or place a thin sheet of lead between them, until the area heals properly.
Likewise, if adhesion occurs between a finger and the palm, sever the adhesion in a manner appropriate for the proper form of the limb.
Chapter Ninety.
In cutting the tendons.
Tendons are twisted, thick veins filled with dark, black fluid that occur in most parts of the body, but most frequently in the legs, particularly in the legs of runners, carriers, and laborers. First, it is necessary to use frequent vigorous rubbing of the body to remove the black fluid.
Then, bleed the affected person with leeches.  As for treatment with iron, there are two types: one is to cut and remove the black blood; the other is to slice the tendon and remove it entirely.  For cutting, first soak the leg in hot water.
Until the thick, murky blood dissolves.  Then, tie the patient's leg from above the thigh to below the knee with a bandage.  Then, cut the tendon in one, two, or three wide incisions.
Then, with your hand, remove the black blood from the bottom of the leg to the top, and from the top to the bottom, until you have removed as much blood as you deem sufficient, or as much as the patient's strength can tolerate. Then, bandage it and instruct him to avoid foods that produce black fluid.  Emetics and bleeding should be repeated.
When the veins are filled and harm the patient, as for slicing, it is done this way: shave the patient's leg if it has much hair, then have them enter a bath and soak their leg in hot water until it turns red and sweats, or have them exercise vigorously.
If a bath is not available to heat the limb, then cut the skin along the tendon, either near the knee or at the heel.  Then, open the skin with hooks and dissect the tendon from all sides until it is visible to the touch.
When it appears, you will see it red and shiny.  Once it is freed from the skin, it will appear like a tendon. Then, insert a probe beneath it until it is raised and exposed beyond the skin.  Secure it with a smooth, blunt hook. Then, make another incision near the first incision, about the width of three fingers.
Then, peel the skin from the tendon until it is exposed. Then, raise it with the probe as before and secure it with another hook, as you did at first. Then, make another incision, or several incisions, if necessary.
Then, dissect and cut it at the end of the incision near the heel. Then pull and dissect it until it emerges from the second incision, then pull it toward the incision above it. Do this until you pull it out through the third incision, the highest of all the incisions. Then cut it.
If pulling and dissecting does not work, insert a needle with a strong, doubled thread, tie it, and pull it. Insert the probe beneath it and twist your hand in every direction until it emerges, being careful not to break it.  If it breaks, dissecting it will be very difficult.
This will cause harm to the patient. Once you have completely dissected it, place wool soaked in a beverage and rose oil or other oil on the surgical site. Treat it until it heals.  If the tendon is spasmodic, with twists and turns in various directions.
If it is not straight, as we said, you should make an incision along each twist and turn. Then secure it with hooks until you have dissected the entire tendon.  Be careful when making the incisions not to cut or injure the tendon, for this will make its dissection difficult, and you will have wasted your efforts.
Image of the incision used for the tendon.
Image of the blind hook.
It should not have a curve like other hooks, nor should the tip be sharp, so as not to injure the tendon.  It should be thick and smooth in its bend, because if it is thin, it will cut the tendon with its thinness.  Rather, it should have the advantage of thickness, as we said.
Chapter Eighty-nine.
On the tendons of the civil body.
This tendon arises in the legs in hot countries like Hijaz and the Arab countries, and in hot, sparsely fertile lands.  It may also arise in other parts of the body besides the legs, arising from a festering that occurs beneath the skin.
As it occurs within the bodies of snakes and worms, and the festering of gourds and worms that develop between skin and flesh.  A sign of the beginning of this tendon's occurrence is that a severe inflammation occurs in the leg, then the area swells, and then the tendon begins to emerge from the location of the swelling.
As if a root of a plant or animal.  When its tip appears, it should be wrapped around a small piece of lead weighing one to two dirhams, tied, and the lead left hanging from the leg.
Whenever anything emerges from it to the outside, wrap it in the lead and tie it. If it grows excessively, cut off some of it and wrap the remainder. Do not cut it from its origin before all of it emerges, because if you cut it, it will retract and enter the flesh, causing a swelling, festering, and a bad ulcer in the area.
Therefore, it should be gently coaxed and pulled out little by little until its entirety is expelled and nothing remains in the body.  Sometimes, this tendon can grow to five or ten cubits in length in some people.
I have heard that it has emerged from a man to a length of twenty cubits.  If it breaks during your treatment, insert a reed into its opening and push it deep into the body until all the material within is expelled.  Attempt to heal the area with medications for several days.
Then treat it with the treatment for tumors.  This tendon may have many branches, especially if it appears in a joint of the leg or on the leg itself, creating many openings and branches emerging from each opening. Treat it as described in the classification and as previously mentioned.
Chapter Ninety-Two.
On the fissure on the worm generated beneath the tendon, called the affliction of cattle.
This disease is called in some of our countries "affliction of cattle" because it often affects cattle.  It is a small, single worm that develops between the skin and flesh, crawling throughout the body, both upward and downward.
Its movement from one limb to another can be sensed, until it pierces the skin at a spot, emerging and creating a lesion from the corruption of certain fluids, as occurs with worms, snakes, and gourd seed in the abdomen.
Its danger lies in its potential to crawl throughout the body, ascend to the head, and reach the eye, possibly rupturing it and causing its loss.  This frequently occurs.  Therefore, if you wish to treat it and extract it, this can only be done when it is crawling and its presence is sensed.
Therefore, you should tightly bind the area above and below it with a good bandage, then cut open the area and remove it. If it has burrowed into the flesh and you cannot find it, apply cauterization with fire to the affected area until it is burned.  Its greatest potential harm is the damage to the eye, as mentioned.
If you see it has moved to the head, near the eye, tightly bind the area below it on the forehead, then cut open and remove it.  The patient should be treated with laxatives to cleanse the body of corrupt fluids and should avoid foods that promote corruption.
Chapter Ninety-Three.
Concerning the incision of the disease known as the "nafar".
This disease, called "nafar" in our country, is a pain that appears in certain parts of the body and then spreads from one part to another.  I have seen it as I describe to you.
I was called to a woman in the countryside who showed me a slight swelling in the vein of her arm.  After an hour, I saw this swelling creeping up her arm towards her shoulder as quickly as mercury.
If it moves from one place to another, the pain leaves that spot and settles in the shoulder.  Then, after an hour, it spread throughout the rest of her body, even to the other arm, and she told me it was circling her entire body as I had witnessed.
I was astonished by the speed of its movement from one part of the body to another.  Before this, I had only heard of this disease causing pain to move from one part to another, but I had never witnessed it firsthand as I saw in this woman.
I attributed it only to the fact that the woman was from the desert, with a lean body and exposed veins.  This is why this moving pain was evident.  It should not appear in a similar manner in those who are wealthy, with soft bodies and hidden veins.
If you wish to treat it, and the patient feels the pain, if it manifests as we have described, then tightly bind above and below the affected area with urgency, and cut into it until the trapped wind is released and the pain subsides.
If you do not see it, treat it by shaking the body and remedies that expel and disperse wind, like rotten seed and the seed of the skabinge plant, and similar medications.
Chapter Ninety-Four.
On the removal of arrows.
Arrows differ according to their types and the locations where they strike the body.  Their differences by type include large and small, hollow and solid glass ones, and those with three or four angles.
Some have tongues, and some have splinters.  As for their location in relation to the body's organs, they fall into two categories: either in major hollow organs like the brain, heart, liver, lungs, kidneys, intestines, bladder, and the like. If an arrow strikes one of these organs, and the signs of death, which I will describe to you later, appear, then you should avoid removing the arrow, for in most cases, death will follow. If these bad signs do not appear and the arrow is not deeply embedded within the organ, remove it and treat the wound.  Signs of a head injury by an arrow piercing the bone and injuring the meninges include severe headache, stupor, and dizziness.
If these bad signs do not appear and the arrow is not deeply embedded within the organ, remove it and treat the wound.  Signs of a head injury by an arrow piercing the bone and injuring the meninges include severe headache, stupor, and dizziness. Redness in the eyes and tongue, spasms, mental confusion, vomiting, and possibly bleeding from the nostrils or ears.  Speech and voice may be lost.  A white, gruel-like substance may flow from the wound, similar to the watery discharge of meat.
If these signs appear, refrain from treating the afflicted person and removing the arrow, if it has not already come out.  As for the signs of an arrow striking the heart, if it is near the left breast, and you feel it as if it has lodged in something solid rather than something hollow...
The arrow may also move with a pulsating motion, and black blood may flow from the wound.  This may be followed by cold extremities, cold sweat, and unconsciousness. Know then that death is inevitable.  A sign of an arrow wounding the lung is a frothy blood discharge from the wound.
The blood vessels near the neck swell, and the patient's complexion changes.  He breathes rapidly and craves inhaling cool air. If the arrow strikes the diaphragm in the chest, it is usually near the small ribs, and the breathing will be heavy with severe pain.
He sighs and all the shoulder muscles move. If the arrow strikes the liver, intense pain follows, and blood resembling the liver in color flows from the wound. If the arrow strikes the stomach, undigested food may be discharged from the wound.
The matter is clear; if the arrow strikes the abdomen, causing festering and discharge of feces, stool, or ruptured intestines from the wound, there is no hope for treatment or removal of the arrow. If the arrow strikes the bladder, urine will be discharged.
If something protrudes from the wound and the patient's pain intensifies, know that he is doomed.  However, other organs, such as the face, eyes, throat, shoulder, arm, spine, collarbone, thigh, leg, and similar organs, usually survive the ordeal.
Unless the arrow strikes an artery or nerve and is not poisoned, I inform you of some observations about these arrows to guide your treatment.  An arrow struck a man's eye socket, at the root of his nose.
I extracted an arrow from the other side, beneath the earlobe, and he recovered without any harm to his eye.  I also removed another arrow for a Jew, which had lodged in his eyelid, under the lower eyelid. The arrow was mostly hidden, and I only retrieved the small tip which was attached to the shaft.
It was a large arrow from a composite bow, with a square, smooth iron head, lacking ears.  The Jew recovered without any further eye trouble.  I also removed another arrow from the throat of a Christian.  The arrow was Arabic.
It had ears, and I cut between the windpipes, as it had penetrated his throat.  I carefully removed it, and the Christian survived and recovered.  I also removed another arrow from a man's abdomen, and we thought he would die from it.
After about thirty days, and without any improvement, I incised around the arrow and carefully extracted it. He recovered and suffered no further harm. I saw a man with an arrow in his back, where the wound had become inflamed.
Seven years later, the arrow emerged from the base of his thigh. I saw a woman with an arrow in her abdomen, where the wound had become inflamed. The arrow remained, and her condition did not change; she experienced no adverse effects on her normal activities.
I saw another man with an arrow in his face, where the wound had become inflamed. He experienced little pain.  Many cases like this occurred.  I removed an arrow from a Sultan's commander, who had an arrow lodged in the middle of his nose, slightly to the right side.
The arrowhead disappeared entirely. I was called to treat him three days after the arrow struck.  The arrow wound was very narrow. I probed it with a fine instrument, but felt nothing.  He was experiencing a prickling sensation and pain beneath his ear, on the right side.
I hoped this prickling was from a part of the arrow, so I applied a dressing that promoted drawing out and maturation. I hoped the area would swell and the arrow's position become apparent, so I could open it up.  However, nothing happened to indicate that the arrow had reached that area.
I continued to apply the dressing for many days, but nothing happened. The wound eventually closed. The patient was hopeless about removing the arrow for a long time, until one day he felt the arrow inside his nose.  He told me, so I applied a strong, corrosive medicine to the wound for many days.
Until it opened, and I probed it. I felt the thin arrowhead that adhered to the shaft. Then, I continued to open the wound with that strong medicine, until the arrowhead was clearly visible. It took approximately four months.
Then, when the wound widened and I could insert forceps, I pulled and moved it, but it wouldn't come out. I kept trying various instruments until, one day, I grasped it with strong forceps, as their image is shown later in this chapter.
The physicians had determined that his nasal cartilage could not be repaired, but I repaired it. The wound healed and the patient recovered completely, unharmed by anything. I am telling you how to remove some arrows so you can use this as a standard and a guide for what I have not mentioned.
Because the parts of this craft and its details cannot be grasped by description or encompassed by a book; instead, the skillful craftsman compares the little to the much, and what is present to what is absent, and he devises a new action and a new instrument in unusual cases.
Concerning this craft, I say that arrows are removed from the members where they are lodged in two ways: either by pulling from the point of entry, or from the opposite side, which is the way out.
If the arrow is protruding into fleshy tissue, pull it out. If it does not come out at the time of its lodging, you should leave it for a few days until the surrounding flesh decays, making it easier to extract.  Similarly, if it is lodged in bone.
If it does not come out, leave it for days and try pulling and moving it each day, for it will come out. If it does not come out after days, you should pierce around the arrow in the same bone from every side with a fine awl until you widen the space for the arrow, and then pull it out.
If the arrow is lodged in the skull, penetrating one of the brain cavities, and some of the symptoms I mentioned appear, refrain from pulling the arrow out and leave it until its condition improves after a few days.
Proceed with pulling out the arrow. If it is deeply embedded and you cannot pull it out, use the awls around the arrow as I described, then treat the area until it heals.  However, if the arrow is hidden in a part of the body... And if it is not felt, search for it with a probe. If you feel it, pull it out with instruments suitable for the task. If you cannot reach it because the wound is constricted and the arrow is deep, and there is no bone, nerve, or vein in the way,
then widen the wound so you can get to the arrow and extract it. If it has two barbs, grasp them and remove all the flesh caught on them from all sides using any method possible.  If you cannot remove the flesh by breaking or twisting the barbs, proceed.
If you attempt to remove the arrow in any location, use tongs to twist it in all directions to extract it. Be extremely gentle so that the arrow does not break, making it harder to pull out. If you cannot remove it after some time,
leave it for a few days until the surrounding flesh becomes putrid, then try again. It will be easier at that point. If bleeding occurs, use the treatments we mentioned in the relevant section.  Be careful not to cut a vein, nerve, or tendon, and use any method possible.
In every way you can, remove the arrow gently, carefully, and firmly, as described.  When pulling the arrow, the patient's position should be the same as it was when the arrow entered; this is best.
If you cannot do that, use whatever methods you can.  As for an arrow that exits on the opposite side, either something will protrude from the wound, or you will feel the arrowhead near the surface of the skin.
Make an incision over it, keeping the incision large enough for the tongs to grip.  Then pull it out; it will be easier to remove.  If it's lodged in bone, twist your tongs in a circle until the arrow is loosened in the bone, then pull it out.
Otherwise, leave it for a few days and try again until it comes out. If the arrowhead is still attached, push it out with it.  If the arrowhead has fallen off and you want to use force, insert a hollow instrument into the arrow's shaft so that its cavity is inside the shaft's tip, then push it out.
If the arrow is hollow, push it out with an instrument that fits into the hollow, as this will make it easier to remove. If the arrow is poisoned, you should cauterize all the affected flesh, if possible, then treat it appropriately.
If the arrow is lodged in the chest, abdomen, bladder, or flank, and is close enough to be felt with a probe, and you can make an incision, incise, being careful not to cut a vein or nerve, and remove it.
