Then suture the wound if necessary, then treat it until it heals.  Image of tongs used for removing arrows:
Its tips are like the beak of a bird, crafted so that if they grasp an arrow or something, they will not release it.  They are made in various sizes, large, small, and medium, all according to the size of the arrow, the width of the wound.
Image of the hollowed-out cannon.
Image of the solid cannon.
This one has a solid tip, like a rod, to easily enter the hollow arrow and push it out.
Chapter Ninety-five.
On bloodletting.
Thirty veins are customarily opened in the body.  Of these, sixteen are in the head: the two pulsating veins behind the ears, known as the *ḥassisīn*; and the two veins on the temples, visible; and the two veins at the corners of the eyes, known as the *nāẓirīn*.
The vein rising in the middle of the forehead, the vein at the tip of the nose, the two veins in the neck, the two veins in the upper lip of the mouth, the two veins in the lower lip, these veins known as the *jayārak*, and the two veins beneath the tongue.
As for the veins opened in the arm and hand, there are five veins: one of them is the *qayfāl*, which is on the outer side; the common people call it the vein of the head.  The *ʾakḥal*, which is the middle vein, is composed of a branch of the *bāsiliq* and a branch of the *qayfāl*; the common people call it the body vein.
And the *bāsiliq*, which is situated on the inner side; it is also called the axillary vein.  The common people call it the belly vein and the arm cord.  It is located on the elbow, where it adheres, and it is the one that clearly appears above the thumb; and the *ʾasīlim*.
It is the vein between the little finger and the ring finger, with two branches.  In the leg and foot, there are three veins: one below the knee on the outer side, the second is the saphenous vein, located at the heel on the inner side, and the vein of the women, located at the heel on the outer side.
And in the other leg, three veins like them.  As for the two veins behind the ears, their opening is beneficial for chronic colds, migraines, gout, and chronic bad head sores, and their opening method is as I describe.
This is done by shaving the patient's head and rubbing the back of the head where the veins are with a rough cloth until it is well rubbed, then the patient's neck is constricted with a turban so that the veins appear behind the ears in the low parts of the head.  Then one probes these with a finger. Where one feels a pulse under the finger, mark the place with ink.
Then take a scalpel, which is known as a lancet, and insert it under the vein into the skin until the scalpel reaches the bone.  Then lift the vein and skin upward and sever the vein with the skin, making a complete cut.  The length of the cut should be the length of two fingers pressed together.
Or a similar amount of blood is drawn, then they are tied with ligatures and left until healed.  Their cutting and cauterization were discussed earlier in the book.  As for the arteries in the temples, their opening is beneficial for chronic migraines, severe headaches, persistent ophthalmia, and sudden, intense eye discharge.
Their opening, as I describe, is to constrict the patient's neck with a turban so that the arteries become clearly visible and their pulse is evident under your finger.  Then mark the location with ink.  Next, raise the skin above the artery with your index finger.
Insert the lancet beneath and lift the artery upward, severing it as you did with the other veins.  Draw the amount of blood needed. Then loosen the constriction on the patient's neck and place your finger on the artery for a while.
Then apply a bandage and ligature, tying it tightly from above.  Leave it until it heals. Their mention, cutting, and draining were previously discussed in the beginning of the book.  As for opening the forehead vein, its benefit, after opening the temporal vein, is for chronic facial ailments such as ringworm, sores, and chronic redness.
To open it as I describe, constrict the patient's neck with a turban until the vein appears.  Then take the instrument called the lancet, whose shape is shown here:.
Place the pointed end of the lancet on the vein and strike it from above with a mallet or similar object. Allow the blood to flow to the desired amount.  Then release the constriction on the patient's neck and apply a bandage until healed.
A wide lancet may be used, but it should not have a sharp point like other lancets, rather should have a slightly broad point.  Open it with a slight deviation, because the bone is close, and the lancet might break if it is thin.
As for the veins located near the eye sockets, their benefit is for eye ailments such as scabies, redness, and sores, and facial diseases.  The method of opening them is for the patient to tighten their neck with a turban, then open them while you stand over their head.  Make the opening slightly angled towards the length with a small, slightly broad lancet.
If the area has little flesh, the lancet might break if it has a sharp point.  Then drain the desired amount of blood and place a bandage over them.  Keep it on for one night, then remove it.  Opening the nasal vein is beneficial for high fevers.
Severe headaches and facial diseases like red sores on the nose, especially if chronic, are treated by this method.  The procedure is to have the patient tighten their neck, then hold their nose with your left hand and use a long, thin lancet.
Insert it into the middle of the nostril itself, between the nasal cartilage, in a straight line, because the vein is not noticeable there.  Blood will appear immediately.  You should use the lancet for a short time, drain the needed amount of blood, and then bandage it.  It will clot quickly.
As for the veins, opening them is beneficial for shortness of breath, the onset of leprosy, and melancholic diseases that appear on the skin's surface, such as black vitiligo, scabies, bad ulcers, and eating sores.  The method of opening them is to have the patient tighten their neck underneath them with a bandage, and the practitioner stand over the patient's head.
The patient sits on a chair, and the vein is opened lengthwise with a wide but gentle incision.  Then drain a moderate amount of blood, or as much as needed.  Do the same with the other vein, then remove the bandage.
The two veins are moderately squeezed so the patient does not suffocate, and left until the next day for the wound to heal.  As for the jugular veins, opening them after opening the cephalic vein is beneficial for mouth sores, gum disease, bad ulcers, cracked lips, and bad sores.
Those that are in and around the nose. The method of opening them is to have the patient sit in front of you, tighten their neck with a head covering, then turn their lips and look for the veins, one on the right side of the lip and the other on the left, and note their darkness.
Also, there are thin, dark veins around them, so cut them off completely.  If it is unclear and you do not know which they are, aim for cutting the largest and clearest ones.  Do the same for the veins on the lower lip.
As for the two veins beneath the tongue, opening them after opening the cephalic vein is beneficial for the constrictions that occur in the throat due to sore throat and mouth diseases.  The method for opening them is to have the patient sit before you facing the sun, lift their tongue, and look beneath the tongue on one side, and then the other, for veins, and they will be dark in color.
Open them and be careful, do not cut them deeply, for there are arteries beneath them; there is a risk of bleeding from these arteries.  As for the three veins that are commonly opened in the elbow, these are the ones that are opened by people in general, and there are two ways to open them.
Either with a wide, fragrant, or olive-shaped lancet, or else with a lancet of a knife-like shape, which is the incision [and this is its picture].  Picture of the wide, fragrant lancet:
It should be wide, as you see, to be suitable for opening hollow, full, prominent, visible, thick veins that contain thick, dark blood.  This is the image of the olive-shaped lancet:
This lancet is narrower and has a thinner tip, suitable for opening thin veins containing thin, yellowish blood.  This is the image of the incision lancet:
This incision lancet, which is suitable for incision, comes in various widths and thicknesses, depending on the width and narrowness of the veins as well.  One may infer other things from it, and it is well-known among artisans.
As for the jugular vein, which is one of these three veins, the benefit of its incision is that it draws blood from the ailments that are under the throat and neck, from the chest and abdomen.  The surgeon, when making the incision, should be cautious and be careful of what is below it; if there is an artery beneath it, and if he errs and increases the penetration of the lancet, he will cut the artery.
This will cause bleeding, therefore, its incision should not be made with a lancet for piercing, but rather with an incision by the lancet. If the jugular vein is not clearly visible, it should be avoided and one should turn to another vein or search for one of its branches.
Or you may open the arm's vein, for it is clear and you may cut it with the incision lancet, as we said. If you wish to open it directly, you should, before tying the arm, feel the area until you identify the pulse point and then mark it with ink.
Then tie the arm and cut the vein in an oblique incision with the lancet, as we said, and be careful that the blow is away from the artery. When you see swelling at the point you marked with ink after tightening the bandage, this swelling is the inflation of the artery. Avoid it.
If you see the blood during the venesection leaping like a young child's urine, and the blood is thin and red, know that it is arterial blood.  In that case, quickly place your finger on it for a long time, then remove your finger. If the bleeding stops, it often does.  Tighten the arm.
Leave it, and warn the patient about neglecting it. Let it be on a pillow and do not move it for days until it heals. If the bleeding does not stop and you are overcome, and no medicine is available at the time, cut the artery if it appears to you, as its ends constrict and the bleeding stops.
Or take a pistachio shell, split it and take one half.  Tighten it firmly over the vein with a bandage and splints until the next day.  If the bleeding stops, do so.  Otherwise, treat it with the previously mentioned methods of applying cauterizing agents to stop the bleeding.  Stopping the bleeding is not usually difficult in most cases.
Due to the small size of the wound and the effectiveness of the bandage on the arm, be aware of this.  As for the dark vein, the benefit of venesection is that it draws blood from the top of the head and the lower part of the body, because it is composed of a branch of the basilar vein and a branch of the cavernous vein, as we said.
The person performing the venesection should be careful of the location of the vein, for beneath it is a nerve.  If the lancet penetrates deeper and strikes the nerve, numbness will result, difficult to perceive, and possibly not heal at all. This nerve is often felt.
If in some people it is hidden and thin, not easily seen, then make your incision for venesection with a shallow cut to avoid the nerve as much as possible. If the vein lies between two nerves, cut the vein lengthwise.  As for the cavernous vein, the benefit of its venesection is that it draws blood from the head.
It is beneficial for eye diseases.  Specifically, if you wish to venesect this vein, make the incision with an olive-shaped lancet or a wide, fragrant lancet, as it is the safest of all veins, because there is neither an artery nor a nerve beneath it.
However, when performing venesection, you should avoid the muscle head with the lancet and seek the soft spot.  It will not harm if, on the first attempt, the vein is not hit, and it can be venesected again.  However, it may swell in some people if it is not opened on the first attempt.
But that swelling will not harm it.  As for the method of venesection, its symptoms, and what should precede its correction, first you should know that venesection is used either to preserve health only.
Or it is used in diseases.  The venesection that is used to preserve and maintain health, and to guard against the onset of illness, should be done in one of the three veins in the elbow, namely the cephalic, the radial, and the basilic veins.  And it should be done in the early spring, if signs of fullness appear.
The venesection should be performed on a Sunday or a Tuesday, after three hours of daylight have passed.  As for venesection used in illnesses, it has no fixed time; but whenever the need and necessity arise, whether during the night or day.
At every hour and every time.  Boys should not be bled until they reach fourteen years of age, nor should elderly men who have passed sixty years of age.  If anyone intends to perform venesection for any reason whatsoever...
He should cleanse his bowels before venesection with a gentle enema if there is a lot of retained phlegm.  This is so that foul impurities from the bowels will not be drawn into the veins during venesection and harm the major organs.  The feverish, the drunk, and the intoxicated should not be bled until this passes from them.
Also, beware of venesection following dysentery, vomiting, fever, excessive sexual activity, exertion, athletic activity, late nights, and fasting—and anything that weakens the body or mind.  Then, consider thinning the humours beforehand if the blood is thick.
With foods, drinks, and medications, if possible.  Then he should enter a bath if nothing prevents him, or engage in some exercise so that the blood is thinned.  His venesection should be performed in the middle of the day, as we have said, and he should attempt to clear his mind of all bad emotions that day.
Such as worries, anger, and fear, and from all bodily ailments such as excessive fatigue and exhaustion, sexual activity, and the like.  Let the things people habitually use for their enjoyment, such as fragrances, herbs, and amusements, be present in his session, for each person according to their ability.
Then the bleeder sits on a cushion higher than the one the person being bled sits on.  Then the bleeder holds the arm and rubs it with his hand two or three times, then tightens the bandage with the tourniquet and twists it two or three times.  The tightening should be moderate.
Because if the tightening is not moderate, either excessive tightening will stop the flow of blood, or if it is too loose, it will also prevent the flow of blood. Then, after the tightening, the person being bled should rub their hands together vigorously.
Until the veins swell and become obvious to the touch. Then the bleeder wipes the lancet with a small amount of old oil, especially. Then he places his left index finger on the same vein, slightly below the spot where he wants to bleed, to prevent the vein from moving and to avoid hitting it. Because some veins are like tendons, which move when lanced.  And some are filled with air, so when the lancet is placed on them, they recede beneath the lancet and deceive the bleeder.  The lancet does not open the vein. If it does open it, the opening is narrow.
Therefore, the bleeder should be cautious and careful in all these matters. Then he lowers the lancet. If the vein opens on that first try, well and good. Otherwise, he should try again a little below or above that spot quickly if the area hasn't swelled up.
If the patient swells or feels pain, leave it a day or two and do not tighten the bandage, as it may cause a hot swelling.  He should not enter a bath.  Then he can have the bleeding repeated, if desired. If the lancet is inserted and the opening is small and the blood flow is thin.
And fearing that not enough blood will come out, he should reinsert the lancet into the same hole gently and straight, enlarging the opening a little, and do this quickly before the area swells, for in many people the area will swell if the opening is small.
If you see it has swelled, do not re-treat it, for it will not help you. Apply some bruised oil to it. It will ease the flow of blood, and is better in this situation than the oil itself, and any other ointments.
Use bruised oil in all your bloodletting of veins when the blood flow is difficult.  This remedy, the 'Fārūq' and 'Shakhzana', may also achieve this if applied to the area. The blood will become thinner and more fluid if it is thick.
If a large swelling occurs at the site of the bloodletting, which often happens, especially for those who have never had bloodletting before or if the vein opening was small, quickly apply a sponge soaked in slightly warmed salt water to the swelling and bandage it for an hour.  It will resolve.
This should be done after the blood has fully exited the vein itself or another vein.  If, after a few days, a black or green discoloration remains, it will not harm. If desired, apply some dissolved gum and resin.
Or something from the juice of the Fūdanj and the like.  A swelling and lump often occur when bloodletting the basil, so place your hand upon it. If you feel it pulsating when you press it, this is a bad lump, so be careful not to apply any of the remedies mentioned.
For it may bleed arterial blood; but bandage it with something constricting to solidify the area, and then treat it with the rest of the remedies until it heals.  One should remove blood from each person according to their strength and the prevailing color of their blood.
If the blood is black, let it flow until it turns red.  Likewise, if you see it thick, let it flow until it thins, and if it is sharp, until its sharpness is gone.  It is necessary for those who are strong and full and need to remove the blood in one sitting.
To widen the vein opening, and the lancet should be wide.  For those who are weak, the opposite is true.  The blood should be drawn in stages and the puncture should be narrow.  The best tool for opening a vein is a slanted, oblique cut, not a piercing.
This type of bloodletting is safe from bleeding and from nerve damage. It is better and safer than bloodletting across or lengthwise. If someone faints frequently during bloodletting, it is necessary to give them something to eat before the procedure, such as bread soaked in pomegranate juice or sherbet.
If he is healthy and has had the blood drawn three or four times, and if he is cold-natured, then he should eat bread soaked in myrrh drink, or honeyed drink scented with fragrant herbs, or in a good herbal drink, before the procedure.
If fainting occurs during bloodletting and is caused by excessive blood loss, then he should be given meat broth and thin herbal drink.  Fragrant perfume should be used, and his chest should be rinsed with it.  All other methods mentioned in the section on fainting should be employed.
For those who have been bled to relieve vomiting, and if one wants to further release and thin their blood, then one who had much blood released through bloodletting, and whose strength is weak, should release the blood little by little, as much as their strength permits, over several days.
And if someone wishes to further release and thin their blood, and their body is strong, then they should do so after seven or nine hours from the initial bloodletting.  However, if one wants to draw blood from their body to the opposite side of where it has gathered, then they should be treated on the second or third day.
If someone has a lot of hot, intense blood in their body that has caused a fever, then blood should be drawn in one session, a large amount, and the opening widened until fainting occurs, after you have observed all the necessary conditions of bloodletting. And place your hand on his pulse while the blood flows, to prevent death instead of fainting, for this often occurs if the bloodletter is unaware and negligence takes place. This should not be done if you intend to release or thin the blood again.
If the vein's opening closes and blood flow is difficult, do not forcefully prod or twist it; this is very harmful.  Instead, either leave it until you can bleed it again, or use the lancet blade to remove any blood clots in the vein's opening.
Or apply something dissolved in water, like salt, or a remedy like the antidote or the 'shakhzana' (a possible herbal preparation). Gently prod it until blood emerges. If the vein has swelled, leave it.
Do not touch it until the swelling subsides.  If, due to necessity, you must bleed it again, then either bleed above that spot, or bleed the other arm, or another vein.
Bleeding the brachial artery is done instead of the temporal and occipital arteries if those aren't available or are hidden, as it is composed of both.  To perform the procedure, the patient should immerse their arm in warm water until the veins are clearly visible and the forearm is red. Then, a moderate bandage is tied somewhat above the vein.
Then bleed the vein slightly off-center, neither laterally nor lengthwise, and make the opening wide.  Bleed it slightly above the wrist joint. If the blood flow is difficult, re-immerse the arm in the warm water vessel, and let the blood flow into the water.
Until you have obtained your desired amount. If it is summertime, you may not need to re-immerse the arm in warm water.  Re-immersion is most often needed in winter. Bleeding this vein is safer than from all other veins. Because there is no vein or nerve below it.  Bleeding from the ulnar vein of the right hand is beneficial for liver ailments.  To perform the procedure, tie the wrist with a bandage or your hand after immersing it in warm water until the vein swells.
Because there is no vein or nerve below it.  Bleeding from the ulnar vein of the right hand is beneficial for liver ailments.  To perform the procedure, tie the wrist with a bandage or your hand after immersing it in warm water until the vein swells. The vein is very clearly visible.  Then bleed it slightly off-center.  If you sever it completely, it will do him no harm.  Be careful; do not use the lancet vigorously, as there is a nerve of the fingers beneath, and the area is devoid of flesh.  Then return the arm to the warm water.
Leave it [the arm] and allow the blood to flow. If you do not return it to the warm water, the blood will clot within the opening of the vein and cease flowing.  When you have extracted the required amount of blood, apply grease and salt to the vein to prevent rapid clotting.
Likewise, you should do this with every constricted vein.  Bleeding from the ulnar vein of the left hand is beneficial for spleen ailments.  Perform the bleeding procedure on it as you did on the second vein, identically.  Bleeding from the saphenous vein is beneficial for illnesses in the lower body, such as uterine ailments.
And for amenorrhea, kidney ailments, chronic ulcers of the thighs and legs, and similar ailments. The procedure for bleeding from this vein is to immerse the leg in warm water and apply massage until the veins dilate.  Then tie above the joint of the leg with a bandage. The vein is located near the heel, visible towards the big toe.
Many branches extend from it [the vein] throughout the leg.  Bleed it from the widest branch or at the heel where it joins, as this is the best and safest approach.  If you bleed it on the front of the leg, be cautious of the nerves beneath the skin on the front of the leg.
Make your incision with a twisting motion, as if you were intending to sever it, and have the lancet sharp. If the blood flow is difficult, immerse the leg in warm water and allow the blood to flow until it is exhausted.  If the bleeder misidentifies the vein on the first attempt.
If the bleeder misses the vein on the first attempt, then he should re-bleed slightly higher up. The area is safe, and there is no cause for alarm if you are cautious of the nerve, as we have stated.  Likewise, do this with the saphenous vein of the other leg. As for the femoral vein, its location, as we stated, is at the heel on the outer side.
Its benefit is for hip pain if it is due to hot blood.  The procedure for bleeding is to have the patient enter a bath and quickly pull the leg from the hip up to the heel with four fingers using a thin long bandage.  It will only appear that way.
If it appears, bleed it in any case, either twisting (which is best), or sever it or slit it, as the location is safe.  It is very hidden in most people, if you do not find it.
If it does not appear at all, bleed some of its branches, which appear on the back of the foot near the little and ring fingers. Be careful of the nerves and release the desired amount of blood.
Then release the bandage, place a cotton pad on the bleeding site, and bandage the area. It will quickly heal.
Chapter Ninety-Six.
On Cupping and its Application.
Cupping vessels may be made of horn, wood, copper, or glass. Cupping can be of two types: one is cupping with a mark and bloodletting, and the other is cupping without a mark.
And this cupping without a mark is of two types.
And cupping of the sides of the neck on both sides, and cupping of the chin below the lower jaw of the mouth, and cupping of the shoulders, and cupping of the coccyx on the tailbone, and cupping of the elbows which are the middle of the arms, and cupping of the legs, and cupping of the heels.
Cupping draws blood from the thin veins dispersed in the flesh, and for this reason, it does not weaken the strength like venesection.  Cupping, whether with or without fire, should not be used in illnesses resulting from fullness until the whole body is emptied.
If cupping is needed because of illness or custom, we use it at all times, at the beginning, end, and middle of the month, and at any time.
For some people, when there is an abundance of blood requiring removal by cupping, they experience heaviness and headache in the head, and some experience fullness, redness in the face, head, and neck, and some experience itching in the face, temples, and darkening and heaviness in their eyes, and some scratch the site of the cupping.
Some laugh excessively, some taste blood in their mouths, and gums bleed, and some spit blood. Some sleep excessively, and some see blood, redness, slain people, and wounds, and things similar to that in their dreams.  When we see any of these things...
Especially if it is in the middle third of the month, we then prescribe cupping after two or three hours have passed of the day.  The benefit of cupping at the neck is in relieving heaviness in the head and water flowing to the eyes.
However, it is necessary that this be done after the body has been emptied.  This cupping may be a substitute for a venesection of the temples.  One should beware of using it for those with a cold head or a cold, as it will harm them greatly.
Therefore, it is not appropriate for the elderly to use it, nor those with cold diseases in their heads.  Those who overuse it will develop forgetfulness.
Therefore, the cupper should lower the cup slightly to avoid the development of forgetfulness.  Cupping of the shoulder is a substitute for venesection of the temples and the veins of the neck.
Therefore, it is beneficial for asthma, shortness of breath, a splitting of the respiratory apparatus, coughs, and fullness.  The cupping of the shoulder should be raised slightly, because if it is positioned lower, it will cause weakness in the heart and stomach.
Cupping of the temples is beneficial for pains in the head, ophthalmia, migraine, constriction, and toothache. It is a substitute for venesection of the neck veins. The cupper should be instructed not to press too deeply with the instrument lest it sever an artery and cause bleeding.
Cupping under the chin is beneficial for sores in the mouth, gum problems, and similar mouth ailments.  It can substitute for venesection of the vessels in the lips. Cupping of the shoulders is beneficial for palpitations caused by fullness and heat.
Cupping of the inner forearms is beneficial for what the venesection of the three veins (the basilic, the temporal, and the cephalic) is beneficial for, because it draws blood from all those fine veins in the flesh, and draws those fine veins from other thicker veins, until the drawing reaches the three thick veins.
The cupper should be instructed not to press too hard, because the area is devoid of flesh and has nerves and arteries underneath.  A single cupping treatment on the coccyx is beneficial for hemorrhoids and sores of the lower rectum.
The cupper should be instructed to use a large, copper cupping vessel, because the area requires strong suction, and glass cupping vessels may break.  A great deal of pressure should be applied. Cupping of the legs significantly reduces fullness.
Because it draws blood from the entire body, it is beneficial for chronic pains in the kidneys, uterus, and bladder. It stimulates menstruation and is beneficial for boils and pimples. It substitutes for venesection of the safenous and saphenous veins. However, it greatly weakens the body, and fainting occurs in most people. Cupping of the saphenous veins is similar in benefit to cupping of the legs.
How to apply cupping: First, place the empty cupping vessel and apply moderate suction. Do not prolong the application of the cupping vessels, but rather apply and remove them quickly to allow the humors to be drawn to the area evenly. Continue to repeat this until the area becomes red and opens.
When the redness of blood appears, then apply pressure and resume suction gradually.  Then, observe the condition of the bodies.  For those with thin, porous flesh, a single application of pressure should suffice, so that the area is not ulcerated.
The cupper should widen the pressure, deepening it slightly, and adjusting the suction gently and with a light movement. If the blood is thick, it should be pressured twice.  The first time, to open a passage for the thin, watery blood; the second, to extract the thick blood.
If the blood is very murky, pressure should be applied a third time to achieve the goal. In general, if we want to remove a small amount of blood, one pressure suffices.  If we want to remove a lot of blood, we apply more pressure, especially if the blood is thick.
The pressure should be deep, but the moderate limit of pressure is only the depth of the skin.  What ointments should be used when applying cupping, and what waters, and what should the cupper be wary of?  For those with thick, hard, dry skin and narrow pores...
The areas for the cupping should be anointed with opening, softening, and dissolving ointments.  In summer, examples include the ointment of the Khariri, or the ointment of the violet, or the ointment of sweet almond, or the ointment of pumpkin seeds.
In winter, examples include the ointment of the narcissus, or the ointment of the lily, or the ointment of chamomile, or the hyacinth, and the like.  If the impurity is thick and cold, let the ointment be the ointment of marjoram, or the ointment of balm, or the ointment of frankincense, or the ointment of fennel, and the like.
If the cupper has wide pores and soft flesh, then ointment should be avoided.  These individuals should wash their cupping vessels after the procedure with rosewater, or cold water, or water from wild grapes, or water from pumpkin, or water from the root of the plant called *rajla*, and the like.  And, as for those whose blood is very moist...
They should wash their cupping vessels with vinegar, or with rue and sumac water, and the like.  As for those whose impurities are thick, they should wash their cupping vessels with aged wine, or marjoram water, or fennel or chamomile decoction, and the like.  Cupping should be avoided in the bath and after the bath.
Rather, it should be used one or two hours after leaving the bath.  One should not sleep after cupping.  This is what the cupper and the leeches should arrange before and after cupping.
First, one should consider if the cupper or leech is bilious, and if their blood is predominantly sharp and inflammatory.  Then they should take cooling agents like pomegranate, chicory with vinegar, lettuce, and orgeat, and the like.  Their food should be fowl, lamb, and various lean meats.
And whoever's temperament is cold should be given honeyed drinks, drinks made with *mayba* or *sakenjebeen* with seeds, and medium-aged aromatic wine, which is between old and new.  A small amount of food should be prescribed, and their diet should consist of fowl, quails, sparrows, and doves, well-cooked.  On the day of cupping and leeching, drinks should be more plentiful than food.
Sometimes, certain people may be given some of the Faruq theriac, musk medicine, or shiltha before cupping and before or after leeching, to strengthen the major organs and thin the blood.  However, feverish individuals should not be given it.
As for cupping without stipulations, these are the cuppings applied to the liver, spleen, breasts, abdomen, navel, kidney area, and groin.  Because these organs cannot withstand stipulations, the purpose is either to draw blood from one organ to another.
For example, placing a cupping on the breasts in cases of epistaxis, or to remove a cold wind that has lodged in an organ, such as placing a cupping on the abdomen and navel.  It loosens the organ, warms it, and removes the pain by dissolving that wind.
Cupping may also be placed on the kidneys if a blockage or stones are present, as its strong suction might open the blockage or dislodge the stones.  Similarly, it is used on the liver and spleen when wind is present, causing them to become agitated.  These cuppings may also be used, empty, by suction alone.
It may be used with fire, or filled with lukewarm water in cases of hemorrhoids.  A large cupping is filled with hot water alone, or water in which some suitable herbs have been boiled.
Then, filled, it is placed on the affected area, held, and removed, repeated as needed.  This is the image of a cupping that is used with fire.
Its mouth opening is the width of two open fingers, as depicted.  Its depth is half a span.  Near the middle of one side, there is a small hole that a needle can enter.  It is made of thick-rimmed Chinese or brass.
It is smooth, flat, and polished so as not to injure the organ when placed upon it.  In the center, a transverse brass or iron bar is present, on which the candle is placed for burning.  This cupping can be made larger or smaller than described, depending on the illnesses.
The practitioner's skill is necessary, for cupping for children and thin people is not the same as for men and those with larger bodies.  As for how to apply this cupping with fire to the affected area, you light a wick of tightly wound cloth or a small candle made of pitch, and place it on the center of the solidified bar in the center of the cupping.
So that the fire rises upwards toward the bottom of the cupping, to prevent the patient's body from burning.  Then it is placed on the affected area, and the finger is placed in the hole we mentioned, so that when we hold the cupping, we do not need to remove the finger.
Steam will escape through that hole, and the cupping will loosen at the spot. Then the wick is re-lit according to the procedure, and repeated if necessary. As for the cupping used in cases of dropsy, it does not have a solidified bar or a hole.
Instead, it is used by filling it with water and placing it directly on the affected area.  This is its form.
The larger the cupping, the better it is, as it can hold more water.
Chapter Seventy-nine.
On applying leeches.
Leeches are used mostly on parts of the body where cupping cannot be applied, either because they are small, like the lips and gums, or because the area is exposed, like the fingers and nose.
To use them, select leeches from clean, fresh water free of impurities, then leave them in fresh water for a day and a night to ensure they are hungry and have nothing in their stomachs. Then the body is first bled by venesection or cupping, and then the afflicted area is cleaned.
Until it turns red, then the leech is placed on it. When it is full, it will fall off. If it is possible to draw the affected area with a cupping glass, that is more beneficial. Otherwise, wash the area with vinegar, then with plenty of water, and massage and squeeze it. If bleeding continues after the leech falls off, and if it is a trickle, then dip a linen cloth in cool water.
Place it over the area until the bleeding stops.  If the bleeding is excessive, sprinkle powdered alum or astringent on it, or similar styptics, until the bleeding stops, or place sliced, peeled dates on the area.
Leave it until the dates adhere to the area; then the bleeding will stop.  If further leech application is needed, do not use the same leeches if others are available. If the leeches refuse to adhere, then wipe the area with fresh blood.
Or, pierce the area with a needle until some blood comes out, then place [the leech] there. If it senses any blood, it will adhere to the spot. When you want it to fall off, sprinkle some of the herb 'sabr' (or dates), salt, or ash on it, and it will fall off in that spot.
Chapter Three.
On Fractures.
This chapter, too, is among the essential topics in the medical arts, concerning the setting of fractures and dislocations of the bones.
Know, my children, that many ignorant physicians and laypeople might claim expertise in this chapter, having never perused the writings of the ancients or read a single word from them.
For this reason, this art of medicine has become lost in our country, and I have never encountered a single skillful practitioner in it. My knowledge of it comes from my long study of the writings of the ancients and my dedication to understanding them, until I extracted the knowledge from them.  Then, I dedicated my life to practice and skill.
I have presented in this chapter everything I know about this. My experience has been applied to this, after I have brought it closer to you, removing lengthy explanations, condensing it to the utmost brevity, clarifying it completely, and showing many images of the tools used within it.
This is an addition to the clarification, as I have done in the previous two chapters; there is no power except through God, the Most High, the Great.
Chapter One.
This chapter contains a summary of the treatment of fractures.  Before we begin describing individual broken and dislocated limbs, it is necessary to give some general comments and sections in the beginning of this chapter. This will first necessitate understanding and recognizing the truth for you, and for whoever else is eager to learn this noble profession.
If someone suffers a fracture, dislocation, sprain, or fall, one should first hasten to reduce the injury, or to ease it, or both. Unless prevented by factors like weakness or the person is a child or very old, or the weather is extremely hot or extremely cold.
Then, the patient's diet should be restricted to cold legumes, poultry, and venison.  Drinks, fatty meats, overeating, and any food that causes a rapid increase in blood flow are prohibited, until the swelling subsides and there is no further expected discharge to the area.
Then, the patient should return to his initial regimen, as usual.  If the fractured bone begins to knit, the patient should eat a very nutritious, thick, and substantial diet with viscosity, such as lentils, rice, heads of grain, and ox-tripe. Eggs, soft fish, and thick drinks, and similar foods, will lead to quicker and better healing of the fracture.  Know that fractures in robust men and the elderly will not readily heal to their original state.
Due to the dryness and hardness of their bones, a fracture in the bones of the elderly may not heal completely.  However, fractures in very pliable bones, like those of young children, may heal.  Nature produces a substance resembling glue, thick and strong, around the fractured bone, attaching it and drawing the bone fragments together until they knit.
Until it achieves the original strength and stability, so that nothing hinders its functions.
Therefore, the patient's diet should consist of foods with strength, viscosity, and thickness, as we mentioned.  Know that fractures vary according to the affected limb, as a fracture of the leg is different from a fracture of the head, and a fracture of the chest is different from a fracture of the spine.
And so are all other limbs, each different from the others. We will describe each type of fracture in detail, separately, in its own section.  The type of bone fracture can also vary within itself.
For a fracture can be a simple break without splinters, or it can be a lengthwise break with splinters, detached fragments, and non-detached fragments.  The fracture may also occur with a wound and skin laceration, or as a slight crack.
Each type requires a specific method of treatment, as will be detailed in its respective section.  Recognizing a bone fracture involves its distortion, swelling, visibility to the touch, and the grating sound when touched with the hand.
If there is no visible distortion, no grating sound, and no discomfort or pain when palpating the bone, then there is no fracture.  It could be a strain, a minor fracture, or a slight crack, and it should not be manipulated with stretching or probing at all.
Instead, apply the appropriate medications to be described later, and then apply a gentle bandage.  Know that when a bone is compressed and broken in two without splinters, each piece has separated from the other.
Therefore, you should immediately attempt to realign and straighten the bone before a hot swelling develops.  If a hot swelling does occur, leave it for several days until it subsides, and then correct it by any means possible, using gentleness and skill.
Know that setting and straightening a bone is easier than one with splinters.  Then, it is bandaged as will be described later.  If the bone has splinters, the injured limb (hand or foot) must be extended from both sides.
Either by your hands if the limb is small, or by two ropes, or with both ropes and hands. Position the limb on a level surface in its natural shape.  Once the broken bone segments have been extended, return the displaced parts to their correct positions by any method possible, with gentleness and skill.
Take great care that your actions do not cause pain or suffering to the patient.  Strive to join the two bone fragments together in the best possible position.  In this process, you should touch and feel the bone fragments with your hand.  If you find anything out of place, correct and straighten it to the best of your ability.
Avoid excessive stretching and forceful manipulation, as many unskilled practitioners do.  Often, their actions cause a hot swelling or inflammation in the limb, as I have repeatedly observed.
After the setting, careful alignment, and bandaging, keep the limb still and at rest. Warn the patient not to move it during wakefulness, sleep, turning over, restlessness, bowel movements, or any other activity to the fullest extent possible.  Ensure that the position of the limb is one that minimizes pain.
If the patient feels pain or discomfort in a particular position, he should change it to one where he feels no pain, taking care that the new position is straight and aligned.
So that no deformity occurs if it is set.  Now, concerning the method of bandaging a fractured limb, as I have described it to you, know that fractured limbs vary in size, shape, and condition.  Those that are small, such as the arm, finger, forearm, and the like...
Therefore, the bandages for small parts should be gentle and soft, while for larger parts such as the thigh, back, and chest, the bandages should be firm and strong.  A wide bandage is needed for a large limb, tightening it evenly from all sides, without any unevenness.
Then, after completing the alignment, apply the appropriate plaster. Apply a soft bandage to the fracture site.  Then, wrap the bandage around the fracture three or four times, depending on the limb.  Slightly tighten the bandage, then move it upwards towards the higher portion of the fracture.
Tighten the bandage less than you did for the fractured area, then move away from the fracture slightly in your wrapping.  Gradually loosen the tension until you achieve some proper positioning of the bone, then take another bandage and wrap it around the fractured area as well.
Wrap several times, then move the wrapping towards the lower part of the fracture.  Maintain the tension and looseness described in the first, upper wrap.  Place soft bandages or cloths between the layers to correct any deformity in the fracture.
If there is a deformity, correct it; otherwise, do not insert anything.  Then wrap another bandage around it.  Finally, apply firm splints over these layers if there is no swelling or inflammation in the limb.
If there is swelling or inflammation, apply something to soothe the swelling and reduce the puffiness.  Leave it for a few days, then apply splints when the swelling subsides.  The splints should be made of cut and prepared cane strips, or splints made from sieve wood.
Made from pine, palm fronds, reeds, or similar readily available materials, and the width of each splint should be the same.
However, the splint placed directly over the fracture should be slightly thicker and wider than the others.  The length of the splint should be appropriate to the size of the fractured limb.  Image of the splint:
Then tighten the splints with another bandage, using the same tension as the first.  Secure them from above with strong threads, tightening more over the fracture site as previously described. The tighter the bandage, the closer to the fracture.
The threads should be of medium thickness and moisture content, preferably linen.  If the threads are too thick, like those made from twisted linen strips, as the ignorant sometimes do, it is a serious error.
Because the tightness will be irregular, and thin threads are also unsuitable as they do not allow the desired tension. There should be at least a finger's width between splints.
If the patient is pained by the edges of the splints after proper tightening, place soft pads or fluffy wool underneath so that nothing irritates him.  If the fracture is accompanied by a wound and the skin has been broken, we will discuss that separately.
Know that not every fractured limb should be splinted from the first day.  If the limb is large, splints should not be applied until five, seven, or more days, depending on the risk of inflammatory swelling.
This is a description of the dressings used by the ancients to treat fractures, dislocations, and sprains.  This is a general, concise dressing suitable for fractures and applicable to most temperaments, especially children and women, as it is neither excessively hot nor cold.
Take flour dust, the residue that clings to the millstones during grinding, knead it as it is without sifting it with egg white. Make the dough neither thick nor thin, then bake it.
Another dressing for fractures, dislocations, and sprains: take ten dirhams each of mastic, gum arabic, acacia, raisin, and poppy seeds; five dirhams each of myrrh and aloes; twenty dirhams of acacia; and twenty dirhams of Armenian or Roman clay. Grind all the ingredients together and mix with acacia water, or egg white if the patient's temperament is hot.  Use this dressing, as it is well-composed and quickly sets fractured bones, and is suitable for most people due to its moderate properties.
Another dressing useful for fractures and sprains: take ten dirhams each of poppy seeds, mastic, and white frankincense; five dirhams each of myrrh and aloes; six dirhams of acacia; and twenty dirhams of Armenian clay. Grind all finely, sift, and mix with water or egg white and apply.
A dressing for joints and bones dislocated from their places, and to relieve accompanying pain, and bone fracture: take prepared wool, dip it in vinegar and cooked oil, and apply to the affected area.  This dressing does not possess the strength to set fractures.
However, it is excellent for relieving hot swelling and pain. Another dressing to set a fractured bone: take the leaves of the common fig and the leaves of the wild poppy, grind them together and apply the paste to the area, moist.  Another brief dressing used for fractures.
If you wish to resolve the remaining swelling, take root of frankincense, chamomile, violet, and carnation flour, one part each. Grind all together, and mix with poultice if the limb is not inflamed. If it is inflamed, mix with coriander water or water, and apply.
Another stronger dressing for resolving than the first, used when a hard swelling occurs with a fracture: take root of frankincense, linseed, fenugreek, rosemary, violet, and chamomile, one part each.
Grind all together, and mix with dill water or fresh water, or poultice, according to the heat of the limb and the abatement of its heat.  Hippocrates, however, did not mention applying this to a fractured limb during its setting.
Except for the plaster made of wax and oil only, which should be of medium thickness, neither too thick nor too thin.  Galen believed that things containing dryness and some heat, such as myrrh, mastic, and frankincense, and similar substances, should be applied to the fractured limb during its setting.
A dressing for weakness and pain: take mugwort, chickpeas, shaved human hair or bird feathers, frankincense, and salt, equal parts. Grind, sift, mix, and apply as a dressing.  And regarding the amount of ligament to remain...
Then it should be resolved. Observe. If the patient does not experience pain, irritation, or movement of the fractured bone from its place, do not resolve it for many days.  If there is severe irritation, troublesome pain, or swelling in the area,
Then immediately resolve it and do not delay this at all. Remove the dressing. Then take a soft cloth or a damp sea sponge, dip it in lukewarm water, and wash the area until the irritation and pain subside. Then allow the limb to rest for an hour.
Then apply moistened wool soaked in vinegar and oil, or rose oil, and bandage it overnight. This will help to alleviate the hot swelling and reduce the swelling and pain of the limb. Then gently reapply a light bandage, but not as tight as the first one.
Treat it gently until it heals. If you see that the swelling, redness, pain, and swelling have completely disappeared and a bandage and wrapping are now needed, then reapply them as you did originally. If nothing of what we have mentioned occurs in the limb,
Do not resolve it until three, four, five, or seven days later. Sometimes it may be left for twenty days.  All this depends on the condition of the limb, as we have said, until the fracture heals and the flesh begins to knit together.
Then increase the bandage more than the first one and increase the patient's diet as previously mentioned. If you see that the fracture site has become dry and emaciated more than it should, know that the nourishment is not reaching it.
Unbandage it with lukewarm water each time you loosen the bandage, every three days.  Slightly reduce the tightness.  By doing this, nourishment will flow to the limb and it will heal quickly.  As for what ignorant practitioners, the unskilled ones, do—re-breaking the limb—
If it is not set properly in the first place and is set crookedly, it is a mistake on their part and a great deception. Even if it were correct, the ancients would have mentioned it in their books and practiced it.  I have seen no trace of such a thing from any of them. The correct approach is not to use it.
The second chapter.
Concerning fractures of the head.
Fractures of the head are numerous, their forms varied, and their causes diverse.  Some fractures result from a sword strike, and in these cases, the bone may be fractured completely down to the peritoneum beneath the bone. As happens with wood.
And thus, this type of fracture is called "coming" or "penetrating."  Alternatively, the sword may only cut a portion of the bone, fracturing the surface but not penetrating completely; this type of fracture is called "simple extraction," and the wound from these fractures may be large or small.
Some fractures are caused by crushing or contusion, resulting from a blow with a stone, a fall on a stone, or similar.  This fracture may be penetrating, approaching the membrane beneath the bone, or superficial, on the surface of the bone.
The wound from these fractures may also be large or small.  There are also fractures that are hidden within the bone, subtle, like a slight fissure.
Therefore, this type of fracture is called "hairline."  A fracture can also occur from a fall or a blow from a stone, or similar, driving a part of the bone inwards, causing a depression in the area, similar to how a piece of copper is dented if struck, with a portion being driven inwards.
This is most common in the wet, young bone of the head, such as in children's heads.  All these types of fractures may have detached or non-detached fragments.  We will discuss the treatment of each in its place, and all these types of fractures are identified through examination.
Inspecting them with probes and removing any putrid flesh from them.  The hairline fracture is recognized by examining the bone, rubbing and staining it with ink, for the fracture will appear black.  The treatment of the fracture must first consider the patient's symptoms.
If you see obvious signs of danger in the patient, such as vomiting bile, prostration, loss of consciousness, loss of voice, fainting, high fever, bulging and redness of the eyes, and similar symptoms, do not approach the patient or treat him.  In most cases, death is inevitable with these symptoms.
If you see symptoms that do not cause alarm and hope for his recovery, then begin his treatment.  This is if the injured person comes to you shortly after the injury, and if this is during the winter.
In any case, you should strive to remove the bone before the fourteenth day. If it is in the summer, you should hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, before the membrane below the bone rots and causes the symptoms we have described.
If the fracture has reached the membrane covering the brain, and there is crushing and laceration, then you should cut away the crushed and lacerated part, as I describe to you: shave the head of the injured patient.
Expose the bone in any way possible, according to the shape of the wound and what is causing discomfort to the patient.  If, during the exposure of the bone, you encounter bleeding or a hot swelling, address it appropriately.
This means to fill the area with cloths dipped in a mixture of wine and rose oil, and to clean the wound until the swelling subsides and bleeding stops. Then, begin to manipulate and remove the bone, and this can be done in one of two ways.
One method is to cut the bone with a narrow, delicate incision. This is its depiction:
Then, use another incision wider than the first. This is its depiction:
Then, use another incision even wider than the second.  You should have several different incisions, some wider than others, some shorter than others, and they should have extremely sharp edges.  They should be made of Indian steel or good quality فولاد.
Use gentle force when striking the incision to avoid jarring the head and causing injury.  If the bone is strong and hard, it is necessary to perforate it around the area before using the incisions, with the type of drill called a "non-dipping" drill.
Because it does not go beyond the skull bone's edge, for the drill has a rounded edge below its sharp head, like a small ring or circle, preventing it from sinking and penetrating the thickness of the bone.
You should have many of these drills, each suitable for the thickness of that bone, so that for each skull, you have a drill with a sharp tip the correct length and shortness according to the thickness of that skull.  These are images of three types. Of drills, large, medium, and small.
As for how to drill around the fractured bone, place the drill on the bone and turn it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been pierced, then move the drill to another location, leaving the distance between each hole equal to the thickness of a reed or similar.
Then, using the chisels, cut between each pair of holes, doing this with the utmost gentleness, as we said, so that you remove the bone either by hand or with other tools that you have prepared for this purpose.
Such as scrapers and fine forceps.  You must be extremely careful that the drill or chisel does not touch the periosteum.  If you have smoothed the bone and separated it from the periosteum, if any was attached, and you have removed it, it should then be cleaned.
Any roughness remaining on the bone is smoothed with another tool similar to a chisel, but it should be thinner and more delicate than the other chisels. If any small bone fragments or splinters remain, remove them gently using the tools at your disposal.
Then, the wound is treated afterward with compresses and ointments, as will be described later.  The other approach to treatment is quite simple, free of risk.  Galen discussed it and praised it highly.  This is what he said. First, you should begin by exposing the portion of the bone where the fracture is most severe and prominent.  Once you have exposed that part, position the tip of this lenticular chisel beneath it.  Here is its image:
The lenticular part of it should be smooth, cutting nothing.  The pointed part, at its tapering ends, as you see, should be positioned so that the lenticular part rests against the periosteum and the sharp edge of the chisel is against the bone.  Then, strike the chisel from one side.
With a small hammer, so that all the bone is broken gently, as it turns, and you are safe from injuring the membrane, no frightening accident will occur, even if the surgeon is the most ignorant and fearful person, indeed, even if he is asleep.
If any part of the membrane remains attached to the bone in some areas, scrape it away with the tip of the lenticular chisel itself, and detach it gently; it will be removed without harm or fear.
However, if the fracture of the bone has not reached the periosteum, and the broken surface of the bone remains rough and splintered, then you should remove those roughnesses and splinters with various-sized delicate instruments, so that you can use the one most suitable for each location, according to what the work requires.
With the same broken bone and its shape, you should initially use the widest of those instruments to scrape the bone, then use a narrower one after that, and continue doing so in succession until you finally use the narrowest and finest of them all.
As for the other small, hairline cracks and minor fractures, each should be treated according to what will repair it; this is something obvious to anyone with even a slight degree of skill in this craft, who has examined what we have written and explained about major fractures.
If the periosteum remains exposed after removing the bone, you should take a linen cloth the size of the wound, soak it in rose oil, and place it over the wound's opening.  Then take another cloth, folded or triangular. Soak it in rose water and oil, place it on the first cloth, doing this as gently as possible so as not to weigh down the peritoneum. Then use a wide bandage on top, and tie it only tightly enough to hold the wound.
Leave it with this treatment for a day or two, until the hot swelling subsides. Then loosen the bandage and use the following remedies afterwards, those that are conducive to drying, such as iris root, cressn, kender powder, and zarawand, etc.  Make poultices from these remedies.
And sprinkle the wound with these dry powders.  In general, use a remedy that cleanses without stinging.  Do your utmost during treatment to ensure the wound is clean, free of grease, dirt, or pus; never allow pus to collect in it.
Because if pus collects on the dura mater, it corrupts and putrefies it, causing a great affliction for the patient.  This can occur in the peritoneum of the head when the bone is exposed, especially if treatment is neglected.
If a dark discoloration appears on its surface, and if the patient exhibits the symptoms we have mentioned, know that he is doomed.  If the discoloration occurred due to a medicine applied, and if the potency of that medicine was such that it would cause this discoloration, .
You should take one part honey and three parts rose oil, blend them thoroughly, and then apply the mixture to a cloth.  Place this cloth on the peritoneum and treat it with the appropriate remedies until it heals.
Chapter Three.
In setting a fractured nose.
Know that the only parts of the nose that can fracture are both or one of its upper halves, because they are bone.  The lower part is cartilaginous and does not fracture, but may be bruised, bent, or perforated.
If one of its halves is fractured, you should insert a small finger into the nasal cavity and straighten the fracture from inside with your index finger and thumb from outside, until you have restored the nose to its natural shape.  Do this gently, being careful not to cause the patient pain.
If the fracture is in the upper part of the nose and your finger cannot reach it, a blunt, slightly thick instrument should be used to straighten it.  If the fracture is in both sides, do likewise.  The reduction should be performed on the first day after the fracture.
If possible, otherwise, after seven or ten days, when the acute swelling has subsided, insert a wick made of linen thread into the nasal cavity if the fracture is on one side, or two wicks if the fracture is on both sides.
Let the wick be of a thickness sufficient to fill the nasal cavity. Some earlier practitioners of bone-setting advised soaking the wick in ghee and changing it every day. I do not agree with this; the wick should be soaked in beaten egg white mixed with mill dust.
Then the wick is left in place until the bone sets and the cartilage hardens.  You may also insert goose feather tubes, wrapped in soft linen, into the nasal cavity in the area of the wick.  This would provide more secure fixation of the fractured nose, and to ensure the patient can continue to breathe.
This is not essential; you may do it if you wish, or you may simply use the wicks.  If a hot swelling develops on the nose during your work, bandage the nose with linen cloth or cotton soaked in vinegar and rose oil or a preparation from the diachylon ointment.
If no hot swelling develops, bandage the outside of the nose with fine semolina and powdered kender mixed with egg white.  Then place a soft bandage over it.  Do not tie the nose at all, if the nasal bones are fractured in small pieces.
Or if it has fractured into pieces, you must cut through it and remove it using the appropriate tool.  Then suture the gap and treat it with ointments that promote healing and suppuration. If there is a wound inside the nose, treat it with a wick and use lead tubes. Until it heals.
Chapter Four.
On setting a fractured lower jaw.
If the lower jaw is fractured and the fracture is not accompanied by an open wound, if the fracture is only from the outside and did not break into two pieces and is curved inward, then its recognition is easy.
If the fracture is in the right side, the index finger of the left hand should be inserted into the patient's mouth.  Likewise, if the fracture is on the left side, the index finger of the right hand should be inserted and gently lift the fracture bump from inside to outside.
With your other hand outside the bone, control its alignment.  If the jaw fracture has broken into two pieces, then traction from both sides is needed to straighten it. If this has occurred in the teeth, it will heal.
Or, if parts have come loose, tie what you hope to remain together with a gold, silver, or silk thread.  Then, place a splint over the fractured jaw.  Then, place a folded cloth over the splint.  Put a large, firm bandage, or a piece of leather the length of the jaw, over the cloth. Then, bind it from above in whatever way you deem appropriate to bring it together so it does not move.  Instruct the patient to be quiet and still.  Give him soft foods to eat. If you suspect a change in shape, reopen it on the third day.
Then, bind it from above in whatever way you deem appropriate to bring it together so it does not move.  Instruct the patient to be quiet and still.  Give him soft foods to eat. If you suspect a change in shape, reopen it on the third day. Then, fix what you have bound with mill dust and egg white or semolina flour, after removing the splint. Apply a soft compress to the bandage.  As long as the bandage adheres and the bone's condition does not change, leave it.
Do not reopen it until the fracture is healed and strong. This fracture often strengthens in three weeks. If a hot swelling appears during this time, use the remedies we have previously mentioned to calm it until it disappears.
And if the fracture is with an open wound, if a piece or pieces of bone have separated, gently remove those pieces with whatever tool is appropriate. If the wound opening is narrow, widen it with a scalpel as needed.
Then, if you have removed all the bone fragments, suture the wound opening if it is wide; otherwise, apply one of the appropriate ointments. And suture the wound until it heals.
Chapter Five.
On the setting of a fractured collarbone.
The collarbone is most often fractured from the front, at the end of the shoulder blade.  Its fracture can occur in one of three ways: either it fractures and splinters into two pieces without causing splinters, which is easiest to set;
Or, the fracture may have splinters, which is more difficult to set; or the fracture may be accompanied by a wound.  If the fracture is without a wound, have two assistants; one to hold the arm near the fractured collarbone, and the other to pull the neck towards the other side.
Then, align the fracture with your fingers until it is in the proper shape, without a bump or deformity.  If further pulling is needed, a ball of cloth or wool should be placed under the patient's armpit.
Hold the bone according to your needs, pulling and elevating the collarbone, and pressing the ball with your hand until the fracture is properly aligned.  If you cannot draw the fractured end of the collarbone outward because it has become buried, the patient should lie on their back.
A medium-sized cushion is placed under the patient's shoulder blade, and the assistant presses down on the patient's shoulder blade so that the buried part of the collarbone rises.  Then, correct and align the fracture with your fingers.
If you sense that a splinter of the collarbone has broken off and is moving, you should carefully separate and remove it. If the splinter is embedded in the bone, use a suitable cutting instrument to extract it. After placing the instrument that protects the diaphragm under the collarbone, which is made of wood or metal, this is its image:.
It resembles a spoon, without a handle, and its width should be adjusted to the size of the bone, while its length should be determined by the work required. Let it have two ends, one wider and the other narrower, as you see.
If the wound you made when extracting the bone splinter is wide and you fear inflammation, stitch the edges of the wound together. If the wound is minor or you fear severe inflammation, close the wound with sutures and dressings proportionate to the size of the incision.
If inflammation arises, soak the dressings in rose-water and vinegar or a potion and apply them.  If the bone is swollen without a wound or incision, apply a bandage made of millstone dust mixed with egg white. Place a soft pad on it, and if needed, place a ball under his armpit. Then take a very long turban, about a span wide or so.  Place a folded dressing over the pad and bandage.  Then take a splint of thin wood. Make it three fingers wide and the same in length. Then place it in a cloth, and then place this cloth with the splint in the part of the turban that covers the fracture site.  Then tighten the turban around the fracture as it circles. Tie it around his neck, and under his healthy armpit and under his injured armpit, and return it repeatedly to each side.  How you see that the tightening secures the fracture firmly is obvious to you.
The whole point is that the splint should not move from the broken bone. Therefore, you should examine the patient every day.  Whenever the ligaments loosen and you see the splint has shifted, correct it and tighten the bandage.  Then, make the patient sleep on his back and place a small pillow under his armpit at night.
So that his arm is raised from his side, and the clavicle fracture rises with the shoulder blade, or tie his arm to his neck. Do not loosen the bandage unless a wound or swelling occurs in the area within twelve days.
Then renew the dressing if you see a reason for it. Maintain the traction and leave it until the clavicle fracture is set and consolidated, and it strengthens further in twenty-eight days. And it may be in some people in less than that.
Chapter Six.
Concerning the setting of a shoulder fracture.  The shoulder rarely breaks in its broad portion, but rather in its bony projections.  If a part breaks or if it breaks in the middle, this can only be known by palpation.  Then, according to the form of the fracture, apply a splint.
Reduce it [the fracture] and restore it to its natural shape by every possible means, then apply to the area a mixture of millstone dust, egg white, and soft plaster.  Place over this a bandage of folded cloth, and then apply a wide splint of wood.
Make the splint as wide as or slightly wider than the entire shoulder.  If there is a protrusion beneath the splint on any part of the shoulder, smooth out that protrusion with soft plaster until the splint lies flat.  Then tie a long bandage over it.
Tie it securely and ensure that the splint does not shift from its position. Examine the bandage every day; if it loosens, tighten it and adjust the splint if it shifts. The patient should lie on his/her healthy side, and the shoulder will be set in twenty [days].
For a period of one or twenty-five days.  Once this period is complete, remove the bandage with confidence, as it is one of the bones that do not fear shattering or displacement.  If a bone fragment protrudes through the skin and is causing irritation, cut around it and remove it. Do what has been previously described for the treatment of clavicle fractures, in the case of any inflammation occurring.
Chapter Seven.
On the treatment of fractured ribs.
The chest can fracture in the middle, though this is infrequent.  Its extremities, however, are more prone to fracturing and fragmentation.  If the middle of the chest breaks, it tends to sag downward, and the patient experiences severe pain, difficulty breathing, and coughing.
Sometimes blood may be coughed up, and the depression in the fractured bone becomes evident, readily apparent to touch.  To treat the fracture, the patient should lie on their back, with a pillow between their shoulders.  Then, the patient's sides should be pressed together, and the ribs should be brought together with the hands, from both sides.  One should carefully align the ribs from all possible angles.
And hold it until the bone's shape returns to normal. Then, apply a bandage and splint, placing a thin board of poplar, willow or similar light wood as a splint over it, after wrapping it in a cloth. Then, carefully tie it to the fractured bone so it does not come loose.  Wrap the bandage around the back in a circular motion several times and tighten it firmly.  Inspect the bandage frequently, and tighten it whenever it loosens.
If the injury requires removing the splint due to an ulcer, pain, or swelling, then immediately remove the splint and bandage, and treat any affected area.  Then, if appropriate, reapply the bandage. And maintain the pressure until it heals.
Chapter Eight.
In the setting of fractured ribs, know that fractures occur in the thick parts of the ribs adjacent to the back, and their anterior ends.  Impacts to these areas often cause fractures, as they are cartilaginous.  This can be easily determined through palpation.
Treat the fracture by aligning the broken pieces with your fingers until they are properly aligned.  Then bandage and splint the fractured bone if necessary. If the ribs are fractured inwards, the patient will experience intense pain.
And the pain is like the pain of a person with a fistula, because the bone pierces the diaphragm, and also causes shortness of breath, coughing, and profuse spitting of blood.  This is a difficult condition to treat, and the ancients resorted to numerous methods to treat it.
Some say that the patient should consume foods that produce wind and gas to inflate the abdomen, thus expanding it and pushing the fractured bone outward.  We reject this, as it could exacerbate the development of a carbuncle if one has not already developed, or if it has, it will only make it worse.
Some said a cupping device should be placed on the area, then forcefully suctioned.  This is more or less logical, but one fears that the cupping device, due to the weakness of the area, might pull excessively at the site. Some others suggested covering the area with wool dipped in hot oil.
The spaces between the ribs become filled in, so that the ligament is even when wrapped around. Then the patient is treated for a fistula, through diet and medication. If the condition is severe, the patient cannot bear it. The bone was piercing the diaphragm in a painful way, and we feared for the patient.  Therefore, it is necessary to make an incision over the area, expose the fractured rib, then place beneath it the instrument that protects the peritoneum, as previously described, and carefully cut and remove the bone.
Then, if the wound is large, we bring together the edges and stitch them, and treat it with ointments until it heals. If a carbuncle arises during this time, quickly apply poultices in rose oil to the affected area, and treat the patient internally with remedies to relieve the swelling.
He should lie on the side on which he sleeps more easily until he heals.
Chapter Nine.
On setting the vertebrae of the back and neck.
Concerning the vertebrae of the neck, if fracture occurs, this is rare.  More common are contusions, and the same applies to the vertebrae of the back.  If this happens to someone, and you wish to know whether it will heal or not, ... If you see his hands have become relaxed, numb, and lifeless, unable to move, stretch, or grasp, and if you pinch or prick them with a needle, he feels no sensation or pain, know that he will likely not recover; he is in danger.
But if he can move them and feel the pinching and pricking, know that the spinal cord is intact and the patient will recover with treatment.  If a similar condition affects the vertebrae of the back and you wish to know if it will also heal, ...
Examine his legs.  If you see that they too have become relaxed, exhibiting the same condition as the hands, and then, when lying on his back, wind and stool are expelled involuntarily, and when lying on his stomach, urine is expelled involuntarily, ... If, lying on his back, he cannot urinate, know that he is doomed.  Do not bother with treatment.  If none of these occur, the matter is less serious.  Treatment for what has occurred should aim to reduce the intense inflammation.
By applying rose oil alone, or with roasted egg yolks, to the affected vertebrae three times a day, until the inflammation subsides.  Then apply a strengthening, absorbent dressing to the area, binding it with a bandage.  Advise rest and stillness.
He should sleep only on the side that does not cause him pain until he heals.  If a piece of bone or something else has broken off as a result of the injury, the skin should be cut open and the bone removed. Then, if the wound is large, its edges should be brought together with stitches, and treated with healing ointments until it heals.  If the coccyx, the tailbone, has also fractured, the little finger of the left hand should be inserted into the anus.
The broken bone should be aligned with the other hand as much as possible.  Then, a dressing and splint should be applied if necessary, and it should be bound tightly.
If you feel a broken fragment in it, cut open the area and remove it.  Treat the wound with the previously described methods until healed.
Chapter Ten.
On the setting of a fractured hip.
The bones of the hips rarely fracture.  If they do, the fracture is usually a splintering at the ends, a longitudinal splitting, and a tilting inward.  The patient experiences pain, throbbing, and numbness in the affected leg.
To set it, place your hand upon it to ascertain the nature of the fracture.  If the fracture is only at the ends, then straighten the fracture in whatever way appears suitable to you, until it resembles its natural shape.
If the fracture is lengthwise or has tilted inward, lay the patient on his stomach until you are ready to set the fracture.  Once you have straightened it, apply a bandage, then place a splint of wood or leather over it and firmly bind it.
Do not fear the fracture shifting or the splint coming loose.  Fill in any hollows around the hips to ensure even pressure.  Tell the patient to sleep on their back or on their sound side, if a hot swelling appears.
Stop pulling and setting it until the hot swelling subsides.  Apply whatever remedies are appropriate to soothe it, as before. Then return to setting and firmly binding it as necessary. If splinters or fragments from the ends of the bone appear, they should not be removed.
Do not touch [the splinters], but rather straighten [the bone] from the outside, as we said. Leave it bound until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
On setting a fractured humerus.
The humerus is the bone between the elbow and the shoulder. If it is broken, set it using one of two methods. One method is to take a smooth, slightly curved stick of medium thickness, like this:
Tie straps to each end of the stick.  Then suspend it from a high point.  The patient sits in a chair, and then places their broken arm on the stick, so that their armpit is positioned in the middle of the curve of the stick.  Then suspend a heavy object from above it.
Or an attendant supports the arm below.  Then the physician aligns the fracture with his hands until the fracture is set correctly.  The other method is for the patient to lie on their back, and have their arm suspended from their neck with a strap.
Then instruct two attendants to hold the fracture, one above and the other below, and to pull in opposite directions. If you want a stronger pull, tie a strap below the fracture and another above, and have each attendant pull in their direction.
If the fracture is near the shoulder joint, the strap should be positioned under the armpit and the other under the fracture, towards the elbow.  Similarly, if the fracture is near the elbow, the straps should be positioned in that area and on the elbow itself.
