Then gently align the fracture without force, until it is correctly aligned and fits well.  Then tighten the bandage, unless a hot swelling appears. If a hot swelling appears, delay tightening until the seventh day. Apply moistened cotton soaked in vinegar and rose oil to the area.
Until the swelling subsides. Then tighten the bandage.  The method of tightening is to hold the dressing over the fracture, then place a new cloth wrap over the dressing.  Then bring the arm up to the upper arm and position the hand open on the shoulder.
Hold the bandages and tighten them over the upper arm and forearm so that the arm acts as a splint, unless something prevents this or the broken bone shows no change. If you fear the bone might shift, use splints.
Place a splint wider and stronger than other splints directly on the fracture. Leave a space of one finger's width between each splint. The length of the splints should be in accordance with the fracture, with an additional three fingers on each side.
Then tighten the splints using the tightening method described in the first section.
The tightening should be more intense at the site of the fracture.
The further from the fracture, the less the tightening. If you find the splint placement and tightening as described earlier during your setting of the limb, then do it.
If you fear a hot swelling, leave the tightening and splints until the seventh day, as we said.  Then examine the ligaments every three days to prevent inflammation, swelling, or the limb's inability to receive nourishment due to excessive tightening.  Correct all these issues according to what we have previously mentioned.
If you are certain that nothing of that sort will happen, do not loosen the bandage until many days have passed.  The patient should lie on their back, with their hand on their abdomen.  A padded sleeve filled with wool, of moderate size, should be placed under the arm.
Examine at all times, night or day, to prevent the fractured limb from changing shape or the bandage from relaxing.  Correct all this with your effort.  Provide the patient with food in the manner we previously described; initially, let the food be soft until the bone has strengthened.
Then, the food should be thickened.  The arm and leg usually strengthen within forty days.  At that time, the bandage should be loosened, and the patient should use a bath and be treated with appropriate ointments. If the fracture is severe or displaced,
Do not loosen the bandage until fifty days or even two months.
Chapter Twelve.
On the setting of a fractured arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, called the radius. One is small, and it is the one next to the thumb. The other is large and is located underneath the small one, at the bottom.  Perhaps only the larger radius bone is broken, or only the smaller one, or perhaps both are broken.
If the smaller, upper radius is broken, its setting is easier and healing is faster. If the lower radius is broken, the fracture is worse and healing is more difficult. The worst case is when both bones are fractured together.  If the bone which is broken is the small, upper radius.
Therefore, the physician, when setting it, should gently apply a slight traction to align it. If the larger radius is broken, the traction should be stronger. If both radii are broken, the traction should be significantly stronger.
The hand should be positioned, when setting and pulling, extended on a cushion, with the thumb raised higher than all the other fingers, the little finger lower than the other fingers, and the patient seated upright.
Let the cushion be at his height so that the patient does not have difficulty.
Then, an attendant supports the arm from below, either with their hand or a bandage, and another attendant supports it from above.  Then, the physician aligns the bone, returning it to the best possible shape. If the fracture has splinters, remove them.
Return each splinter to its place; if a splinter appears protruding, irritating the skin, and you have no hope of setting it, cut into it and remove it as described above. If the fracture is accompanied by a wound, a separate chapter is dedicated to it.
Treat it from there [the separate chapter].  If a hot swelling appears at the beginning of the setting, apply a bandage with qīrūṭī (a medicated cloth) made with rose oil and white wax, of medium thickness, and bandage it gently until the swelling subsides.
Remove the qīrūṭī and apply a bandage prepared from mill dust and egg white. Then, apply the splints. The splint over the fracture itself should be wider and stronger. Know that, in most cases, six splints are needed for the arm.
The fracture may be in one ulna or in both ulnae.  Then, make the tightening over the fracture area stronger and firmer.  As you move the tightening up or down, gradually loosen the tension, as previously mentioned in the beginning of the chapter.
Use soft, damp bandages to wrap the fracture, avoiding overly firm bandages.  Use linen thread of medium thickness, as previously described, to secure it. Check the limb and ligaments after a few days.
If any problem arises, such as irritation on the limb, warm water should be applied to the limb until the irritation subsides.  Leave the limb unbandaged for a night to rest, then reapply the bandage.
If the bandage has become loose and the bone has shifted, or something similar has occurred, correct all of it to the best of your ability. Also, if the limb is deprived of nourishment due to excessive tightening, loosen the bandage slightly and leave it for a few days until the nourishment reaches it, then tighten it again.
If the patient experiences none of the problems mentioned, the bandage should not be removed until approximately twenty days have passed.  Then, suspend the patient's arm from their neck, keeping the arm straight.  The patient should avoid strenuous or jarring movements and sleep on their back.
Know that this arm fracture may heal in thirty days, or thirty-two, or perhaps in twenty-eight days, all depending on the constitution and strength of the individual.
Chapter Thirteen.
On setting fractures of the hand and fingers.
The metacarpals of the hand and the phalanges of the fingers rarely fracture, but often suffer contusions.  When a fracture or contusion occurs to the hand...
The patient should sit with legs folded, and a chair before him at the same level.
Then, the patient places his hand outstretched on the chair, and the hand bone's attendant extends and straightens it. The physician adjusts it until a good alignment is achieved.  At that time, a bandage and splint should be applied, unless a hot swelling has developed. Then, a splint is to be placed over the area.
Wrap it in a soft cloth. If the fracture is towards the palm of the hand, shape the cloth into a ball and tell the patient to grasp it with their injured hand, then secure it with a long bandage.
The splint should be made of soft leather so that the skin heals with all the hand's movements, and it should be tightened appropriately. If the fracture is outward, then a splint should be placed on top and another underneath the hand to keep it open and upright.
Then, the splint should be tightened as the hand moves and tied together with a bandage around the fingers. If the fracture is in one of the fingers, if it's the thumb, straighten it appropriately, then splint it with the hand.  If you like, you can use a small, separate splint to straighten the fracture.
If the fracture is in any other finger, such as the middle, index, ring, or little finger, straighten them and bandage them to the healthy adjacent finger, or bandage them all together, which is better, or use a small separate splint.
As we said about the thumb, and check carefully after setting the fracture and away from the hot swelling.  Address it appropriately if anything like that occurs, as previously described.
Chapter Fourteen.
In the setting of a fractured thigh.
The thighbone often fractures and is easily felt because it shifts back and forth.  Its reduction is done by applying a bandage above the fracture and another below, with the patient lying on their face.
Then each bandage's attendant stretches it evenly to their side, if the fracture is in the middle of the bone.  However, if it is near the origin of the thigh, a soft bandage, either of wool or similar material, should be applied.
At the origin of the thigh, towards the pubic area, the stretching should be upwards, and the other bandage below the fracture.  Similarly, if the fracture is near the knee, the bandage should be near the knee to allow stretching downwards.  Then the physician should align the bone with both hands until it is restored to its natural form.
The bone will heal well.  Then, the bandage and tension should be maintained unless inflammation occurs in the limb.  If inflammation occurs, leave it for a few days until the heat subsides, then resume treatment.
As for the tension, a wide, firm bandage should be wrapped around the fracture twice or thrice, leaving excess material.  Then the leg should be twisted so that the heel is at the origin of the thigh, and a long thread is inserted between the thigh and leg near the lower part of the knee, with the thread ends extending from both sides.
Then, wrap the remaining bandage around the leg and thigh.  Next, place splints on the thigh, directly over the fractured area, and create a single splint on the leg bone.  Fill the gap between the thigh and leg with soft padding to ensure even tension.
Then begin with firm tension from the middle over the fracture site, three or four turns.  As the bandage moves away from the fracture, the tension should be lighter, softer, and looser.
Then, take the ends of the thread you inserted between the thigh and leg, and tie the adjacent splints above it.  Then, pass the thread ends downwards to the heel of the leg and use them to tighten the splint ends on the other side.
So that the bandage does not shift from its position, maintain the tension as long as there is no ulceration, swelling, or puffiness in the limb.  If any of these occur, quickly loosen it and rectify any resulting problem as previously described.
If the bone has a fragment that pierces, it should be smoothed if possible.  Otherwise, cut around it and remove it. Treat the wound as previously described until it heals.  Sometimes, this thigh fracture can be splinted without including the leg.
As previously mentioned for the humerus and arm, splinting in this way does not cause lameness in the patient. However, if you splint only the arm, and not the leg as well, the patient will always be lame.
Know that the thigh takes fifty days or slightly more or less to heal, depending on the differences in temperaments and other conditions.
Chapter Fifteen.
In the fracture of the kneecap.
The kneecap rarely fractures, but it is often bruised. If a fracture does occur, it is either a crack or a splintering of its parts, and this may or may not be accompanied by a wound.
One must ascertain all of this through examination, and treat it by aligning the separated parts with the fingers until they come together, according to the best possible alignment, gentleness, and precision.  Then apply a bandage and a circular splint if necessary.
And apply further binding in accordance with that, then treat all situations described for other fractures, such as inflammation and the like, by responding to each symptom with the appropriate treatment until healed.
Chapter Sixteen.
On the setting of a fractured leg.
The leg has two bones, one thick called the tibia and the other thin called the ulna.  They are subject to the same types of fractures as the arm bones. Therefore, setting it is similar to setting the arm; the procedure is the same. If both bones are fractured, the leg tilts in all directions. If the thinner bone is fractured, the leg tilts forward.  If the thicker bone is fractured, particularly from below, this is evident to you.
Therefore, use extension and straightening, and bind the splints accordingly, just as in the case of the arm.  However, if the fracture of the leg is severe, with many fragments, the extension should be less and gentler, and apply your efforts to the setting carefully.
There is something extra in the procedure for the leg compared to the arm.  Once you have straightened the splints and completed all your work, take two pieces of pine wood, used for smoothing the surfaces of rooms, or pieces of palm fronds, or something similar.
Choose from them something of moderate thickness, not thin, and let its length be the length of the leg from the knee downwards. Then wrap each one with two layers of cloth lengthwise, placing one on the leg and the other on the opposite side.
Let them extend from the knee to the foot. Then tie the two pieces of wood in three places at the ends and the middle. With this restraint, the leg will not tilt to the right or left and will be properly straightened.  A trough of wood may also be used along the length of the leg.
Place it in this trough to prevent movement.  This is particularly important in fractures with accompanying wounds. Then examine the leg every day, paying close attention to any swelling, puffiness, or other such things. If anything of this sort occurs, address it appropriately until it heals. The leg bone will be rejoined in thirty days or thereabouts.
Chapter Seventeen.
In the fracture of the leg bones and fingers.
The heel bone is never subject to fracture.  The bones of the leg may be fractured, and the fingers rarely fracture; rather, they are more often injured by contusion.
If a fracture of the leg bones occurs and you see the bones overlapping, have the patient stand on the ground upright, then place your foot on the raised portion of the bones and press down, aligning them.
Until they return to their proper positions. Then, take the bandage and splint, place a small, flat board with two ends beneath the sole of the foot.  Securely bind the foot with the bandage, cloth, and other necessary items.
If three or four days pass, loosen the bandage. You will find the bones aligned, whether they were fractured or dislocated. The procedure is as described.  If some of the fingers are fractured, realign and straighten them according to the instructions given for realigning the fingers of the hand.
Then, create a splint for the fractured finger, extending the length of the finger and slightly wider than it.  Place the board, as previously described, beneath the foot and secure it tightly. If two, three, or more bones are fractured.
Place a splint of appropriate size, wrapped in soft cloth, on each finger. Secure the board beneath the foot.  Ensure each end of the board extends beyond the foot's sole for proper stabilization.
You should not forget to consider all the previously mentioned symptoms in all fractures, and address each symptom appropriately.
Chapter Eighteen.
Fractures of the female pubic bone and the male pubic bone.
When the woman's vulva is fractured, have her sit crouched, then gently lean her back slightly.  Have someone hold her from behind.  Then, the midwife should pack her vulva with cotton until it is full, like a ball.
Then, the woman should be shaken, and her pelvis raised slightly, bit by bit.  The cotton will then emerge, forming a ball at the entrance of the vulva. The fracture of the bone will then be reduced.  Then, apply a pad to her back. When she wants to urinate, remove the cotton gently so she can urinate.
And replace it in the position it was initially placed, then return to the first pad. Do this for seven days or so, and it will set.  If you wish, take a sheep's bladder, tie a tube of reed to its opening, and insert the entire bladder into her vulva.
Then, blow into the tube forcefully so that the bladder inflates inside the vulva.  The fracture will then be reduced.  Then pack it with cotton and keep it as described until it heals.  As for when the pubic bone of a man or woman is fractured, this [method] is used for its reduction.
And its reduction is as described for the hip bone.  The correct procedure for these rare fractures should be clear to you, especially for those with some skill, and who understand this book. Indeed, the fracture itself often provides guidance.
Following the correct method for its reduction and fixation, understand this.  As for the penis of a man, if it is fractured, take a goose's windpipe, insert it into the penis, and place a bandage of cloth over it, binding it. Leave it for three days or so until healed.
Chapter Nineteen.
On the reduction of bone fractures when accompanied by a wound.
Whoever is presented with a fracture accompanied by a wound, especially if the bone is large, such as the femur or humerus, or similar, should immediately perform venesection if the conditions for venesection, as previously mentioned, are met.
If the wound is bleeding, you should immediately cauterize it with powdered alum, if nothing else is available, and then proceed with the reduction of the fracture on that very day, and do not delay it.
If no inflammatory swelling occurs, if an inflammatory swelling does occur, then postpone the reduction until the ninth day, until the inflammatory swelling subsides. Do not approach it at all on the third and fourth days, as it exposes it to undesirable symptoms.
If the fractured bone is protruding and exposed on the skin, you should gently and gradually attempt to return it to its place and align it with your hands. If you cannot return it and align it with your hands, then return it using this instrument, which is made of iron, and is seven or eight fingers long. And its width should match the size of the wound.
Therefore, the physician should make three or four of these, according to the treatment needed for each type of fracture. Let them be rounded and have slight thickness, so that they do not bend when being poked during use.
And they should be sharp-pointed, with a bend at the tip. The top should be thick, and from the middle to the bottom, much thinner. This is its image/shape.
And it is called in Greek "perem," meaning a small lever.  The sharp, bent end should be placed on the protruding bone end and pushed once until the bone returns and is somewhat aligned, then the fracture ends are further aligned.
If the broken end is thin and the instrument does not grip it well, then the end of that bone should be cut so that the instrument can hold it.  If it is still not possible to restore the bone as described, then cut it with a similar cut as those mentioned.
Or spread it open with one of the saws, in whatever way is possible for you, then remove any remaining roughness and thin scales from the bone. If, after returning the bone, the patient experiences severe, harmful pain, then know that the bone has not returned to its natural position.
If you can return it to its natural position, do so, for you will greatly benefit the patient.  Once the bone is completely set, soak a cloth in a black astringent drink, especially if it is summer. Do not place any bandages or anything oily on the wound.
So that no putrefaction or decay occurs. Then apply splints when you have finished setting the bone. Leave the wound uncovered, making a hole in the bandages large enough for the wound using scissors.
Beware, most carefully, of tightening the wound along with the fracture.  Often the ignorant physicians do this and cause their patients either death, or ulceration, or a cold.  Let your tightening be gentle, loose, and different from the tightening of other fractures.
If the wound is bad, or large, and you fear some of the bad symptoms we have described, and he feels distressing pain at the site, then you should not apply splints. Instead, make bandages of strong cloths in place of the splints, and bind it tightly with them.
If after a day or two, you see pus beginning to form in the wound, remove the cloth soaked in the solution that you placed on it. Then use dressings and ointments as we are accustomed to use for wounds, like the four-fold ointment and similar ones.  You should also loosen the bandage.
Examine the wound each evening and morning until it heals and is cured.  The limb should be positioned so that the pus drains downwards easily. If the wound takes many days and does not close, or the pus does not stop, know that there are small pieces of bone.
You should then probe the wound with a probe.  Remove any of those fragments that have come loose.  If any remain unloosened, and are piercing the limb, causing pain, then cut them out and remove them by any means possible.
If the wound develops a cold, or an eating away, or another type of corruption or putrefaction, you should counter each symptom with a similar treatment as described in its chapter.
When a large, prominent bone, such as the thigh bone or the upper arm bone, or similar large bones, is fractured, do not attempt to pull it out or remove it. Often death results from this.  Leave it until it rots.  Perhaps it will fall out of itself after twenty or thirty days.
Then treat the wound if you see an opportunity for treatment; otherwise, leave it.
Chapter Twenty.
On treating the suppuration that occurs after some fractures.
This suppuration often occurs after the fracture has healed, especially when near the joints.  It disfigures the limb and may prevent it from its natural function.
If the suppuration is fresh, use astringent remedies such as mastic, frankincense, myrrh, asafoetida, acacia, and the like, taking some or all of these, and mixing them with an astringent liquid, egg white, or vinegar.
Apply it to the suppuration, tightly bandaging it, and leave the bandage on for many days.  Then, remove it and repeat the process until the suppuration disappears, God willing, or apply a tight lead plate over it.
Lead has a property of removing any matter in the limbs.  If the suppuration has hardened and become severe, and necessity demands its removal, cut through it from above, removing the excess tissue or scraping it with some instrument until it disappears. Then treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-One.
On treating a fracture, if the limb, after being set, remains thin and not in its original form.
If a fractured bone is set and the limb afterward remains thin and weak, this is due to many reasons. One reason is either excessive loosening and re-tying of the ligaments improperly, or excessive tightening of the ligaments, preventing nourishment from reaching the limb, or excessive and improper poulticing, or excessive and inappropriate movement, or insufficient and weak blood in the patient's body.
The treatment for this is to nourish the patient and strengthen his body so that there is more blood in him, and to use baths, bring joy and happiness to him, and similar methods.  Then, apply asphalt to the limb to draw a lot of nourishment to it, or continuously poultice it with lukewarm water. Until nourishment flows to it and it returns to its natural shape.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
On the treatment of fractured bones that have been set crookedly and prevented from functioning properly.
If, after healing, a limb that has been set shows a crookedness or protrusion of the fractured bone, or a deformity, and the appearance of the limb is therefore marred. However, if the limb is not prevented from its natural function, then one should not accept the claim of those who assert that the limb should be broken from the head, a practice often performed by ignorant physicians and setters in our country.
This action is highly reprehensible and leads to great harm and likely injury.  But if the crookedness and deformity are recent, then it is appropriate to apply water in which relaxing herbs such as plantain leaves, its roots, and kings' crown, and similar ones, have been boiled.
And it is bandaged with relaxing dressings such as well-made diachylon, or the juice of the plantain root is taken and mixed with chicken fat and ghee, and bandaged with it, or the oily figs are taken, pounded with pigeon droppings and similar remedies called 'resolvers of inflammation'.
And the deformity will resolve through gentle, continuous massage with the hands, and the limb is moved in all directions at all times.  If the curvature has become severe, old, and hardened, and the need arises to treat it with iron.
Then it is necessary to cut it open above, releasing the connection of the bone and cutting off any excess deformity or bone with delicate cuts, using care and gentleness.  Then the wound is treated as previously mentioned until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
The jaw is a dislocated joint that prevents movement, spoils the appearance of the limb, and causes the patient severe pain and discomfort. If a person experiences a dislocated jaw, immediate repositioning is essential.
Delaying treatment is absolutely not permitted.  If delayed, the area will swell and become a hardened mass, making jaw repositioning difficult.  Therefore, it should not be delayed, nor should it be moved or stretched while it is swollen, for the patient often experiences painful spasms and discomfort.
However, if this occurs, the patient should first be bled, then allowed to rest until the swelling subsides somewhat.  The limb should then be bathed with warm water and oil, and the jaw gently repositioned.  Each limb should be treated as described in its respective section.
The chapters on the jaw are also arranged according to the preceding descriptions of fractures, from the upper to the lower parts of the body.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
On the treatment of the lower jaw.
The jaws rarely dislocate, and their dislocation occurs in one of two ways: either they slightly depart from their positions, relaxing slightly, or they completely and entirely dislocate, relaxing completely. To the extent of the chest, so that the patient's saliva drips, and he is unable to retain it, nor can he close his jaw or speak, articulating his tongue.  However, if the dislocation is slight, it often returns to its normal position easily.
But if the dislocation is complete, then it is necessary to promptly return it, and never delay.  The attendant should grasp the patient's head, and the physician should insert the thumb of one hand into the base of the jaw inside the patient's mouth, if the jaw is dislocated on one side.
Or insert both thumbs, if the jaw is dislocated on both sides, and use the rest of his fingers from the outside to adjust. He should instruct the patient to relax his jaw and let it go in every direction, while the physician adjusts and pushes the jaw back into its place.
If returning it is difficult, especially if both jaws are involved, use a hot water compress and ointment to make it easier to return them, and never delay their return as we have said.  Once they return and are aligned, and the patient's mouth is closed and the jaws are not relaxed, then apply strips of cloth soaked in ointment and rose-scented wax and ointment.
Then gently tie with a loose bandage. The patient should sleep on their back, with their head supported between two pillows so that they do not move it to the right or left, and do not attempt to chew anything; instead, their food should be soft and easily swallowed.
Until the pain is gone and the jaw is set, let him eat as he desires, and he should use it gently, and not exert himself to open his mouth for eating, drinking, or yawning until the jaw is set and healed.
If returning the jaws is difficult, when they have become dislocated and not returned to their positions, it often leads to fever, persistent headache, and possibly the patient's stomach will upset, and he may vomit repeatedly. If you see this, know that it is damaged.
Often, someone who experiences this dies within ten days.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
On the reduction of the clavicle and shoulder joint.
Regarding the clavicle, it does not dislocate from the inner side due to its connection to the chest.  It may dislocate outwards, and this is noticeable to the touch.  To reduce it, the patient should lie on their back and extend their arms.
Then, firmly press the area with your hand, and it will return to its place.  Then, apply bandages and splints, and secure them.  As for the portion of the clavicle near the shoulder, it rarely dislocates.  If it does, it should be reduced and set as described, and as much as you can manage.
Then you apply bandages, splints, and dressings, and instruct the patient to remain still and quiet until healed.  By the same treatment, the shoulder joint is reduced if it also comes out of place.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
In reducing the shoulder joint.
Know that the shoulder joint dislocates in three ways: one, downwards towards the armpit; two, towards the chest; and three, upwards, behind the shoulder.  This last is rare.
It does not dislocate behind the shoulder blade, nor in front of the nerve.  It most commonly dislocates downwards, towards the armpit, especially in those with little muscle, for it quickly exits and quickly re-enters.
However, in those with substantial muscle, the opposite is true; it exits with difficulty and re-enters with difficulty.  Sometimes, a blow or fall may cause the shoulder to swell with a hot, inflamed lump, leading one to believe a dislocation has occurred;  it is thus necessary to examine this carefully to determine its nature.
Then, in treating it, if the dislocation is downwards toward the armpit, if you place the dislocated shoulder against the healthy one, you will find a noticeable difference; you will find a deformity in the head of the shoulder and, under the armpit when touched, the head of the shoulder is like an egg.
The afflicted person cannot raise their arm to their ear, nor perform any other movements.  Likewise, if dislocated towards the chest or upwards, this will be evident upon examination.
This dislocation may be easily reduced if the shoulder is soft, or if the patient is a child.  To reduce it, have an attendant raise the arm above the head, then place your thumb beneath the armpit, and forcibly lift the joint upward to its proper position, while the attendant raises and extends the arm upwards.
Then, lower it downwards, and it will return quickly. If it does not return by the method described, and the dislocation has occurred many days prior, the patient should bathe in hot water and use remedies that loosen and soften the muscles, such as cooking the root of a plant called 'al-khatmi', fenugreek, and "crown of the king" in water, and apply it.
Then the patient lies on their back, and a ball of wool of moderate firmness is placed under the armpit.  The physician places his heel on the ball, holds the patient's hand in his hand, and extends it downwards.
And pushes the ball with his foot, and the dislocation will return to its proper place. Or, if you wish, use this method: have a man taller than the patient stand on the side, placing his shoulder under the patient's armpit and lifting the armpit upward so that the patient is suspended in the air. And another attendant pulls the patient's hand down toward the patient's abdomen. If the patient is light, something should be attached to weigh him down, and the joint will return immediately.  It may also be reduced by another method.
Another method is to place a long, round-headed wooden board on the ground, similar to the pestle head of a mortar, neither thick nor thin.  A soft cloth is placed on the top of the board, and the patient, standing along the length of the board, has the board placed under their armpit.
Then the patient extends their hand downwards on the other side, and stretches their body forcefully in the other direction, and the joint returns quickly to its place.  If reduction is difficult by all the previously mentioned methods, use this treatment: take a board about two cubits long. Its width should be about four fingers and its thickness about two fingers. It should have a rounded head to easily fit into the armpit, in this manner:
Then a soft cloth is tied to the rounded head so that the board does not injure the patient, and it is placed under the armpit.  The entire hand and arm are extended downward along the board.  The board is tied to the upper arm, forearm, and hand.
Then the arm is placed on a crossbar.  The arm is extended downward, and the rest of the body is allowed to hang from the other side.  Then the joint will enter its place.  If the joint enters by any means possible, a moderately sized ball of wool should be placed under the armpit.
Then the prepared dressing of mill dust with frankincense and egg white is applied to the entire shoulder, moving from above.  The ball is firmly tied under the armpit, and the bandage is wrapped around the dressing from above. The patient's hand is suspended from their neck.
The patient's hand should not be moved for seven days.  The patient's diet should be kept light to strengthen the limb, which speeds recovery.  The bandage is then removed after seven or five days, and the patient is encouraged to move the limb.  If the limb remains firm and does not relax, the patient is cured.
If the joint dislocates frequently due to moisture or another cause, cauterization with three layers of cauterizing material, as described in the chapter on cauterization, should be used. If all of this is done, and the bandage is removed after seven days, and the joint does not remain stable, ...
If the bandage and tightening are repeated many times, but the joint remains unstable, dislocates, and relaxes, and the patient cannot raise their arm, then know that the nerve in the head of the shoulder has been severed, stretched, or relaxed. In that case, know that the joint will never be stable.
For the jaw, which is towards the chest and breast, and to the back, it should be repositioned by pushing and pulling with the hands until it returns to place. All other methods of tightening and treatment should be used until it heals.  If, after healing, there is sensitivity or sluggishness in the limb, ...
The patient should use a bath frequently until the sensitivity subsides and returns to its original state.
Chapter Twenty-seven.
On the treatment of the elbow joint.
The elbow joint dislocates with difficulty, and also returns with difficulty.  It dislocates in all directions, especially forward or backward.  Its dislocation is not hidden from you, as it is situated under the sight and under the touch; its displaced form is evident.
If you compare the dislocated elbow to the healthy one, the dislocation will be evident to you through the depression of the joint. The arm cannot be bent, nor can the shoulder be touched. You must act quickly to reduce the dislocation before a hot swelling develops.
If a hot swelling develops, reducing the dislocation will be difficult, and it may never heal.  Especially if the dislocation is backward, this is the worst of all dislocations, the most painful, and often leads to death.
If it is possible to reduce the dislocation, the attendant should extend his arm fully outstretched with both hands, and the physician's hands should be positioned above and below the elbow.  He should push the joint back into place with both thumbs or the base of his palms.
If the dislocation is forward, the hand may be bent once so that the base of the palm strikes the corresponding shoulder. If the dislocation does not return, use very strong and forceful traction; have two attendants extend the arm.
Two attendants should also hold the patient to prevent movement during the traction. Then, the arm is rotated in each direction after wrapping a folded cloth or wide bandage around it.  If the physician is applying his hands to the joint, he should use ointment on it to aid in smoothly dislocating the joint.
Then the joint is pushed forcefully until it returns.  After reduction, a bandage containing compression, drying agent, and egg white should be firmly applied, and the arm should be suspended from the patient's neck for several days.
Then remove the bandage. If the joint is securely in place and the ligaments are released, leave it. If you see the joint hasn't fully stabilized, reapply the bandage and splint, and leave it for several days until it strengthens, then remove it. If swelling occurs after the joint is reduced and the bandage is removed,
If movement is difficult, use moist heat in a bath, gentle massage, and kneading until it becomes soft, or apply a thick, padded pad to the joint, bind it, and leave it on for a day and night. Then remove it and place him in a bath. Once he sweats, massage the joint gently.
Then reapply the pad one, two, and three times, with each bath, until it softens. If you want to use moist cow dung mixed with ghee and firmly bind it, do so repeatedly.  It will soften and return to its original condition.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
On treating a dislocated wrist.
The wrist frequently dislocates, and its reduction is easier than that of other joints, but it is necessary to hasten its reduction at the time of dislocation, before inflammation or a hot swelling develops in the area.
To reduce it, place the patient's wrist on a board, and have an attendant extend the arm. The physician should place their hand over the joint protuberance and push until it returns.  However, it is necessary to observe if the dislocation has moved to the back of the hand.
Let the patient place the outside of their hand on the board when extending and reducing it.  If the dislocation is outward on the hand, then let the inside of the hand be on the board so the physician's hand can be placed over the same joint protuberance. If it returns immediately, do so, otherwise, secure it with a compress that is soothing for the swelling.
Leave it alone; do not repeat the attempt, for it cannot bear it, and you cannot reduce it after days have passed, but the joint will remain crooked. It will not harm the patient, unless the hand is weakened, and cannot grasp anything.
Then you will know that the nerve is severed or crushed, and there is no remedy except to apply cauterization.  Perhaps this will help, or perhaps not.  If the wrist is reduced, apply the compress that we described, then secure it and leave it for five days.
Then it is released, and the hand is exercised.  If its movement is impeded and some form of stiffness appears, soften it with heated water and rub it repeatedly until it becomes soft.
The twenty-ninth chapter.
On the treatment of dislocated fingers.
Fingers may dislocate in any direction.  If a finger dislocates to the palm's surface, whether on the front or back, extend the finger and push the dislocation with your thumb until it returns to its place, then bandage the finger's head and secure it in the direction to which it dislocated.
Leave it for two days, then release it and extend it until it stands straight on that day.  If it is night, bandage it as described. Continue to loosen it during the day and exercise it with movement, and bandage it at night.  Do this for several days until it strengthens.
And do the same if it dislocates to the inside of the hand, bandaging it towards the same direction, and apply your first procedure until it heals.  Do the same if it dislocates to any other direction.
Chapter Thirty.
In the treatment of the dislocation of the spine's vertebrae.
When a vertebra of the spine or neck is completely dislocated, or many vertebrae are displaced from their positions, there is no treatment, for death quickly comes to the patient.
A sign of this is that the patient's stool is expelled involuntarily; he cannot control it.  Often, some of his limbs, either both legs, or both arms, or one of each, become relaxed.  If one vertebra is displaced from its position, it often relocates itself.
Its displacement can occur in four directions.  The one that moves backward is called a hump.  If the hump has developed from childhood, there is no treatment, nor will it ever heal.  However, if it was caused by a fall, a blow, or similar injury...
The ancients described various treatments with lengthy explanations, much of which is of little use.  I have summarized what is necessary, providing a concise approach, better than their extensive methods.  I have clarified the matter and illustrated the process differently from what they have described and explained.
Therefore, I say that a hump that develops in the front of the chest has no cure.  The same is true for those in the sides.  However, the only one that can be treated is that which develops in the back, specifically by the method I am describing.  This involves placing the patient on his face.
On a level platform near a wall, a damp mat is spread beneath to prevent injury to the chest.  Then, a vertical plank, planted in a hole in the ground, is placed near the head at one end of the platform, and another plank near the feet at the other end of the platform.
A servant holds one plank, not secured in the hole, and another servant holds the other plank in the same manner.  A soft, strong bandage is then wrapped around the patient's chest and under their armpits.  The end of the bandage is extended to the plank near the head and tied to it.
Another bandage is then tied above the hips, above the knees, and at the ankles.  Then all the bandages are gathered together and tied to the other plank, near the feet.  Each servant then pulls on their plank with the bandage, and the planks remain fixed in their positions.
However, the planks, as we said, are not secured. The physician places their hands firmly on the hump until it straightens, or places a board on it and leans on the board with their legs until it straightens. If it does not straighten with this treatment, a board approximately three cubits long is taken.
A space is hollowed out in the wall, near the patient, into which the end of the board is placed. The middle of the board is then placed on the hump.  The physician places their legs on the opposite end of the board and pulls firmly to apply pressure to the hump.
And it returns to its place.  If you wish, you can make it with a hand-wound torsion, which means driving two planks, each one cubit long, into the ground at the patient's head, at the end of the shop, and also two similar planks at their feet, also at the end of the shop.
Let there be a span between each plank. A hole is made in each plank for the torsion to run through. The planks are securely fastened to the ground so that they do not move at all.  A twisted stick, which is the torsion, is placed in the holes of the two planks to wrap the bandage around.
At one end of the stick is a hole, securely fitted with a stick one span long, which is twisted, and the same is done for the other two planks.  Then the bandage applied to the patient's chest is tightened to the torsion at the head, and the bandages applied to the legs are tightened to the torsion at the feet.
Then a servant stands beside each torsion, twisting the torsion with the twisting implement, while the physician adjusts the hump as previously described. This is an illustration of the torsion, the shop, and the patient.
Then, after the fracture returns to its proper position and the area is aligned, it is necessary to apply a bandage that has been dried with egg white, then padded, and then place a splint over the bandage made of a board, which is about three fingers wide or thereabouts, and as long as needed to cover the hump.
And place some correct beads over it, then bind it with the necessary bandage. The patient should use gentle food until recovery. If some swelling remains in the area at the end of the healing process, then it is necessary to use medications that loosen and soften, along with the use of the splint that we described for a long time.
A lead plate may also be used in this case.  A swelling may appear at the end of the vertebrae, and it may be mistaken for dislocation.  It could be an extra bone that has protruded.  Do not use this treatment for it, as death may result.
Chapter Thirty-one.
On the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Know that the hip joint and the shoulder joint are only subject to dislocation, and are not subject to the slight displacement and displacement that occur in other joints. The hip joint dislocates in four ways.
This is because it dislocates inward, outward, forward, and backward.  It dislocates inward most often, and rarely forward or backward.  A sign of an inward dislocation is that if you align the patient's healthy leg with the affected one, the healthy leg will be longer.
The knee will be more prominent than the healthy one, and the patient will not be able to bend their leg at the groin. The area below the groin will be swollen and clearly noticeable, because the head of the femur has moved there.
The signs of an outward dislocation are opposite to these signs.  The sign of a forward dislocation is that the leg is fully extended, but cannot be bent without pain in the knee.  If the patient attempts to walk, they cannot do so forward.
The patient retains urine and their groin is swollen. When walking, they place their weight on their heel.  A sign of a backward dislocation is that the knee does not extend, and the patient cannot bend it until the groin is bent.  The leg is also shorter than the other one.
The groin is relaxed, and the head of the femur is clearly visible near the hip.  As for the treatment of these types of dislocations, if the dislocation is longstanding and the patient has not attempted to reduce it, and it remains in its condition, then there is absolutely no cure.
Therefore, one should not attempt to treat it.  As for a recent dislocation, whatever type of the four types of dislocation it may be, promptly manipulate the joint, extending it inward and outward, and moving it to the right and left.  Perhaps...
If it returns, no further treatment is needed.  If it does not return this way, a strong, capable assistant should be positioned to extend the leg from below, either with their hands or with a bandage tied above the knee.  A second assistant should extend it from above.
The first assistant should place their hands under the armpits, then apply a soft bandage to the base of the thigh.  A third assistant holds the end of the bandage, and the extension is done either from the front, from the groin toward the collarbone, or from behind toward the back.
All the assistants should extend simultaneously until the patient is lifted off the ground and suspended.  This type of extension is common to all four types. If the dislocation is reduced as described, then no further treatment is necessary. Otherwise, each type requires a specific treatment, as I have described.
For a dislocation inward, the patient should lie on their sound side, and a bandage should be applied to the base of the thigh, between the top of the thigh and the area below the groin.
Then the bandage is extended upward from the groin to the upper body, towards the collarbone.  A strong assistant then takes hold of the thick part of the affected thigh with both arms and pulls outwards forcefully, and it will return to its place.
This method is easier than the other methods for reducing this limb. If this approach is unsuccessful and you cannot use this method at all, then the patient's legs should be bound with a strong, soft bandage, around the heels and knees.
Each bandage should be approximately four fingers' width apart.  The affected leg should be extended further than the other by about two fingers' width.  The patient should be suspended from the head by a beam in the house, with the beam a distance of two arms' length from the ground.
Then a strong servant should grasp the upper part of the thigh, and another servant should pull the patient upwards.  The servant grasping the thigh should push forcefully, and the joint will quickly return to its place.  As for its specific reduction if the dislocation is outward,
The patient should lie on a raised platform, as described in the section on the humpback, and the bandage should be tightly applied to their affected leg and chest.  Then, two boards should be placed, one at the patient's feet and the other at their head, and a further board should be placed firmly in the middle of the platform.
Damp cloths should be wrapped around the boards to prevent injury to the patient. The board should be positioned between the patient's thighs to prevent them from pulling down during the stretching.  Then, each attendant should pull in their respective direction, and the physician should align the hip with their hands, if it returns to its place.
Otherwise, place the board on the patient and apply pressure as described for the humpback case, except that the patient should lie on their healthy side.  If the dislocation is forward, the patient's leg should be stretched in this same position on the platform.
The physician places the palm of their right hand on the affected hip, then presses it with the other hand.  The pressure should be extended downward towards the knee.  If the dislocation is backward, the patient should not be pulled downwards.
The patient should be elevated off the ground, but placed on a firm surface.  The hip should also be externally dislocated, as previously described, when lying on the platform on their face, with the bandages tight.
As we previously stated, pressure should also be applied with the board to the area where the joint has dislocated.  When the hip joint has returned correctly, a sign of which is apparent, and that is the stretching of the patient's legs.
If you see both legs even, and the patient can flex and extend the leg without difficulty, know that the limb has returned correctly.  Then join the thighs, apply the bandage, and bind tightly with a turban so that the hip does not move in any direction.
The patient should remain at rest for three or four days.  Then, loosen the bandages and dressing, and compare the legs.  If you find them equal in size, know that the fracture has set. Release the patient to walk.  If you see any looseness, [do not release].
Return and bandage it, tightening it as before. Leave it for three more days, and then loosen it.  The patient should walk slowly for several days until it strengthens, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-two.
On the treatment of a fractured knee.
The knee dislocates in three ways: inward, outward, and downward (i.e., backward).  It does not dislocate forward.  A sign of its dislocation is that you should instruct the patient to bring their leg close to their thigh.
If the leg does not adhere to the thigh, know that the knee is dislocated.  The method of reduction for all types of dislocation is that the patient should sit down and extend their legs if able.  A servant should sit behind the patient, holding the patient's waist and gently turning it backward.  Then, you should sit on the patient's thighs.
You should place your back against the patient's face, and place their leg between your legs.  Then, grasp the knee with both your hands, interlocking your fingers around the knee.  Next, firmly press the sides of the knee with your hands.  Another attendant should extend the patient's leg until the knee returns to its place.
A sign of its return is that the leg adheres to the thigh with no forced movement. Then bandage it, securing the leg to the thigh, and wrap them both with a bandage for three or four days.  Then remove it.  For a few days, only allow limited walking until strength returns.
If you cannot reduce it by the method described, then use forceful traction with the bandages previously described in the treatment of the hip, until it returns.
Chapter Thirty-three.
On the treatment of dislocated heels.
The heel may be slightly displaced or completely dislocated.  Dislocation can occur either inward or outward.  A sign of dislocation is that the heel will appear swollen and prominent in the direction of the displacement.
As for treating its slight displacement, it is easily reduced by gently pulling and aligning it until it returns.  However, if it is completely dislocated, the patient should sit, and a strong attendant should hold the patient firmly from behind, in the middle of their back.
Then, with your right hand, grasp the foot from above, and with your left hand, from below at the heel.  Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, then with your left, twice.  Then extend it with your left hand and push the front of the foot towards the leg with your right hand, without force.
Repeat this twice as described. Then, on the third time, push the front of the foot towards the leg while pulling at the heel.  If it returns in one or two attempts, and you see the foot is aligned, then stop. Otherwise, repeat the procedure, as it will return.
If it does not return using the method described, then lay the patient on their back on the ground, and firmly insert a tightly bound rod into the ground, placing it between their legs.  It should be wrapped with cloth to prevent harming the patient, and an attendant should hold the patient's thigh.
Then another attendant extends the leg, either with their hands or with a bandage tied around the leg.  Each attendant pulls in the opposite direction. The rod, positioned between the patient's thighs, is held to prevent the patient's body from moving downwards during the pulling.  Then, the physician aligns the joint with his hands.
Another attendant holds the healthy leg downwards. If the joint returns quickly, then take the bandage and splint and secure it with tight bandages. Tie the foot with a bandage downwards.  Care must be taken with the nerve located above the heel from behind.
So that the bandage is not too tight and causes pain. Then leave it for two or three days. If the bandage loosens, tighten it again, then release it on the third or fourth day.  The patient should refrain from walking for forty days.
If the patient attempts to walk before this time, there is a risk that the joint will dislocate and become unusable, and treatment will no longer be effective.  If a hot swelling appears, use the treatments previously described in other sections for swelling and inflammation until it disappears.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
On the treatment of fractured fingers of the foot.
The fractured parts should be aligned with a gentle pull, without force. This is not difficult, but easy.  If the fracture is in some of the phalanges of the foot, the patient should place their foot on a flat surface.
Or on a board, while standing like a walker.  Then place your foot on the protruding joints, and press down firmly with your foot until they return to alignment and no protrusion remains visible.
Then place a board under the sole of their foot, encompassing the entire foot with two ends.  Bind it firmly for three days.  Then unbind it and keep them from walking for many days until it strengthens and a return is unlikely, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-five.
On the types of fracture that occurs with a wound, or with a break, or with both.
When something like that occurs and you attempt treatment and reduction, it often results in death.
Therefore, one should not undertake such treatment unless they are skilled in the craft, experienced, gentle, compassionate, cautious, and not rash or reckless.  In the beginning, they should use medications that relieve only the hot inflammations and let the patient recover.
By God's will, except for what one hopes will be spared from harm with the slightness of the disease, and where some hope appears, then return it from the very beginning, before the hot inflammation occurs.
If the limb returns to the desired state, use the treatment that relieves the hot inflammation and treat the wound with suitable drying ointments. If the fracture involves a broken bone with detached fragments, then remove them. Follow in that regard what we have mentioned concerning simple illnesses in the preceding sections.
Strive diligently and purify yourself from entering into a path of uncertainty, as I have previously instructed and advised you. This will be more lasting for your reputation and safer for your presentation, God willing.
